Causality and Created Things

10 min
Ibn Sina's Proof for the Enumeration of Accidents into Nine Categories
Ibn Sina's exhaustive division of accidents into three primary categories, leading to nine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's logical proof that accidents are exhaustively divided into nine categories through a systematic division starting from the essence of accident.

Core Idea
Accidents are logically divided into three primary categories, with relation subdividing into six, yielding nine.

Ibn Sina's proof is not empirical but a priori, based on the logical properties of accident. He begins by classifying accidents according to whether they entail division or relation, then subdivides relation into six further types. This systematic reasoning demonstrates that accidents can only be nine distinct kinds. The passage includes interlinear commentary clarifying terms, but the main argument is clear.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary division
Read the first part of the passage where Ibn Sina divides accidents into quantity, quality, and relation based on whether they require division or relation by essence.
Focus on the logical dichotomy: division or not; if not, relation or not.
2
Trace the subdivision of relation
Identify how relation is further divided: between parts (position) or to something external, then external branches to quantity, relation, quality, etc.
Notice the terms: وضع, أين, متى, ملك, إضافة, فعل, انفعال.
3
Count the total categories
Verify that substance plus nine accidents yields ten categories overall. The passage concludes with this result.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد احتج ابن سينا على الحصر بما خلاصته أنّه ) أي العرض ( ينقسم ( انقساماً دائراً بين النفي والاثبات ( إلى كم وكيف ونسبة كما مر من أن العرض إما أن يقتضي لذاته القسمة أو لا، والثاني إما أن يقتضي لذاته النسبة أو لا فهذه أقسام ثلاثة لا مخرج للعرض عنها (وغيرها الجوهر) فانحصر أقسام الموجود الممكن في أربعة وعلى هذا ( فالنسبة إما للأجزاء )
قوله : ( وهو عارض لها ) لم يقل وهو مشترك لفظي لبعده . قوله : ( لا عالياً) إشارة إلى أن المقصود من كونه مفرداً نفي كونه عالياً فلا يرد جواز أن لا يكون فوقه جنساً فلا يكون مفرداً. قوله: (أو يكون اثنان إلخ) دخول واحد منها تحت جنس يوجب كونه جنساً متوسطاً أو سافلاً إلا أنَّه تعرض لدخول اثنين أو أكثر بناء على ما ذهب إليه بعض المنطقيين من أنه لا بد للجنس من كونه مقولاً على كثيرين بالفعل ونفوا انحصاره في نوع واحد كما في شرح المطالع. قوله : (لا) مخرج للعرض عنها لكونها دائرة بين النفي والإثبات والتعاريف الحاصلة من القسمة مساوية للأقسام كما لا يخفى . قوله: (وغيرها الجوهر) هذه المقدمة مستدركة في بيان حصر العرض في تسعة وأن ما ذكره الشيخ في الشفاء لبيان حصر الموجود في مقولات عشر
المعتبر في الكيف أن لا يلزم من تصوره تصور شي خارج عن محله فأما ما يلزم من تصوره تصور محله أو تصور ما يوجد في محله فهو من الكيف فالوحدة والنقطة من الكيف لأنَّ الوحدة لا يلزم من تصورها إلا تصور محلها أو تصور حال من أحوال محلها وكذا القول في النقطة انتهى كلامه . قوله : ( فيكون) جنساً مفرداً هذا على تقدير أن لا يكون فوقه جنس وقد يقال : المراد هاهنا من كونها عالية أنَّ لا جنس فوقها فجاز أن يكون بعضها أجناساً مفردة .
أي لأجزاء موضوعها بعضها إلى بعض ( وهو الوضع أو لا تكون النسبة بين أجزاء موضوعها بل لمجموعه إلى أمر خارج عنه ( وهي ( أي هذه النسبة (إما إلى كم فإن ) كان ) ذلك الكم ( قاراً) لجواز اجتماع أجزائه معا ( فإن انتقل) ذلك الكم القار ( به ) أي بانتقال موضوع النسبة ) فهو الملك وإلا فهو الأين وإن كان ذلك الكم ( غير قار فهو متى وإما إلى النسبة فالمضاف ) لأن النسبة حينئذ متكررة ( وإما إلى كيف ولا ) ) تعقل النسبة إلى الكيف إلا) بأن يكون منه غيره وهو أن يفعل أو ) يكون هو من غيره وهو أن ينفعل وإما إلى الجوهر وهو لا يقبل النسبة لذاته بل لعارض من عوارضه ( ولا يخرجُ ) ذلك العارض ( مما ذكرنا ) من الأعراض الثلاثة فالبنسبة إلى الجوهر تكون راجعة إلى النسب المذكورة لا قسما برأسه فانحصرت الممكنات الموجودة في عشر مقولات والأعراض في تسع منها
And Ibn Sina argued for the enumeration with a summary: that an accident is divided—by a division that alternates between negation and affirmation—into quantity, quality, and relation, as was mentioned: an accident either requires by its essence division or not, and the second either requires by its essence relation or not. These are three divisions from which an accident cannot escape. And substance is other than them. Thus the divisions of possible existents are restricted to four. Based on this, the relation is either to the parts... [commentary on phrases] ... So the possible existents are restricted to ten categories, and the accidents to nine of them.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's proof for the enumeration of accidents into nine categories. The proof begins by dividing accidents into three primary categories: quantity (kam), quality (kayf), and relation (nisba). The division is exhaustive: an accident either requires division (i.e., is divisible) or not; if not, it either requires relation or not. This yields three categories. Substance (jawhar) is separate, making four categories of possible existents. The relation category is further subdivided: it may be between parts of a subject (position, wad'), or between the whole subject and something external. The external relation can be to a stationary quantity (leading to 'ain, if the quantity remains, or malik, if transferred), a non-stationary quantity (mata), to another relation (idafa), to quality (which involves action or passion), or to substance (which does not accept relation by itself but through an accident). Thus the entire division yields ten categories overall, with accidents being nine. The commentary interwoven clarifies specific points, such as the definition of quality and the possibility of genera not being highest.

Key Terms
كم (quantity)
كيف (quality)
نسبة (relation)
جوهر (substance)
مقولات عشر (ten categories)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the enumeration is purely empirical or based on observation.
Correction: Ibn Sina's proof is a priori, based on logical division of the concept of accident, not empirical observation.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's proof, what are the three primary categories of accidents?
A) Substance, quantity, quality.
B) Quantity, quality, relation.
C) Position, condition, action.
D) Genus, species, difference.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are exhaustively divided into nine categories through a logical division: quantity, quality, and six subcategories of relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina's proof claims to exhaust all possible accidents through pure logic, without any empirical observation. When you face doubts about faith or the unseen, do you rely more on rational proofs or personal experiences? How does this logical certainty challenge you to ground your belief in necessary truths rather than subjective feelings? What change can you make today to prioritize rational conviction over emotional comfort?
?
This lesson demonstrates that even the categories of everyday changes (accidents) are orderly and predefined by necessity. Reflect on a recent event in your life that felt chaotic or meaningless. Can you identify it as one of these nine categories? How does knowing that every accident belongs to a rational system affect your trust in the wisdom of the Creator? Will you now actively look for the order behind apparent randomness?

Objection: Relation to Stable Quantity Beyond Encompassing
First objection: relation to stable quantity is not exclusively by encompassing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the enumeration of accidents: that relation to stable quantity can occur through contact and matching, not just encompassing.

Core Idea
Encompassing is not the only relation to quantity; contact and matching also matter.

The enumeration of accidents into nine categories was a key achievement of Aristotelian philosophy. However, critics argued that the relation to stable quantity was artificially limited to encompassing, which gives location and possession. This objection opens the door to additional accidental categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the specific terms used: ihata (encompassing), mumassa (contact), mutabaqa (matching).
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the three relations
The objection lists three possible relations: encompassing, contact, and matching. Understand how each differs.
3
Consider the implication
If relation to quantity is not only by encompassing, then the categories of location (ayn) and possession (milk) are not the only ones. More categories may be needed.
This challenges the exhaustiveness of the nine categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض على ما ذكر في هذا الحصر ( أنا لا نسلم أن النسبة إلى الكم القار تكون بالإحاطة فقط ) حتى تنحصر في الأين والملك) بل قد تكون النسبة إلى الكم القار بوجه آخر ( كالمماسة ) بين سطحي جسمين ( والمطابقة ) التي هي الاتحاد في الأطراف ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء وإلى الخارج كما مر ( فقد جاء التركيب وأنه يوجب تكثر الأقسام إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يعتبر التركيب بين النسبة إلى الكم والنسبة إلى الكيف ) مثلاً فيكون قسماً خارجاً عن الأقسام المذكورة ) وأيضاً فبقي ) من الأقسام الممكنة النسبة إلى العدد ) الذي هو الكم المنفصل ( ولا برهان على انتفائه ) أي انتفاء هذا القسم ( وأيضاً فالنسبة إلى الزمان ) الذي هو كم متصل غير قار (لا يتعين أن تكون
قوله : (فاعتبرت) على صيغة المجهول بقرينة قوله كما مر يعني أن القوم اعتبروا في الوضع النسبتين معاً والمقصود بيان الحصر في الأقسام التسعة التي قررها القوم لا التقسيم ابتداء وبيان الحصر في أقسامه فلا يرد أن الشيخ لم يعتبر النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع واعتبار غيره لا يصير حجة عليه فلا يلزم تكثيرُ الأقسام واعلم أنَّ الشيخ نقل أولاً وجه الحصر من فقال : العرض إما أن يكون مستقراً في موضوعه وارد عليه بسبب غيره من خارج ولا محتاجاً إلى النسبة إلى ذلك الخارج وهو أقسام ثلاثة كمية وكيفية ووضع إلخ ثم قال : في وجه الحصر الذي أحدثه أن كل عرض لا يخلو إما أن يحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عن الموضوع أو لا يحوج، والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك على ذلك ثلاثة أقسام : إما أن يكون لم يحوج إلى ذلك فقد يحوج إلى وقوع نسبة في أشياء هي فيه ليست خارجة عنه وإما أن لا يحوج إلى ذلك بالنسبة والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك فهذه الخارجة تجعل الموضوع منقسماً بوجه ما حتى يكون له أجزاء لبعضها عند بعض حال متغايرة في النسبة وذلك هو مقولة الوضع إذ هو نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض وأنَّ كل واحد منها أين هو من الكل إلخ، ولا يخفى أنه ليس على شيء من هذين الوجهين اعتبار النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع ولعل اعتبارها كما أراد المتأخرون لئلا يكون القيام عين الانعكاس لكن اللازم من عدم اعتبارها هو اتحادهما في الجنس لا في النوع فيجوز أن يختلف بالفصول المقومة وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من أنَّ الجنس والفصل متحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصور أن حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر إنما يرد لو قيل : إِنَّ النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل والنسبة بين الأجزاء جنس بل نقول إنَّ الجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل للجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة بين الأمور الداخلة كالحيوان المأخوذ من البدن والناطق المأخوذ من الصورة النوعية فبعد مفارقته لا تبقى تلك الحصة من الجنس بل تنعدم وإنما تبقى النسبة في الأجزاء التي هي مبدأ لحصة أخرى من الوضع ويقارنها النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة التي هي مبدأ لفصل آخر .
قوله : ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع إلخ) أي على المذهب المختار الذي أثبت بالدليل فيما سبق وإن ذهب أبو علي إلى خلافه كما أشير إليه الآن .
And the objection to what was mentioned in this enumeration: we do not concede that the relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing, so that it is limited to location and possession. Rather, the relation to stable quantity may be in another way, like contact between the surfaces of two bodies, and matching which is coincidence at boundaries. Also, in position, the relation of parts to parts and to the exterior was considered, as has passed, so composition occurs, and it necessitates multiplication of categories, since then it is permissible to consider composition between the relation to quantity and the relation to quality, for example, making a category outside the mentioned categories. Also, the relation to number, which is discrete quantity, remains among the possible categories, and there is no proof of its non-existence. Also, the relation to time, which is continuous non-stable quantity, is not necessarily [limited]...
His statement: 'it was considered' is in the passive voice, indicated by his statement 'as has passed,' meaning that the people considered both relations in position, and the intent is to explain the enumeration in the nine categories that the people established, not the division from the beginning, and to explain the enumeration in its categories. So it is not objected that the master did not consider the relation to external things in position, and the consideration of others is not a proof against him, so multiplication of categories does not follow. Know that the master first transmitted the aspect of enumeration from someone: the accident is either stable in its subject and received from another from outside, not needing relation to that outside, and these are three categories: quantity, quality, and position, etc. Then he said: in the aspect of enumeration that he originated, that every accident either requires in its conception the conception of something outside the subject or not. That which does not require that has three categories: either it does not require that but may require the occurrence of a relation in things that are in it not external to it, or it does not require that in relation. That which does not require that: these external things make the subject divided in some way so that it has parts for each other in a state differing in relation, and that is the category of position, for it is the relation of the parts of the body to each other and that each one of them is where it is from the whole, etc. It is not hidden that in neither of these two aspects is there consideration of the relation to external things in position. Perhaps its consideration is as the later ones intended so that standing would not be identical to reflection, but the necessary consequence of not considering it is their union in genus not in species, so it is permissible for them to differ by constitutive differentiate. And what the commentator (may he be sanctified) mentioned earlier that the genus and differentiate are united in existence and composition, so how can it be conceived that a portion of the genus accompanied a differentia then separated to another differentia? That objection arises only if it is said that the relation to external things is a differentia and the relation between parts is a genus. Rather, we say that the mental part taken from the relation to external things is a differentia for the mental part taken from the relation between internal things, like 'animal' taken from the body and 'rational' taken from the specific form. So after its separation, that portion of the genus does not remain but is annihilated; rather, the relation in the parts that are the beginning of another portion of position remains, and it is accompanied by the relation to external things which is the beginning of another differentia.
His statement: 'And also, it was considered in position, etc.' means according to the chosen doctrine proved by evidence previously, although Abu Ali held the contrary, as indicated now.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the enumeration of nine accident categories and the commentary's response. The objection has several points: first, it denies that relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing (which gives location and possession); there are other modes like contact and matching. Second, in the category of position, both internal and external relations are considered, which allows composition of relations (e.g., quantity and quality), leading to more categories. Third, relation to discrete quantity (number) is omitted without proof. Fourth, relation to time is not necessarily limited. The commentary (starting with 'قوله : (فاعتبرت)') defends the enumeration by explaining that the intended enumeration is based on the method of the early philosophers (the 'people'), not a novel division. It clarifies that the master (Avicenna) did not consider external relations in position, so the objection does not apply. The commentary then discusses the relation between position and reflection, arguing that if external relation is not considered, both collapse into the same genus but differ by species. It refutes the idea that separation of a specific portion of genus is incoherent by explaining that the mental parts (genus and differentia) are taken from different aspects and change accordingly. Finally, it notes that the chosen doctrine is that position involves both internal and external relations, contrary to Abu Ali's view.

Key Terms
اعتراض
كم قار
إحاطة
مماسة
مطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that the only relation of an accident to stable quantity is being inside or containing (encompassing).
Correction: The objection shows that contact and matching are also relations to stable quantity, not reducible to location or possession.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, which of the following is NOT presented as a possible relation to stable quantity?
A) Encompassing (ihata)
B) Contact (mumassa)
C) Matching (mutabaqa)
D) Proportionality (tanasub)

Key Takeaway
The relation to stable quantity can be by encompassing, contact, or matching, challenging the traditional limitation to only two categories.

Objection: Composition in Position Multiplies Categories
Second objection: composition of relations in position leads to new categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the category of position (wad') involves composition of relations, leading to more than nine accident categories.

Core Idea
Position combines internal and external relations, allowing new composite categories.

The second objection targets the category of position (wad'), which is the relation of parts of a body to each other and to external things. The objector argues that because position involves both internal and external relations, these can be combined with other categories like quantity and quality, generating further accidental types.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that position is said to consider relations of parts to parts and to the outside.
2
Understand composition
The objector says that this composition (tarkib) of relations allows further combinations, e.g., combining quantity relation with quality relation.
This would create a new category outside the original nine.
3
Evaluate the implication
If composition is allowed, then the original enumeration is not exhaustive because new composite categories emerge.
This is a fundamental challenge to the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض على ما ذكر في هذا الحصر ( أنا لا نسلم أن النسبة إلى الكم القار تكون بالإحاطة فقط ) حتى تنحصر في الأين والملك) بل قد تكون النسبة إلى الكم القار بوجه آخر ( كالمماسة ) بين سطحي جسمين ( والمطابقة ) التي هي الاتحاد في الأطراف ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأجزاء وإلى الخارج كما مر ( فقد جاء التركيب وأنه يوجب تكثر الأقسام إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يعتبر التركيب بين النسبة إلى الكم والنسبة إلى الكيف ) مثلاً فيكون قسماً خارجاً عن الأقسام المذكورة ) وأيضاً فبقي ) من الأقسام الممكنة النسبة إلى العدد ) الذي هو الكم المنفصل ( ولا برهان على انتفائه ) أي انتفاء هذا القسم ( وأيضاً فالنسبة إلى الزمان ) الذي هو كم متصل غير قار (لا يتعين أن تكون
قوله : (فاعتبرت) على صيغة المجهول بقرينة قوله كما مر يعني أن القوم اعتبروا في الوضع النسبتين معاً والمقصود بيان الحصر في الأقسام التسعة التي قررها القوم لا التقسيم ابتداء وبيان الحصر في أقسامه فلا يرد أن الشيخ لم يعتبر النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع واعتبار غيره لا يصير حجة عليه فلا يلزم تكثيرُ الأقسام واعلم أنَّ الشيخ نقل أولاً وجه الحصر من فقال : العرض إما أن يكون مستقراً في موضوعه وارد عليه بسبب غيره من خارج ولا محتاجاً إلى النسبة إلى ذلك الخارج وهو أقسام ثلاثة كمية وكيفية ووضع إلخ ثم قال : في وجه الحصر الذي أحدثه أن كل عرض لا يخلو إما أن يحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عن الموضوع أو لا يحوج، والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك على ذلك ثلاثة أقسام : إما أن يكون لم يحوج إلى ذلك فقد يحوج إلى وقوع نسبة في أشياء هي فيه ليست خارجة عنه وإما أن لا يحوج إلى ذلك بالنسبة والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك فهذه الخارجة تجعل الموضوع منقسماً بوجه ما حتى يكون له أجزاء لبعضها عند بعض حال متغايرة في النسبة وذلك هو مقولة الوضع إذ هو نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض وأنَّ كل واحد منها أين هو من الكل إلخ، ولا يخفى أنه ليس على شيء من هذين الوجهين اعتبار النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع ولعل اعتبارها كما أراد المتأخرون لئلا يكون القيام عين الانعكاس لكن اللازم من عدم اعتبارها هو اتحادهما في الجنس لا في النوع فيجوز أن يختلف بالفصول المقومة وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من أنَّ الجنس والفصل متحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصور أن حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر إنما يرد لو قيل : إِنَّ النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل والنسبة بين الأجزاء جنس بل نقول إنَّ الجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل للجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة بين الأمور الداخلة كالحيوان المأخوذ من البدن والناطق المأخوذ من الصورة النوعية فبعد مفارقته لا تبقى تلك الحصة من الجنس بل تنعدم وإنما تبقى النسبة في الأجزاء التي هي مبدأ لحصة أخرى من الوضع ويقارنها النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة التي هي مبدأ لفصل آخر .
قوله : ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع إلخ) أي على المذهب المختار الذي أثبت بالدليل فيما سبق وإن ذهب أبو علي إلى خلافه كما أشير إليه الآن .
And the objection to what was mentioned in this enumeration: we do not concede that the relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing, so that it is limited to location and possession. Rather, the relation to stable quantity may be in another way, like contact between the surfaces of two bodies, and matching which is coincidence at boundaries. Also, in position, the relation of parts to parts and to the exterior was considered, as has passed, so composition occurs, and it necessitates multiplication of categories, since then it is permissible to consider composition between the relation to quantity and the relation to quality, for example, making a category outside the mentioned categories. Also, the relation to number, which is discrete quantity, remains among the possible categories, and there is no proof of its non-existence. Also, the relation to time, which is continuous non-stable quantity, is not necessarily [limited]...
His statement: 'it was considered' is in the passive voice, indicated by his statement 'as has passed,' meaning that the people considered both relations in position, and the intent is to explain the enumeration in the nine categories that the people established, not the division from the beginning, and to explain the enumeration in its categories. So it is not objected that the master did not consider the relation to external things in position, and the consideration of others is not a proof against him, so multiplication of categories does not follow. Know that the master first transmitted the aspect of enumeration from someone: the accident is either stable in its subject and received from another from outside, not needing relation to that outside, and these are three categories: quantity, quality, and position, etc. Then he said: in the aspect of enumeration that he originated, that every accident either requires in its conception the conception of something outside the subject or not. That which does not require that has three categories: either it does not require that but may require the occurrence of a relation in things that are in it not external to it, or it does not require that in relation. That which does not require that: these external things make the subject divided in some way so that it has parts for each other in a state differing in relation, and that is the category of position, for it is the relation of the parts of the body to each other and that each one of them is where it is from the whole, etc. It is not hidden that in neither of these two aspects is there consideration of the relation to external things in position. Perhaps its consideration is as the later ones intended so that standing would not be identical to reflection, but the necessary consequence of not considering it is their union in genus not in species, so it is permissible for them to differ by constitutive differentiate. And what the commentator (may he be sanctified) mentioned earlier that the genus and differentiate are united in existence and composition, so how can it be conceived that a portion of the genus accompanied a differentia then separated to another differentia? That objection arises only if it is said that the relation to external things is a differentia and the relation between parts is a genus. Rather, we say that the mental part taken from the relation to external things is a differentia for the mental part taken from the relation between internal things, like 'animal' taken from the body and 'rational' taken from the specific form. So after its separation, that portion of the genus does not remain but is annihilated; rather, the relation in the parts that are the beginning of another portion of position remains, and it is accompanied by the relation to external things which is the beginning of another differentia.
His statement: 'And also, it was considered in position, etc.' means according to the chosen doctrine proved by evidence previously, although Abu Ali held the contrary, as indicated now.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the enumeration of nine accident categories and the commentary's response. The objection has several points: first, it denies that relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing (which gives location and possession); there are other modes like contact and matching. Second, in the category of position, both internal and external relations are considered, which allows composition of relations (e.g., quantity and quality), leading to more categories. Third, relation to discrete quantity (number) is omitted without proof. Fourth, relation to time is not necessarily limited. The commentary (starting with 'قوله : (فاعتبرت)') defends the enumeration by explaining that the intended enumeration is based on the method of the early philosophers (the 'people'), not a novel division. It clarifies that the master (Avicenna) did not consider external relations in position, so the objection does not apply. The commentary then discusses the relation between position and reflection, arguing that if external relation is not considered, both collapse into the same genus but differ by species. It refutes the idea that separation of a specific portion of genus is incoherent by explaining that the mental parts (genus and differentia) are taken from different aspects and change accordingly. Finally, it notes that the chosen doctrine is that position involves both internal and external relations, contrary to Abu Ali's view.

Key Terms
وضع
تركيب
نسبة الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that position is a simple category with no internal composition.
Correction: The objection shows that position inherently involves two types of relations, and their composition can generate new categories.

Checkpoint
What does the objector say about composition in position?
A) It is impossible
B) It multiplies categories
C) It is ignored by the commentators
D) It is well-known

Key Takeaway
The composition of relations in position allows for new composite accident categories beyond the original nine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector shows that a seemingly simple category (position) actually involves composition of relations. Think of a 'simple' category in your own life—like a 'good deed' or a 'sin'. What hidden relations might it contain? How does this challenge your tendency to judge actions as straightforward? Are you willing to investigate the internal and external relations of your own actions before classifying them?
?
The passage suggests that composition can multiply categories beyond what was originally listed. If you could combine two aspects of your character (e.g., 'sincerity' and 'knowledge') to form a new moral category, what would it be? How would living by that new category change your daily accountability?
?
The objection forces us to question the completeness of our systems of classification. Where in your understanding of the deen do you assume a complete list (e.g., the 99 names, the five pillars, the sins)? Could there be hidden composite categories that you are neglecting? How does that make you feel—anxious or motivated to seek deeper understanding?

Objection: Omission of Relation to Number and Time
Third objection: omission of relation to discrete quantity and time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the enumeration omits the relation to discrete quantity (number) and to time, without proof of their impossibility.

Core Idea
Number and time as possible accident categories are left out without justification.

The third objection points out two major omissions in the traditional enumeration: the relation to discrete quantity (number) and the relation to time (continuous non-stable quantity). The objector notes that these are possible accident categories, and there is no proof that they do not exist, so the enumeration is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the terms: 'adad (number), zamān (time), and the claim of no proof.
2
Identify the omissions
The two omissions are relation to number (discrete quantity) and relation to time (continuous non-stable quantity).
These are distinct categories not included in the nine.
3
Assess the force of the objection
The objector says there is no proof (burhān) of their non-existence, so they should be considered possible categories.
This shifts the burden of proof to the defenders.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the nine categories are exhaustive and that number and time are not accident categories.

According to the objection, what is lacking regarding the relation to number?
A) A clear definition

C) A proper category
D) A relation to quality

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector challenges the theologian to prove that number and time cannot be accidents. When you hold a belief, do you ever test it by actively looking for what might be missing or what must be proven false? What 'missing categories' in your own understanding of faith are you avoiding examining?
?
Time is presented as a possible accident, a created dimension that Allah brings into existence. In your daily life, do you treat time as a mere container for events, or as a contingent entity that Allah controls moment by moment? How would your urgency in worship change if you truly believed time itself is an accident being renewed every instant?
?
The passage shows that even a well-known classification (like the nine accidents) can be incomplete if assumptions go unchallenged. Is there any aspect of your religious practice or worldview that you have accepted as 'complete' without considering alternative possibilities? What would it cost you to honestly explore those 'unaccounted' areas?

Commentary: Defense of the Enumeration and the Role of Position
Commentary defense: enumeration follows a traditional method; position is only internal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentary's defense of the enumeration, clarifying that the intended enumeration is based on a specific method and that position involves only internal relations in the original view.

Core Idea
The enumeration is methodologically sound; objections arise from misunderstanding the traditional approach.

After presenting objections, the commentary provides a defense. It clarifies that the enumeration follows the method of early philosophers ('the people'), not a novel division. It focuses on the category of position, arguing that the original view only considers internal relations, so the objections about composition and external relations are invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the passive voice and the reference to 'the people' (al-qawm).
2
Understand the defense of position
The commentary explains that the master (Avicenna) did not consider external relations in position, so the objection about composition does not apply to his enumeration.
This is crucial for rebutting the second objection.
3
Grasp the genus-differentia discussion
The commentary addresses the concern about separation of genus and differentia, offering a refined understanding of mental parts.
This is advanced but important for the broader argument.

قوله : (فاعتبرت) على صيغة المجهول بقرينة قوله كما مر يعني أن القوم اعتبروا في الوضع النسبتين معاً والمقصود بيان الحصر في الأقسام التسعة التي قررها القوم لا التقسيم ابتداء وبيان الحصر في أقسامه فلا يرد أن الشيخ لم يعتبر النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع واعتبار غيره لا يصير حجة عليه فلا يلزم تكثيرُ الأقسام واعلم أنَّ الشيخ نقل أولاً وجه الحصر من فقال : العرض إما أن يكون مستقراً في موضوعه وارد عليه بسبب غيره من خارج ولا محتاجاً إلى النسبة إلى ذلك الخارج وهو أقسام ثلاثة كمية وكيفية ووضع إلخ ثم قال : في وجه الحصر الذي أحدثه أن كل عرض لا يخلو إما أن يحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عن الموضوع أو لا يحوج، والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك على ذلك ثلاثة أقسام : إما أن يكون لم يحوج إلى ذلك فقد يحوج إلى وقوع نسبة في أشياء هي فيه ليست خارجة عنه وإما أن لا يحوج إلى ذلك بالنسبة والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك فهذه الخارجة تجعل الموضوع منقسماً بوجه ما حتى يكون له أجزاء لبعضها عند بعض حال متغايرة في النسبة وذلك هو مقولة الوضع إذ هو نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض وأنَّ كل واحد منها أين هو من الكل إلخ، ولا يخفى أنه ليس على شيء من هذين الوجهين اعتبار النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع ولعل اعتبارها كما أراد المتأخرون لئلا يكون القيام عين الانعكاس لكن اللازم من عدم اعتبارها هو اتحادهما في الجنس لا في النوع فيجوز أن يختلف بالفصول المقومة وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من أنَّ الجنس والفصل متحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصور أن حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر إنما يرد لو قيل : إِنَّ النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل والنسبة بين الأجزاء جنس بل نقول إنَّ الجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل للجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة بين الأمور الداخلة كالحيوان المأخوذ من البدن والناطق المأخوذ من الصورة النوعية فبعد مفارقته لا تبقى تلك الحصة من الجنس بل تنعدم وإنما تبقى النسبة في الأجزاء التي هي مبدأ لحصة أخرى من الوضع ويقارنها النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة التي هي مبدأ لفصل آخر .
قوله : ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع إلخ) أي على المذهب المختار الذي أثبت بالدليل فيما سبق وإن ذهب أبو علي إلى خلافه كما أشير إليه الآن .
And the objection to what was mentioned in this enumeration: we do not concede that the relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing, so that it is limited to location and possession. Rather, the relation to stable quantity may be in another way, like contact between the surfaces of two bodies, and matching which is coincidence at boundaries. Also, in position, the relation of parts to parts and to the exterior was considered, as has passed, so composition occurs, and it necessitates multiplication of categories, since then it is permissible to consider composition between the relation to quantity and the relation to quality, for example, making a category outside the mentioned categories. Also, the relation to number, which is discrete quantity, remains among the possible categories, and there is no proof of its non-existence. Also, the relation to time, which is continuous non-stable quantity, is not necessarily [limited]...
His statement: 'it was considered' is in the passive voice, indicated by his statement 'as has passed,' meaning that the people considered both relations in position, and the intent is to explain the enumeration in the nine categories that the people established, not the division from the beginning, and to explain the enumeration in its categories. So it is not objected that the master did not consider the relation to external things in position, and the consideration of others is not a proof against him, so multiplication of categories does not follow. Know that the master first transmitted the aspect of enumeration from someone: the accident is either stable in its subject and received from another from outside, not needing relation to that outside, and these are three categories: quantity, quality, and position, etc. Then he said: in the aspect of enumeration that he originated, that every accident either requires in its conception the conception of something outside the subject or not. That which does not require that has three categories: either it does not require that but may require the occurrence of a relation in things that are in it not external to it, or it does not require that in relation. That which does not require that: these external things make the subject divided in some way so that it has parts for each other in a state differing in relation, and that is the category of position, for it is the relation of the parts of the body to each other and that each one of them is where it is from the whole, etc. It is not hidden that in neither of these two aspects is there consideration of the relation to external things in position. Perhaps its consideration is as the later ones intended so that standing would not be identical to reflection, but the necessary consequence of not considering it is their union in genus not in species, so it is permissible for them to differ by constitutive differentiate. And what the commentator (may he be sanctified) mentioned earlier that the genus and differentiate are united in existence and composition, so how can it be conceived that a portion of the genus accompanied a differentia then separated to another differentia? That objection arises only if it is said that the relation to external things is a differentia and the relation between parts is a genus. Rather, we say that the mental part taken from the relation to external things is a differentia for the mental part taken from the relation between internal things, like 'animal' taken from the body and 'rational' taken from the specific form. So after its separation, that portion of the genus does not remain but is annihilated; rather, the relation in the parts that are the beginning of another portion of position remains, and it is accompanied by the relation to external things which is the beginning of another differentia.
His statement: 'And also, it was considered in position, etc.' means according to the chosen doctrine proved by evidence previously, although Abu Ali held the contrary, as indicated now.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the enumeration of nine accident categories and the commentary's response. The objection has several points: first, it denies that relation to stable quantity is only by encompassing (which gives location and possession); there are other modes like contact and matching. Second, in the category of position, both internal and external relations are considered, which allows composition of relations (e.g., quantity and quality), leading to more categories. Third, relation to discrete quantity (number) is omitted without proof. Fourth, relation to time is not necessarily limited. The commentary (starting with 'قوله : (فاعتبرت)') defends the enumeration by explaining that the intended enumeration is based on the method of the early philosophers (the 'people'), not a novel division. It clarifies that the master (Avicenna) did not consider external relations in position, so the objection does not apply. The commentary then discusses the relation between position and reflection, arguing that if external relation is not considered, both collapse into the same genus but differ by species. It refutes the idea that separation of a specific portion of genus is incoherent by explaining that the mental parts (genus and differentia) are taken from different aspects and change accordingly. Finally, it notes that the chosen doctrine is that position involves both internal and external relations, contrary to Abu Ali's view.

Causality and Created Things
beginner

Proof that an Accident Cannot Transfer Between Substrates
Al-Jurjani proves that an accident cannot transfer because its individuation depends on the substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam argument that an accident's individuation is tied to its substrate, so it cannot transfer to another substrate without being destroyed and replaced by a new individual of the same type.

Core Idea
An accident's identity depends on its substrate; leaving destroys it.

Al-Jurjani presents a rigorous proof that accidents do not move from one substance to another. The argument hinges on the concept of individuation (tashakhkhus): if an accident's individuality depends on the very substrate it inheres in, then separation would annihilate that individuality. What appears in a second substrate must be a fresh accident, not a transferred one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the argument is dense and proceeds in conditional form.
2
Understand the conditional premise
The passage starts by stating that if an accident were to transfer, it would have to reside in the new substrate; otherwise there would be circularity. Grasp why this conditional is necessary.
The term 'circularity' refers to a logical regress.
3
Follow the argument against a separate cause
The author argues that the accident's residing depends on its individuation. If the individuation were due to something separate from the substrate, that would be a 'preference without a preponderator' – an unjustified differentiation. Therefore the individuation must be due to the substrate itself.
This is a core kalam principle: no arbitrary distinction.
4
Grasp the conclusion about identity and transfer
Since the substrate determines the accident's identity, leaving the substrate would destroy that identity. What appears in a second substrate is a new individual, not a transferred one. Thus transfer is impossible.
Reflect on the difference between numerical identity and generic similarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الخامس : أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل يحل فيه وإلا دار لأن حلوله في العرض يتوقف على تشخصه ولا لمنفصل لا يكون حالاً فيه ولا محلا له لأن نسبته إلى الكل سواء فكونه علة لتشخص هذا الفرد دون غيره ترجيح بلا مرجح فهو أي تشخصه لمحله فالحاصل في المحل الثاني هوية أخرى أي تشخص آخر غير التشخص الذي كان حاصلا في المحل الأول لأنه لما كان لمحله مدخل في تشخصه لم يتصور مفارقته عنه باقياً تشخصه بل يجب انتفاؤه حينئذ فلا يكون الحاصل في المحل الآخر عين الذي عدم بل شخصاً آخر من نوعه والانتقال من محل إلى آخر لا يتصور إلا مع بقاء الهوية المنتقلة من أحدهما إلى الآخر وإذ لا بقاء للهوية هاهنا فلا انتقال أصلاً.
The first observatory - the fifth objective: that the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another; it would have to reside in it, otherwise there would be circularity because its residing in the accident depends on its individuation, and [it cannot be] for something separate that is neither a locus nor a substrate for it, because its relation to the whole is equal; so its being a cause for the individuation of this particular individual without another is a preference without a preponderator. So it [i.e., its individuation] is due to its substrate. Thus what occurs in the second substrate is another identity, i.e., another individuation other than the individuation that was present in the first substrate, because since its substrate has an influence in its individuation, it is inconceivable that it separates from it while its individuation remains; rather, it must then cease to exist, so what occurs in the other substrate is not the same as what ceased to exist, but rather another individual of the same type. And transfer from one substrate to another is only conceivable with the persistence of the identity transferred from one to the other; and since there is no persistence of identity here, there is no transfer at all.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage states the proof that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another. The argument begins with the claim that if the accident were to transfer, it would have to reside in the new substrate; otherwise circularity would result. The reason given is that the accident's residing in the substrate depends on its individuation (tashakhkhus). If the individuation were due to something separate, that would be a preference without a preponderator. Therefore, the accident's individuation must be due to its substrate. Consequently, what appears in the second substrate is a new identity (huwiyya) — a different individual of the same type, not the original accident. Since the original accident's individuation is tied to its first substrate, leaving would destroy it. Transfer requires the persistence of the same identity, which is impossible here. Hence, no transfer occurs.

Correction: This passage shows that any appearance in a new substrate is a new individual of the same type, not the original accident.

C) Because the accident would need a new cause for its existence.
D) Because the substrate is part of the accident's essence.

Key Takeaway
An accident's individual identity is inseparable from the substrate it inheres in, making true transfer impossible.

The Objection of Self-Individuation and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani refutes the objection that an accident's individuation could be by itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot be individuated by its own essence or identity, and how this leads to the necessity of the substrate.

Core Idea
Self-individuation is impossible because it leads to circularity or part-whole problems.

Al-Jurjani addresses an objection to the earlier proof that an accident cannot transfer. The objector claims that the accident might be individuated by its own essence, avoiding dependence on the substrate. Al-Jurjani examines this claim by unpacking what 'by its own identity' could mean, showing each meaning leads to a logical fallacy. This passage establishes that the substrate is indispensable for individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then refer to the translation. Notice the structure: an objection is raised, then a detailed analysis of the possible meanings of 'by its own identity'.
2
Identify the three meanings of identity
The passage lists three interpretations of 'identity' (al-huwiyya): the thing's own specific identity, its quiddity with individuation, and its concrete existence. Find each one and note the problem associated with each.
Each interpretation is rejected because it leads to a logical contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion
The analysis concludes that none of the three meanings can serve as the cause of individuation without absurdity. This forces the conclusion that the accident's individuation must involve the substrate.
The phrase 'فيكون للمحل مدخل فيه' explicitly brings in the substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر لجواز أن يكون تشخصه الخاصة ولا يلزم حينئذ انحصار النوع في الشخص إنما يلزم ذلك إذا كان بهويته تشخصه بماهيته وفيه بحث لأنه إن أريد بهويته الخاصة تشخصه لزم كون الشيء علة لنفسه وإن أريد ماهيته مع تشخصه كان الكل علة لجزئه وإن أريد وجوده العيني فإن قوله : (لمحله) إما بنفسه أو بما حل فيه فيكون للمحل مدخل فيه فلا يرد أن هاهنا احتمالاً آخر وهو أن يكون تشخصه بما حل في محله كذا قيل وفيه أنه حينئذ يجوز الانتقال عليه لأن المحل لا دخل له في العلية إلا باعتبار الحلول لما هو علة لتشخص العرض فيه وفي شرح المقاصد في رد الاحتمال المذكور أنا ننقل الكلام إلى تشخص ذلك الأمر ويرجع الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور والتسلسل وأورد عليه أنه يجوز أن يحل في محل العرض على سبيل التعاقب أمور غير متناهية يكون كل سابق معداً للتشخص ومثله جائز عند الحكماء والجواب أنَّ الكلام في العلة الفاعلية للتشخص فيجب اجتماعها. قوله : ( لأنه لما كان لمحله مدخل إلخ ) قيل : يجوز أن يكون مدخلية المحل في تشخصه من حيث أنه محل ما لا محل معين فيجوز مفارقته وفيه أنَّ المحل المطلق كيف يوجب تشخص العرض وإن أريد به المحل المعين أي معين كان يلزم توارد العلل على سبيل البدل على معلول واحد شخصي أعني تشخص العرض. قوله : ( وفيه بحث إلخ ) حاصله أنَّ الهوية تطلق على معان ثلاثة لا يصح أن يكون شيء منها علة للتشخص . أنا ننقل قوله : ( فهو أي تشخصه لمحله يعني إذا لم يكن الاقتضاء التام للأمور المذكور فلمحله دخل في تشخصه البتة ويتم المطلوب فعلى هذا لا يرد أن يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون تشخص العرض لأمر حال في محله إذ على هذا التقدير يصدق أيضاً أنَّ للمحل دخلاً في التشخص ولو بالواسطة ويتم المطلوب وأما ما ذكره شارح المقاصد في رد الاحتمال المذكور من الكلام إلى تشخص ذلك الأمر ونرجع آخر الأمر إلى المحل دفعاً للدور أو التسلسل فيرد عليه أنه لم لا يجوز أن يحل في محل العرض على سبيل التعاقب أمور غير متناهية ويكون كل سابق علة معدة لتشخص اللاحق ومثله جائز عند الحكماء هذا وقد يعترض على أصل الاستدلال بأنه يجوز أن يحتاج العرض في تشخصه إلى المحل من حيث هو محل لا إلى محل معين وحينئذ لم لا
And there is a consideration in it [the proof] because it is possible that its individuation is by its own [essence], and then the confinement of the species to the individual does not follow; that would only follow if its individuation by its identity were by its quiddity. And there is a discussion in it because if by its own identity its individuation is intended, then a thing would be a cause of itself; and if its quiddity together with its individuation is intended, then the whole would be a cause of its part; and if its concrete existence is intended, then... His saying: '(due to its substrate)' either by itself or by what resides in it, so the substrate has an influence in it, so it is not refuted that there is another possibility here, namely that its individuation is by what resides in its substrate — thus it is said, and there is in it that then transfer would be possible because the substrate has no role in causality except by virtue of the indwelling of what is the cause of the accident's individuation in it. And in Sharh al-Maqasid, in refuting the mentioned possibility, [it is said]: we transfer the discussion to the individuation of that thing and the matter returns to the substrate to avoid circularity and infinite regress. And an objection was raised against it: it is possible that infinite things reside in the accident's substrate successively, each preceding being a preparer for individuation, and such is permissible for the philosophers. And the answer is that the discussion is about the efficient cause of individuation, so they must be simultaneous. His saying: '(because since its substrate has an influence, etc.)' It was said: it is possible that the substrate's influence in its individuation is insofar as it is a substrate, not a specific substrate, so it is possible for it to separate. And in it is that how can the absolute substrate necessitate the accident's individuation? And if what is intended is the specific substrate, whatever specific it is, then the succession of causes in exchange for one individual effect, namely the accident's individuation, would follow. His saying: '(and there is a discussion, etc.)' Its summary is that 'identity' is used in three meanings, none of which can be a cause of individuation. We transmit his saying: '(so it, i.e., its individuation, is due to its substrate)' means that if the complete exigency of the mentioned matters is not present, then the substrate has an influence in its individuation absolutely, and the desired conclusion is established. On this basis, it is not objected that it is said: why is it not permissible that the accident's individuation be due to a thing residing in its substrate? For on this assumption, it is also true that the substrate has an influence in the individuation, even indirectly, and the desired conclusion is established. As for what the commentator of al-Maqasid mentioned in refuting the mentioned possibility of transferring the discussion to the individuation of that thing, and we return in the final analysis to the substrate to avoid circularity or infinite regress, an objection is raised against it: why is it not permissible that infinite things reside in the accident's substrate successively, each preceding being a preparative cause for the individuation of the following, and such is permissible for the philosophers? This, and it may be objected to the original proof that it is permissible for the accident to need the substrate for its individuation insofar as it is a substrate, not a specific substrate, and then why not...
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the earlier proof that an accident cannot transfer. The objection suggests that the accident could be individuated by its own essence, avoiding dependence on the substrate. The author then analyzes the concept of identity (al-huwiyya) and shows that none of its three possible meanings — the thing's own identity, its quiddity with individuation, or its concrete existence — can serve as the cause of individuation without leading to self-causation or circularity. The discussion then turns to the role of the substrate: whether the substrate's influence is due to it being a specific substrate or just a substrate in general. The author considers various possibilities, including that the accident might be individuated by something residing in the substrate, but this leads to regress. The response from Sharh al-Maqasid is cited, arguing that one must ultimately refer back to the substrate to avoid circularity. However, the author raises further objections about infinite successive causes. The passage concludes by noting an objection to the original proof: that the accident might need a substrate in general, not a particular one, but the text ends incomplete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the accident could be individuated by its own essence without any problem.
Correction: The text shows that 'by its own essence' has three possible interpretations, each leading to a logical contradiction: self-causation, whole-part fallacy, or implicit dependence on the substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what problem arises if we interpret 'by its own identity' as meaning the accident's specific identity?
A) The accident would be a cause of itself.
B) The accident would be a cause of its substrate.
C) The accident would be a cause of its quiddity.
D) The accident would require an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The accident cannot be individuated by its own identity because each interpretation leads to a logical absurdity, proving that the substrate is necessary.

What would it mean for you to truly believe that everything you have and are depends on Allah? What one action can you take today to live out that dependence?
?

In your own faith, have you ever tried to find a 'loophole' to avoid full submission to Allah? Al-Jurjani shows that assuming the accident is individuated by itself leads to absurdity. Is there any belief or habit in your life where you assume you can be good or righteous by your own effort, independent of Allah's grace? How will you remind yourself of your utter need for Him today?

The Substrate's Role and the Regress Objection
Al-Jurjani analyzes further possibilities for the substrate's role and objections to them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various attempts to explain the accident's individuation through the substrate and why they ultimately fail, including the issue of infinite regress.

Core Idea
The substrate's influence must be specific, otherwise the accident could transfer or regress occurs.

Having established that the substrate plays a role in the accident's individuation, Al-Jurjani now considers objections about how exactly the substrate exerts its influence. Could the accident be individuated by something residing in the substrate? Does the substrate need to be a specific substrate or just any substrate? These questions lead to discussions about infinite regress and the nature of causality. This passage exposes the difficulties in pinpointing the cause of individuation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the first objection
The passage begins by raising another possibility: that the accident is individuated by something that resides in its substrate. Understand why this leads to the possibility of transfer.
2
Follow the response from Sharh al-Maqasid
Al-Jurjani cites a response that transfers the discussion to the individuation of that residing thing, ultimately back to the substrate to avoid circularity. Note how this argument works.
3
Consider the infinite regress objection
An objection is raised against the response: why not allow an infinite chain of successive causes? The answer distinguishes between preparative and efficient causes.
The philosophers allow infinite preparatory causes but not simultaneous efficient causes.
4
Analyze the substrate's influence: specific or absolute?
The passage considers whether the substrate's influence is because it is a specific substrate or just any substrate. Each option leads to problems: the first allows transfer, the second leads to succession of causes.
5
Understand the final objection to the original proof
The passage ends with a new objection: perhaps the accident needs a substrate only as such, not a particular one. The text is incomplete, but the challenge is clear.
This objection undermines the original argument that the accident cannot leave its substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is a consideration in it [the proof] because it is possible that its individuation is by its own [essence], and then the confinement of the species to the individual does not follow; that would only follow if its individuation by its identity were by its quiddity. Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the earlier proof that an accident cannot transfer. The objection suggests that the accident could be individuated by its own essence, avoiding dependence on the substrate. The author then analyzes the concept of identity (al-huwiyya) and shows that none of its three possible meanings — the thing's own identity, its quiddity with individuation, or its concrete existence — can serve as the cause of individuation without leading to self-causation or circularity. The discussion then turns to the role of the substrate: whether the substrate's influence is due to it being a specific substrate or just a substrate in general. The author considers various possibilities, including that the accident might be individuated by something residing in the substrate, but this leads to regress. The response from Sharh al-Maqasid is cited, arguing that one must ultimately refer back to the substrate to avoid circularity. However, the author raises further objections about infinite successive causes. The passage concludes by noting an objection to the original proof: that the accident might need a substrate in general, not a particular one, but the text ends incomplete.

Actual vs Essential Possibility for Flowing Accidents
Philosophers argue non-persisting accidents have possibility restricted to their moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers’ distinction between actual possibility (al-imkān al-wuqū'ī) and essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) for non-persisting accidents, and why actual possibility is restricted to the moment of existence.

Core Idea
For flowing accidents, only actual possibility is tied to the moment; essential possibility is eternal.

The philosophers hold that certain accidents—called 'flowing' because they do not persist—have their possibility strictly limited to the instant they exist. To avoid logical contradiction, they carefully distinguish between two types of possibility: actual (the readiness of the subject at that time) and essential (the inherent possibility irrespective of time). This passage explains that restriction and why it is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence: 'They said: And what does not persist from the flowing accidents, its possibility is restricted to its time in which it exists, not before nor after.' Notice the absolute restriction.
2
Identify the two types of possibility
The commentary clarifies that 'its possibility' means actual possibility (al-imkān al-wuqū'ī) – the readiness of the subject – not essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī). Understand why essential possibility cannot be restricted.
Essential possibility is eternal and unchanging; if it were restricted, that would entail a change from impossibility to possibility.
3
Examine the logical argument
The passage says that if actual possibility were not restricted to that moment, before that moment the accident would be impossible (since it lacks readiness), then later becomes possible – an unacceptable change. Thus actual possibility must be restricted.
This avoids the problem of 'al-inqilāb' (change from impossibility to possibility).
4
Understand the context
The passage notes that this discussion is placed here because earlier the Ash'aris argued that every accident is renewed and its specifier is God's will. The philosophers object: only some accidents are renewed, and their specifier is natural causation.
The text is cut off but likely continues with 'and that the specifier is nature.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا وما لا يبقى من الأعراض السيالة ( يختص إمكانه (بوقته الذي وجد فيه ( لا قبل ولا بعد ) أي لا يمكن أن يوجد قبل ذلك الوقت ولا بعده لاستناده إلى سلسلة مقتضية لذلك الاختصاص قوله : ( يختص إمكانه أي إمكان وقوعه دون الذاتي إذ لا اختصاص له بوقت دون وقت . قوله : ( أي لا يمكن أن يوجد إلخ) لامتناع حصول استعداده التام إلا في ذلك الوقت . قوله (قالوا: وما لا يبقى يختص إمكانه (بوقته المراد إمكانه الوقوعي وهو استعداد موضوعه بالفعل لا الذاتي لأن القول باختصاص إمكانه بوقته يستدعي نفي الإمكان قبل ذلك الوقت فيلزم الانقلاب من الامتناع إلى الإمكان ولهذا قالوا بأزلية إمكان كل ممكن ثم إنهم وإن قالوا باختصاص إمكان كل حادث بوقت وقوعه كما علم من قواعدهم إلا أن تخصيص ما لا يبقى بالذكر لاقتضاء سياق الكلام وحسن الانتظام إياه فإنه لما ذكر أولاً أنَّ الأشاعرة حكموا بوجوب تجدد كل عرض وأن مخصص كل من المتجددات بوقته إرادة الفاعل المختار ذكر ثانياً أنَّ الفلاسفة يألفون في كل من الأمرين حيث يحكمون بأن المتجدد بعض الأعراض وأنَّ المخصص
They said: And what does not persist from the flowing accidents (al-a'rāḍ al-sayyāla), its possibility is restricted to its time in which it exists, not before nor after; i.e., it cannot exist before that time nor after it, because it depends on a chain necessitating that restriction. His saying: (its possibility is restricted) meaning the possibility of its occurrence, not the essential [possibility], for there is no restriction of it to a time without time. His saying: (i.e., it cannot exist etc.) due to the impossibility of obtaining its complete readiness except at that time. His saying: (they said: and what does not persist, its possibility is restricted to its time) intended is the actual possibility (al-imkān al-wuqū'ī), which is the actual readiness of its subject, not the essential [possibility], because the statement that its possibility is restricted to its time necessitates negating possibility before that time, thus implying a change from impossibility to possibility. For this reason they said that the possibility of every possible is eternal. Then even though they said that the possibility of every originated thing is restricted to the time of its occurrence, as is known from their principles, nevertheless specifying what does not persist by mention is due to the context of the discourse and good order. For when he first mentioned that the Ash'aris ruled that every accident must be renewed and that the specifier of each of the renewed things to its time is the will of the Agent of Choice, he mentioned secondly that the philosophers harmonize between both matters, as they rule that the renewed is some accidents and that the specifier is...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' view on non-persisting (flowing) accidents. They hold that such accidents have their possibility (imkān) restricted to the exact moment in which they exist—they cannot exist before or after. The reason is that their existence depends on a chain of causes that necessitates this specific timing. The commentary clarifies that 'possibility' here means actual possibility (al-imkān al-wuqū'ī), i.e., the actual readiness of the subject (the substrate) at that time, not essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī) which is unrestricted. If actual possibility were not restricted to that moment, it would imply that before that moment the accident was impossible (since it lacked the readiness), then became possible—a change from impossibility to possibility. To avoid this, they assert that possibility itself (essential) is eternal and does not change. The restriction of actual possibility to the time of occurrence is a separate concept. The commentary also explains why this discussion is specifically about non-persisting accidents: the context is that earlier the Ash'aris had claimed that every accident is renewed and its specifier is God's will; here the philosophers are shown to have a different view: they hold that only some accidents are renewed (the flowing ones) and their specifier is not will but natural causation. The passage ends mid-sentence in the OCR.

الإمكان الوقوعي (al-imkān al-wuqū'ī)
الإمكان الذاتي (al-imkān al-dhātī)
الاستعداد (al-isti'dād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the philosophers deny possibility entirely for flowing accidents, or that they consider the accident itself impossible before its moment.
Correction: They only restrict actual possibility (readiness) to the moment, not essential possibility. The accident is still essentially possible before, but lacks the actual readiness.

Checkpoint

D) Because time itself does not allow it

Key Takeaway
The philosophers maintain that flowing accidents are only actually possible at the moment they occur, because their complete readiness is present only then; essential possibility remains eternal.

The Conditional Premise and Refutation of Self-Perishing and Opposite-Perishing
Al-Jurjani presents the main proof and refutes the first two possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani establishes the conditional premise that if accidents endured they would never perish, and why an accident cannot perish by itself or by the arrival of its opposite.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot perish by themselves or by opposites without circularity.

This lesson begins the main proof that accidents do not endure. The argument hinges on a conditional: if accidents endured, they could never perish. But since they do perish, endurance is false. Here we examine the first two possible ways an accident might perish after enduring and why they are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's opening statement
Carefully read the passage from the beginning up to the explanation of the connection. Notice how the proof is structured: a conditional, a statement that the consequent is false, and then a conclusion.
The proof is called 'the main proof' and is attributed to the authorities.
2
Identify the first two refutations
The passage then explains the conditional by considering four possibilities for how an accident could perish after enduring. Focus on the refutation of the first two: perishing by itself and perishing by an opposite.
The refutation of perishing by itself is brief: if its essence necessitated non-existence, it would never have come to be.
3
Analyze the circularity argument
For perishing by an opposite, two arguments are given: one based on circularity (the opposite's arrival is conditioned on the accident's absence, leading to a loop) and one based on mutual opposition (the remaining accident would repel the opposite more easily).
The second argument is later noted as rhetorical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث وهو العمدة عند الأصحاب في إثبات هذا المطلب أنها أي الأعراض لو بقيت في الزمان الثاني من وجودها امتنع زوالها في الزمان الثالث وما بعده واللازم الذي هو امتناع الزوال باطل بالإجماع وشهادة الحس فإنه يشهد بأن زوال الأعراض واقع بلا اشتباه فيكون الملزوم الذي هو بقاء الأعراض باطلاً أيضاً . بيان الملازمة أنه لو زال العرض بعد بقائه فإما أن يزول بنفسه وإما أن يزول بغيره والغير إما أمر وجودي يوجب عدمه لذاته أي لا باختياره فيكون فاعلاً موجباً وهو طرو الضد على محل العرض أو لا يوجبه لذاته بل باختياره وهو الفاعل المعدوم بالاختيار وإما أمر عدمي وهو زوال الشرط وهذه الأقسام الأربعة الحاصرة للاحتمالات العقلية باطلة أما زواله بنفسه فلان ذاته لو كانت مقتضية لعدمه لوجب أن لا يوجد ابتداء وأما زواله بطرو ضده على محله فلان حدوث الضد في ذلك المحل مشروط بانتفائه عنه فإن المحل ما لم يخل عن ضد لم يمكن اتصافه بضد آخر فلو كان انتفاؤه عن المحل معللاً بطريانه عليه لزم الدور أو نقول في إبطال هذا القسم لما كان التضاد من الطرفين فليس الطارئ بإزالة الباقي أولى من العكس وهو أن يدفع الباقي الطارئ بل الدفع الصادر عن الباقي أهون من الرفع الصادر عن الطارئ فيكون الدفع أقرب إلى الوقوع من الرفع وأما زواله بمعدم مختار فلان الفاعل بالاختيار لا بد له من أثر يصدر عنه والعدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً لمختار . قوله : ( وهذه الأقسام إلخ) الاحتمالات العقلية أربعة لأنَّ زواله إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره والغير إما موجود أو معدوم والموجود إما موجب أو مختار أما حصرها في الأقسام الأربعة فممنوع لأن الموجب لا ينحصر في طرو الضد والمعدوم لا ينحصر في زوال الشرط. قوله : أهون من الرفع إلخ لاحتياج الرفع إلى طريان الطارئ وإزالة الباقي بخلاف دفع الباقي فإنه يحتاج إلى منع الطريان فقط وهذا كما ترى خطابي . قوله : ( لا بد له من أثر إلخ ) إذ الإرادة لا تتعلق بالنفي المحض ولا يكون مقصوداً.
The third aspect, and it is the main proof according to the authorities in establishing this thesis: that they, i.e. accidents, if they remained in the second moment of their existence, their perishing in the third moment and what follows would be impossible. And the consequent, which is the impossibility of perishing, is false by consensus and sensory testimony, for it testifies that the perishing of accidents occurs without ambiguity, so the antecedent, which is the endurance of accidents, is also false. Explanation of the connection: if the accident perished after its endurance, then either it perishes by itself, or it perishes by something else. And that something else is either an existent thing that necessitates its non-existence by itself (i.e., not by choice), so it is a necessitating agent, and that is the advent of the opposite upon the substrate of the accident; or it does not necessitate it by itself but by choice, and that is the agent that causes non-existence by choice; or it is a non-existent thing, and that is the removal of the condition. These four divisions, which exhaust the rational possibilities, are false. As for its perishing by itself, then if its essence necessitated its non-existence, it would have to not exist in the first place. As for its perishing by the advent of its opposite upon its substrate, then the occurrence of the opposite in that substrate is conditioned by its [the first's] absence from it, for the substrate, as long as it is not empty of an opposite, cannot be described by another opposite. So if its absence from the substrate were caused by the advent of the opposite upon it, circularity would follow. Or we say in refutation of this division: since opposition is from both sides, the arriving opposite is no more entitled to remove the remaining one than the reverse, which is that the remaining one repels the arriving one. Rather, the repulsion emanating from the remaining one is easier than the removal emanating from the arriving one, so repulsion is closer to occurrence than removal. As for its perishing by a non-existent [agent] by choice, then an agent by choice must have an effect emanating from him, and non-existence is a pure negation, not suitable as an effect for a chooser. His saying: (And these divisions etc.) The rational possibilities are four because its perishing is either by itself or by something else, and that something else is either existent or non-existent, and the existent is either necessitating or choosing. But its being limited to these four divisions is denied, because the necessitating is not confined to the advent of the opposite, and the non-existent is not confined to the removal of the condition. His saying: easier than removal etc. because removal requires the advent of the arriving one and the removal of the remaining one, unlike repelling the remaining one, for it only requires preventing the advent, and this, as you see, is rhetorical. His saying: (must have an effect etc.) because will does not attach to pure negation and it is not intended.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the third and main proof that accidents do not endure. It begins by stating that if accidents remained in a second moment, they would never perish in the third moment or beyond. But we know by consensus and sensory observation that accidents do perish (e.g., motion becomes rest, light becomes darkness). Therefore, the premise that accidents endure is false. To explain the connection (the conditional premise), the text argues that if an accident were to perish after having endured, it would have to do so in one of four ways: (1) by itself, (2) by an external necessary cause (the arrival of its opposite), (3) by an external free agent, or (4) by the removal of a condition. Each possibility is refuted. (1) If it perished by itself, its essence would necessitate its own non-existence, which would have prevented it from coming into being in the first place. (2) If it perished by the arrival of an opposite, that opposite's arrival would be conditioned on the prior absence of the first, leading to circularity (since the first's absence would then be caused by the opposite's arrival, and the opposite's arrival would be caused by the first's absence). Alternatively, a dialectical argument is given: since opposition is mutual, the arriving opposite is no more able to remove the existing one than the existing one is to repel the arriving one; in fact, repelling is easier than removing, so the existing one would more likely repel the opposite than be removed. (3) If it perished by a free agent (chooser), that agent's act would produce an effect, but pure non-existence is not a positive effect and cannot be intended by a free will. (4) If it perished by removal of a condition, that would also reduce to one of the previous cases. The marginal notes clarify that the enumeration of four possibilities may not be exhaustive (e.g., a necessitating cause could be something other than an opposite, and a non-existent cause could be other than removal of a condition), and that the argument about repulsion being easier is rhetorical rather than decisive.

Key Terms
الوجه الثالث
زوال العرض
طرو الضد
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that accidents can simply 'fade away' on their own over time, without any external cause.
Correction: The proof shows that if an accident perished by itself, its essence would necessitate non-existence, which would have prevented its existence from the start. Therefore, self-perishing is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what is the main reason an accident cannot perish by itself?
A) Because it would require a free agent
B) Because its essence would necessitate non-existence, preventing its initial existence
C) Because opposites are mutually exclusive
D) Because removal of a condition is circular

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot perish by themselves or by the arrival of an opposite without leading to contradiction or circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely experienced a change in your state (e.g., from happiness to sadness). According to this passage, that change cannot be explained by the new state removing the old one. So what must you acknowledge about the real source of all change? How does this affect your reliance on created causes?
?
If accidents cannot endure, then every single characteristic you have—your health, your mood, your knowledge—is being created anew by Allah at every moment. Are you living as if that is true? When you feel a bad habit returning, do you turn to Allah for new creation, or do you assume it just 'stays' and struggle in your own strength?
?
The proof refutes the idea that an opposite can remove what already exists. Think of something in your life that you are trying to remove (e.g., a sin, a negative attitude). Have you been assuming that adding its opposite (more prayer, more knowledge) will automatically remove it? What must you truly rely on for that removal?
