Refutation of Free Agent and Marginal Critique
Al-Jurjani refutes perishing by a free agent and includes marginal critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot perish by a free agent and how the marginal notes critique the enumeration and the rhetorical nature of the dialectical argument.

Core Idea
A free agent cannot produce pure non‑existence as an effect.

After refuting self‑caused and opposite‑caused perishing, al‑Jurjani turns to perishing by a free agent. He argues that non‑existence is not a suitable effect for a chooser. The text then includes marginal notes that critique the exhaustiveness of the four possibilities and note that the dialectical argument about repulsion is rhetorical rather than decisive. Unfortunately, the refutation of the fourth possibility (removal of condition) is missing from our copy.

Guided Path
1
Examine the free agent argument
Read the part starting with 'وأما زواله بمعدم مختار'. Understand why non-existence cannot be an effect of a free agent: the agent's will attaches to existent effects, not to pure absence.
This argument ties into the broader theme of divine action and creation.
2
Analyze the marginal notes
The passage includes comments that challenge the exhaustiveness of the four possibilities. Note that a necessitating cause could be something other than an opposite, and a non-existent cause could be something other than removal of a condition.
The text also comments that the repulsion argument is rhetorical, not decisive.
3
Reflect on the missing part
The refutation of the fourth possibility (removal of condition) is not present in this source. Consider why the author might have considered it reducible to the previous cases or simply omitted it.
This is noted in the review of the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث وهو العمدة عند الأصحاب في إثبات هذا المطلب أنها أي الأعراض لو بقيت في الزمان الثاني من وجودها امتنع زوالها في الزمان الثالث وما بعده واللازم الذي هو امتناع الزوال باطل بالإجماع وشهادة الحس فإنه يشهد بأن زوال الأعراض واقع بلا اشتباه فيكون الملزوم الذي هو بقاء الأعراض باطلاً أيضاً . بيان الملازمة أنه لو زال العرض بعد بقائه فإما أن يزول بنفسه وإما أن يزول بغيره والغير إما أمر وجودي يوجب عدمه لذاته أي لا باختياره فيكون فاعلاً موجباً وهو طرو الضد على محل العرض أو لا يوجبه لذاته بل باختياره وهو الفاعل المعدوم بالاختيار وإما أمر عدمي وهو زوال الشرط وهذه الأقسام الأربعة الحاصرة للاحتمالات العقلية باطلة أما زواله بنفسه فلان ذاته لو كانت مقتضية لعدمه لوجب أن لا يوجد ابتداء وأما زواله بطرو ضده على محله فلان حدوث الضد في ذلك المحل مشروط بانتفائه عنه فإن المحل ما لم يخل عن ضد لم يمكن اتصافه بضد آخر فلو كان انتفاؤه عن المحل معللاً بطريانه عليه لزم الدور أو نقول في إبطال هذا القسم لما كان التضاد من الطرفين فليس الطارئ بإزالة الباقي أولى من العكس وهو أن يدفع الباقي الطارئ بل الدفع الصادر عن الباقي أهون من الرفع الصادر عن الطارئ فيكون الدفع أقرب إلى الوقوع من الرفع وأما زواله بمعدم مختار فلان الفاعل بالاختيار لا بد له من أثر يصدر عنه والعدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً لمختار . قوله : ( وهذه الأقسام إلخ) الاحتمالات العقلية أربعة لأنَّ زواله إما أن يكون لذاته أو لغيره والغير إما موجود أو معدوم والموجود إما موجب أو مختار أما حصرها في الأقسام الأربعة فممنوع لأن الموجب لا ينحصر في طرو الضد والمعدوم لا ينحصر في زوال الشرط. قوله : أهون من الرفع إلخ لاحتياج الرفع إلى طريان الطارئ وإزالة الباقي بخلاف دفع الباقي فإنه يحتاج إلى منع الطريان فقط وهذا كما ترى خطابي . قوله : ( لا بد له من أثر إلخ ) إذ الإرادة لا تتعلق بالنفي المحض ولا يكون مقصوداً.
The third aspect, and it is the main proof according to the authorities in establishing this thesis: that they, i.e. accidents, if they remained in the second moment of their existence, their perishing in the third moment and what follows would be impossible. And the consequent, which is the impossibility of perishing, is false by consensus and sensory testimony, for it testifies that the perishing of accidents occurs without ambiguity, so the antecedent, which is the endurance of accidents, is also false. Explanation of the connection: if the accident perished after its endurance, then either it perishes by itself, or it perishes by something else. And that something else is either an existent thing that necessitates its non-existence by itself (i.e., not by choice), so it is a necessitating agent, and that is the advent of the opposite upon the substrate of the accident; or it does not necessitate it by itself but by choice, and that is the agent that causes non-existence by choice; or it is a non-existent thing, and that is the removal of the condition. These four divisions, which exhaust the rational possibilities, are false. As for its perishing by itself, then if its essence necessitated its non-existence, it would have to not exist in the first place. As for its perishing by the advent of its opposite upon its substrate, then the occurrence of the opposite in that substrate is conditioned by its [the first's] absence from it, for the substrate, as long as it is not empty of an opposite, cannot be described by another opposite. So if its absence from the substrate were caused by the advent of the opposite upon it, circularity would follow. Or we say in refutation of this division: since opposition is from both sides, the arriving opposite is no more entitled to remove the remaining one than the reverse, which is that the remaining one repels the arriving one. Rather, the repulsion emanating from the remaining one is easier than the removal emanating from the arriving one, so repulsion is closer to occurrence than removal. As for its perishing by a non-existent [agent] by choice, then an agent by choice must have an effect emanating from him, and non-existence is a pure negation, not suitable as an effect for a chooser. His saying: (And these divisions etc.) The rational possibilities are four because its perishing is either by itself or by something else, and that something else is either existent or non-existent, and the existent is either necessitating or choosing. But its being limited to these four divisions is denied, because the necessitating is not confined to the advent of the opposite, and the non-existent is not confined to the removal of the condition. His saying: easier than removal etc. because removal requires the advent of the arriving one and the removal of the remaining one, unlike repelling the remaining one, for it only requires preventing the advent, and this, as you see, is rhetorical. His saying: (must have an effect etc.) because will does not attach to pure negation and it is not intended.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents the third and main proof that accidents do not endure. It begins by stating that if accidents remained in a second moment, they would never perish in the third moment or beyond. But we know by consensus and sensory observation that accidents do perish (e.g., motion becomes rest, light becomes darkness). Therefore, the premise that accidents endure is false. To explain the connection (the conditional premise), the text argues that if an accident were to perish after having endured, it would have to do so in one of four ways: (1) by itself, (2) by an external necessary cause (the arrival of its opposite), (3) by an external free agent, or (4) by the removal of a condition. Each possibility is refuted. (1) If it perished by itself, its essence would necessitate its own non-existence, which would have prevented it from coming into being in the first place. (2) If it perished by the arrival of an opposite, that opposite's arrival would be conditioned on the prior absence of the first, leading to circularity (since the first's absence would then be caused by the opposite's arrival, and the opposite's arrival would be caused by the first's absence). Alternatively, a dialectical argument is given: since opposition is mutual, the arriving opposite is no more able to remove the existing one than the existing one is to repel the arriving one; in fact, repelling is easier than removing, so the existing one would more likely repel the opposite than be removed. (3) If it perished by a free agent (chooser), that agent's act would produce an effect, but pure non-existence is not a positive effect and cannot be intended by a free will. (4) If it perished by removal of a condition, that would also reduce to one of the previous cases. The marginal notes clarify that the enumeration of four possibilities may not be exhaustive (e.g., a necessitating cause could be something other than an opposite, and a non-existent cause could be other than removal of a condition), and that the argument about repulsion being easier is rhetorical rather than decisive.

زوال الشرط
الفاعل الموجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God can directly annihilate an accident, creating non-existence as an effect.
Correction: The argument shows that non-existence is not a positive effect and cannot be intended by a free agent; annihilation, if it occurs, must be through something else.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Jurjani argue that an accident cannot perish by a free agent?
A) Because free agents always choose what is best
B) Because non-existence is a pure negation, not a suitable effect for a chooser
C) Because free agents only cause existence, not non-existence
D) Because the accident would have to will its own destruction

Key Takeaway
An accident cannot perish by a free agent because non-existence is not a valid effect, and the marginal notes critique the exhaustiveness and rhetorical aspects of the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read a critique that limiting possibilities to four is not exhaustive. In your own life, do you unconsciously box Allah into a set of 'possible' actions, ruling out how He might answer your duʿā' or guide you? What is one narrow assumption you hold about Him that you need to break? Write it down and then destroy the paper as a sign of abandoning that limitation.
?
The author dismisses an argument as 'rhetorical, not logical.' How often do you accept comforting but shallow reasoning about your faith—concepts like 'everything happens for a reason'—without building a deeper intellectual foundation? Will you commit today to studying one difficult aspect of ʿaqīdah (like this very topic of causality) until it is no longer a rhetorical comfort but a settled certainty in your heart?

Refutation of Cessation by a Volitional Agent
Al-Jurjani refutes the possibility of an accident ceasing by a volitional agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a volitional agent cannot cause the non-existence of an accident, based on the arguments from the nature of non-existence and the requirement of causation for temporal events.

Core Idea
Pure non-existence cannot be an effect of will; temporal events need a cause.

In this passage, al-Jurjani continues his refutation of the theories of how accidents cease. He now addresses the possibility that a volitional agent could cause an accident to cease. The argument rests on two key insights: non-existence is not a proper effect, and temporal events are always in need of a cause, whether they are existence or non-existence. Let's unpack this dense reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refuted claim
The claim is that an accident might cease to exist through the action of a volitional agent (فاعل مختار). Al-Jurjani will show this is impossible.
This is the third possibility for the cessation of accidents.
2
First argument: non-existence as effect
Read the first part of the passage: 'because the volitional agent must have an effect... non-existence is pure negation and is not suitable as an effect for a volitional agent.' The will cannot aim at pure nothingness.
The key term is 'نفي محض' (pure negation).
3
Second argument: temporality requires a cause
The second argument: any temporal event (حادث) needs a cause because of its temporality (حدوث), whether it is existence or non-existence. This refutes the objection that the proof only shows non-occurrence.
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between possibility (إمكان) and temporality as the ground of need for a cause.
4
Final point: agent with no effect is not an agent
The passage concludes: if the effect is non-existence, then there is no effect, and the alleged agent is not an agent at all.
This reinforces the first argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما زواله بمعدم مختار فلان الفاعل بالاختيار لا بد له من أثر ) يصدر عنه ( والعدم نفي محض لا يصلح أثراً) لمختار بل والحادث لا بد له من علة لأنَّ الحدوث علة الحاجة سواء كان وجوداً أو عدماً وبهذا اندفع أنَّ اللازم من الدليل على تقدير تمامه عدم وقوع الزوال لا امتناعه وأنَّ عدم المعلول لعدم علته فالترديد المذكور لا معنى له لأنَّ ذلك على تقدير أن يكون علة الاحتياج الإمكان وأما على تقدير كونها الحدوث فالحادث لا يحتاج في بقائه إلى علة فضلاً عن أن يكون عدمه لعدمها كما ولا لفاعل أصلاً ( أو نقول ) في الظال كون زوال للمختار ( ما أثره عدم فلا أثر له إذ لا فرق بين قولنا أثره لا وقولنا لا أثر له كما مر في بحث الإمكان ( فليس) الفاعل الذي أسند إليه زوال العرض (فاعلاً ) أصلاً سواء فرض حاراً أو موجناً

قوله : ( لا بد له من أثر إلخ ) إذ الإرادة لا تتعلق بالنفي المحض ولا يكون مقصوداً. قوله : ( ولا لفاعل أصلاً) إذ أثر الفاعل لا يكون لا شيئاً محضاً .
And as for its cessation by a non-existent (i.e., one who causes non-existence) by choice, because the volitional agent must have an effect that proceeds from it, and non-existence is pure negation and is not suitable as an effect for a volitional agent. Rather, the event also must have a cause, because temporality (ḥudūth) is the cause of need, whether the event is existence or non-existence. And by this it is repelled that the implication of the proof, assuming its completeness, is the non-occurrence of cessation, not its impossibility, and that the non-existence of the effect is due to the non-existence of its cause. So the aforementioned disjunction has no meaning, because that is on the assumption that the cause of need is possibility (imkān); but on the assumption that it is temporality, the temporal does not need a cause for its continuance, let alone that its non-existence be due to the non-existence of its cause, as... and no agent at all (or we say) in the invalidation of its cessation by the volitional: (what has non-existence as its effect has no effect, for there is no difference between our saying 'its effect is not' and our saying 'it has no effect', as was mentioned in the discussion of possibility. So) the agent to whom the cessation of the accident is attributed is not an agent at all, whether it be assumed hot or cold.

His statement: (Must have an effect, etc.): Because the will does not attach to pure negation, nor can it be intended. His statement: (And no agent at all): Because the effect of an agent cannot be pure nothing.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage refutes the third possibility: that an accident ceases by a volitional agent (فاعل مختار). The argument proceeds as follows: A volitional agent acts by choice and must produce an effect (أثر). Non-existence (عدم) is pure negation (نفي محض) and cannot be an effect, because the will (إرادة) does not attach to pure negation; no one intends non-existence for its own sake. Moreover, any event (حادث) requires a cause (علة) because temporality (حدوث) itself is what necessitates a cause, whether the event is existence or non-existence. This refutes the objection that the proof only shows non-occurrence, not impossibility. The author explains that if the cause of need is possibility (إمكان), then the argument stands; but if it is temporality, then a temporal thing does not need a cause for its persistence, so its non-existence is not due to the cause's non-existence. Then a second argument is given: if the volitional agent's effect is non-existence, it has no effect, because 'its effect is not' is the same as 'it has no effect'. Thus the alleged agent is not an agent at all, regardless of whether it is characterized as hot or cold. The marginal glosses clarify: the will cannot aim at pure non-existence, and an agent's effect cannot be mere nothing.

Key Terms
فاعل مختار (volitional agent)
أثر (effect)
عدم (non-existence)
نفي محض (pure negation)
حدوث (temporality)
إرادة (will)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that non-existence can be an effect of a volitional agent, e.g., when someone destroys something, they intend the non-existence.
Correction: The text clarifies that pure non-existence as such cannot be intended; only the existence of a destroyer can be intended. Non-existence is pure negation and not a suitable effect for will.

Checkpoint
Why can't a volitional agent cause the cessation of an accident according to this refutation?
A) Because the agent would have to be an opposite
B) Because non-existence is pure negation and cannot be an effect of will
C) Because the accident is eternal
D) Because volitional agents can only create, not destroy

Key Takeaway
A volitional agent cannot cause pure non-existence, and the cessation of an accident is a temporal event requiring a cause, thus it cannot be attributed to a willing agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you desire something to cease—like a bad habit or a difficult situation—do you focus on the non-existence itself, or do you actively seek a cause that would bring about its removal? How does this passage correct your understanding of intention?
?
You have likely prayed for hardship to end. According to this lesson, can non-existence (the end of hardship) be directly willed? What does that imply about how you should direct your prayers and actions?
?
Consider a time you blamed someone for 'causing' something to cease. How does Al-Jurjani's argument that such an agent is 'not an agent at all' challenge your view of responsibility and causation in your interactions with others?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate

Refutation of Cessation by Removal of a Condition: Infinite Regress and Circularity
Al-Jurjani refutes cessation by removal of condition using infinite regress and circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an accident cannot cease to exist simply because a condition is removed, and how al-Jurjani uses infinite regress and circularity arguments to refute this possibility.

Core Idea
Removing a condition does not explain an accident's cessation without leading to impossibility.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles the fourth possibility: that an accident ceases because a condition for its persistence is removed. He examines two cases—whether the condition is another accident or a substance—and shows that both lead to absurdities: infinite regress or circularity. The commentary then responds to potential objections, strengthening the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Read the opening lines of the source passage where al-Jurjani presents the two cases (condition as accident or substance) and the claimed consequences (infinite regress, circularity).
Notice the structure: the author first states the possibility, then refutes it.
2
Understand the infinite regress argument
Focus on the first case: if the condition is another accident, then its removal requires a further condition, leading to an infinite chain. Ask why this chain is impossible.
The impossibility is that infinite accidents would exist simultaneously.
3
Understand the circularity argument
Second case: if the condition is a substance, then each depends on the other for persistence, creating a circle. Consider why this is problematic.
The marginal gloss explains that the circle is not automatic; it requires specific identity conditions.
4
Examine the objections and responses
Read the marginal notes where al-Jurjani's commentator addresses potential objections, such as the possibility of reciprocal condition or observation of accidents ceasing without locus removal.
These responses refine the argument and show its universal applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما زواله بزوال شرط، فلأن ذلك الشرط إن كان عرضاً ) آخر ( تسلسل ) لأنَّا ننقل الكلام إلى العرض الذي هو الشرط فيكون زواله بزوال شرطه الذي هو عرض ثالث وهكذا فيلزم وجود أعراض غير متناهية بعضها شرط لبعض ( وإن كان ذلك الشرط (جوهراً والجوهر) في بقائه مشروط بالعرض لزم (الدور لأن بقاء كل واحد من الجوهر والعرض مشروط ببقاء الآخر موقوف عليه ) والاعتراض عليه ) أي على هذا الدليل الذي عده عمدة أنا نختار

قوله : ( فيلزم وجود إلخ ) أي حين زوال العرض وجود أعراض غير متناهية وهو محال . قوله : ( لأن بقاء كل واحد من الجوهر إلخ) أما كون بقاء العرض مشروطاً بالجوهر فبالعرض وأما كون الجوهر مشروطاً ببقاء العرض فلان وجود الجوهر مشروط بوجود العرض كالكون في الحيز مثلاً في كل زمان فإن قلتم بتجدد الأكوان ثبت المطلوب وهو امتناع بقاء العرض وإن قلتم ببقائها كان بقاء الجوهر مشروطاً ببقائه وفيه بحث أما أولاً فلأنه إنما يلزم الدور لو كان العرض الذي هو مشروط بعينه العرض الذي هو شرط وكذا الجوهر الذي هو شرط بعينه الجوهر الذي هو مشروط وكلا الأمرين غير لازم وأما ثانيا فلأنا لا نسلم ثبوت المطلوب على تقدير القول بتجدد العرض الذي هو شرط بقاء الجوهر إنما اللازم منه امتناع بقاء ذلك العرض. فيلزم الاجتماع المستحيل مدفوع بأن وجوده في محل الباقي وزوال الباقي عنه في آن واحد وإن تقدم الأول على الثاني بالذات فلا محذور. قوله : ( وإن كان جوهراً والجوهر مشروط بالعرض لزم الدور) قد أشرنا في أول المقصد إلى جواز أن يكون مثله من قبيل الشرط المتعاكس لا بد لنفيه من دليل على أن الدور لو سلم فإنما هو على تقدير كون الجوهر الشرط محل العرض الزائل فأما لو عمم فلا يظهر الدور وإنما لم ينقل الكلام إلى زوال الجوهر الشرط لأنه محسوس والتشكيك فيه سفسطة لا يعبأ به فإن قلت : قد يشاهد أنَّ العرض يزول محله فلا حاجة في إبطال كون زوال العرض بزوال شرطه الجوهر إلى إلزام الدور، قلت : ما ذكرته إبطال في بعض المواد والمدعى كلي بقي شيء وهو أن بقاء الجوهر عند المستدل مشروط بوجود العرض لا ببقائه على تقدير عدم بقاء الأعراض كما سلف منا في أول المقصد فعلى تقدير بقاء الأعراض ينبغي أن يثبت المستدل لزوم كون شرط بقاء الجوهر بقاء العرض لا نفس وجوده حتى يلزم الدور بزعمه إذ مجرد بقاء العرض لا يستلزم أن يكون نفسه شرط بقاء الجوهر كما لا يخفى .
As for its cessation by the removal of a condition, because if that condition is another accident, there is an infinite regress (tasalsul), for we transfer the discussion to the accident that is the condition, so its cessation is by the removal of its condition which is a third accident, and so on; thus infinite accidents are implied, some being conditions for others. (And if that condition is a substance) and the substance in its persistence is conditional upon the accident, then circularity (dawr) follows, because the persistence of each—substance and accident—is conditional upon the persistence of the other and dependent on it.) And the objection to it, i.e., to this proof which is considered a main proof, is that we choose...

His statement: (Thus the existence of infinite accidents is implied): i.e., at the time of the accident's cessation, the existence of infinite accidents, which is impossible. His statement: (Because the persistence of each of the substance and accident, etc.): As for the persistence of the accident being conditional upon the substance, it is by accident; as for the substance being conditional upon the persistence of the accident, it is because the existence of the substance is conditional upon the existence of the accident, like being-in-a-locus (al-kawn fī al-ḥayyiz) for example, at every time. If you say that the accidents are renewed (mutaqaddida), the desired conclusion is established, which is the impossibility of the accident persisting. But if you say that they persist, then the persistence of the substance is conditional upon its persistence. But there is discussion: First, the circularity would only follow if the accident that is the conditioned were the very accident that is the condition, and similarly the substance that is the condition were the very substance that is conditioned—and neither is necessary. Second, we do not grant the establishment of the desired conclusion on the assumption of the renewal of the accident that is the condition for the persistence of the substance; what follows from it is only the impossibility of that accident persisting. And the claim that impossible conjunction follows is refuted because: its existence in the locus of the remaining and the removal of the remaining from it occur in one instant, even if the first precedes the second in essence, so there is no problem. His statement: (And if it is a substance, and the substance is conditional upon the accident, circularity follows); we have already indicated at the beginning of the topic that such a case may be an instance of reciprocal condition (sharṭ mutaʿākis). Its negation requires a proof that, even if circularity is granted, it is only on the assumption that the substance which is the condition is the locus of the acc-removed accident. But if generalised, the circularity does not appear. And the discussion was not transferred to the removal of the substance-condition because it is perceptible (maḥsūs), and doubt about it is sophistry (safsata) not to be considered. If you say: It is sometimes observed that the accident ceases while its locus remains, so there is no need in invalidating the cessation of the accident by the removal of its substance-condition to necessitate circularity. I say: What you mentioned is an invalidation in some particular cases, while the claim is universal. There remains a point: the persistence of the substance according to the proponent is conditional upon the existence of the accident, not upon its persistence, on the assumption that accidents do not persist, as we previously mentioned at the beginning of the topic. So on the assumption of the persistence of accidents, the proponent should prove that the condition for the persistence of the substance is the persistence of the accident, not its mere existence, so that circularity would follow as he claims. For mere persistence of the accident does not imply that it itself is the condition for the substance's persistence, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth possibility: that an accident ceases by the removal of a condition. Two cases are considered: (1) If the condition is another accident, then an infinite regress (تسلسل) follows because the removal of that condition would itself require a condition, ad infinitum, implying an impossible infinite chain of accidents. (2) If the condition is a substance (جوهر), then circularity (دور) arises because the substance's persistence depends on the accident (e.g., its location), and the accident's persistence depends on the substance (as its locus). The marginal glosses provide detailed responses and objections. The first marginal note explains that the infinite regress is impossible. The second discusses the circularity argument: it is not automatic because the conditioned and condition must be identical in a specific way; also, if accidents are renewed, it only proves the impossibility of that particular accident persisting, not the general claim. The objection that an accident can be observed to cease while its locus remains is addressed: the argument is universal, and the proponent must prove that the substance's persistence requires the persistence of the accident (not just its existence) for circularity to hold. Overall, this refutation relies on the impossibility of infinite regress and circularity in causal conditions.

Key Terms
شرط (condition)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
دور (circularity)
جوهر (substance)
عرض (accident)
بقاء (persistence)
علة (cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any removal of a condition automatically explains an accident's cessation without further problems.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that this leads to either infinite regress (if condition is another accident) or circularity (if condition is a substance), both of which are impossible or require careful qualification.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani argue would happen if the condition for an accident's persistence is another accident?
A) The accident would persist eternally
B) An infinite regress of accidents would follow
C) The substance would cease to exist
D) The condition would become a substance

Key Takeaway
An accident cannot cease by removal of a condition because it leads to either infinite regress or circularity; the refutation is nuanced and accounts for objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief or habit you maintain because you think it is sustained by some condition (e.g., 'I will feel secure if I have wealth'). How does this lesson's refutation of cessation by removal of a condition challenge you to see that such conditions are themselves dependent on something else, leading to either an infinite chain or circular reasoning? What does this reveal about your need for an ultimate, unconditional foundation?
?
The lesson distinguishes between two types of conditions—accidents and substances. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse different kinds of dependencies? For instance, do you treat a temporary state (like a feeling) as if it were a stable support (like a virtue)? How can this lesson's precision help you avoid circular reasoning in your spiritual self-assessment?
?
If the removal of a condition cannot truly explain cessation, then what does that imply about the source of all persistence? Consider something you deeply rely on—could it be that you have been placing your trust in conditions that are themselves impermanent? How does this lesson push you to anchor your hope solely in the One who is not subject to conditions?

The First Proof: Turning the Opponent's Argument Against Them
Refutation of the first argument against accident cessation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator refutes the claim that an accident cannot perish by itself by using the opponent's own argument against them.

Core Idea
The opponent's argument is turned back on them.

The commentator begins by addressing the claim that if an accident perishes by itself, it would never have existed originally. He denies this, explaining that its essence could bring about non-existence in a later moment, conditioned on prior existence. Then he shows that the same reasoning applies to the opponent's position regarding the second moment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the bracketed insertions typical of commentary style.
Focus on how the opponent's claim is initially countered.
2
Identify the initial claim and counter
The opponent says if an accident perishes by itself, it would never exist. The commentator denies this, arguing that it could perish in a later moment, not immediately.
The key is the conditional nature: its essence's requirement of non-existence is conditioned on prior existence.
3
Observe the reversal
The commentator then applies the same reasoning to the opponent: their own argument about the second moment can be turned against them. Whatever answer they give for the counterexample is the same answer the commentator gives.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
This argument shows that the opponent's reasoning is self-defeating. The commentator does not need a separate proof; the opponent's own logic suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والحكم بأن الطارئ ليس أولى بإزالة الباقي من عكسه باطل لأن الطارئ أقوى لقربه من السبب وبعد الباقي عنه ( وأيضا فقد يزول لأن الفاعل الذي فعله لا يفعله لا لأنه يفعل عدمه وذلك لا يحتاج إلى أثر (للفاعل) صادر عنه بل مجرد امتناع الفاعل من إبقاء ما فعله كاف في زواله ) وأيضاً لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أن يكون ( أثراً ) صادراً عن الفاعل ( نعم ذلك ) مسلم ( في العدم المستمر وأما العدم الحادث فقد يكون بفعل الفاعل) كالوجود الحادث ( وما الدليل على امتناعه وأيضاً فقد يزول بزوال شرط قولك هو الجوهر ) إذ لو كان عرضاً تسلسل وإذا كان ذلك الشرط هو الجوهر المشروط في بقائه بالعرض ( فيدور قلنا : ممنوع ) إذا لا دور ولا تسلسل ( ولم لا يجوز أن يكون )
( That it perishes by itself, your statement that it does not exist ) originally ( is denied because it is possible that ) its essence ( brings about non-existence in the third or fourth moment specifically, i.e., not in the second moment, so it does not follow that its essence brings about non-existence absolutely such that it is impossible and thus it does not exist originally. Rather, it follows that its essence's requirement of its non-existence in a moment is conditioned by its existence in a previous moment, and its impossibility is denied. (Then this) proof that you mentioned ( applies to you regarding the second moment exactly ), namely by saying: its perishing in the second moment is not permissible because its perishing in it is either due to another, etc. ( So whatever your answer ) regarding it in the form of a counterargument ( is our answer ) regarding it in the form of the dispute. ( And also it may perish by a contrary ) that arises in its substrate. ( Your statement that its occurrence ) in that substrate ( is conditioned by its perishing ) from it ( we say: If you require in the condition its priority over the conditioned, we deny ) that the occurrence of the arising contrary is conditioned by the perishing of the remaining contrary, since there is no proof for it except the impossibility of coexistence, and that does not indicate this conditionality. ( Otherwise ) i.e., if you do not require priority in the condition but are content with mere inseparability, ( then reciprocity is not impossible ), so it is possible that each is a condition for the other, and the resulting circularity is a circularity of simultaneity, ( just as the entry of each part of the ) ring ( rotating on itself ) into the space of the other part is conditioned by the exit of the other from it, and vice versa, and there is no harm in that because its reference is their mutual entailment. ( In summary ) i.e., whether we allow reciprocity in conditionality or not, ( they are ) i.e., the perishing of the remaining and the arising of the new ( simultaneous in ) time, and this simultaneity does not contradict causality, ( for causality is priority in intellect; thus its arising may be the cause of the perishing of the remaining ) while they are simultaneous in time, like cause and effect, ( for they are approximate in terms of time while the cause is prior in intellect ).

( And the judgment that the arising is not more entitled to remove the remaining than its opposite is false, because the arising is stronger due to its closeness to the cause and the remaining's distance from it. ( And also it may perish because the agent that did it does not do it ), not because it brings about its non-existence, and that does not require an effect from the agent, but rather mere refraining of the agent from sustaining what it did is sufficient for its perishing. ( And also we do not grant that non-existence cannot be an effect ) proceeding from the agent. ( Yes, that ) is granted ( regarding perpetual non-existence, but as for originated non-existence, it may be by the act of the agent ) like originated existence. ( And what is the proof for its impossibility? And also it may perish by the removal of a condition. Your statement that it is the substance ), for if it were an accident, there would be an infinite regress, and if that condition is the substance conditioned in its persistence by the accident, ( then there is circularity. We say: It is denied ), for there is no circularity and no infinite regress. ( And why should it not be that )
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a series of five refutations of the claim that an accident does not endure for two moments. The author addresses an opponent who argues that if an accident perishes by itself, it would not exist originally. The author first denies this, noting that its essence could bring about non-existence in a later moment, not immediately, so the requirement is conditional. Then, the opponent's own argument is turned against them: their reasoning about the second moment applies equally to them, and whatever answer they give, the author gives the same. Second, the accident may perish due to a contrary that arises in its substrate. The author argues against the condition that the contrary's occurrence requires the prior perishing of the accident; the only proof is the impossibility of coexistence, which does not imply that conditionality. If one does not require temporal priority, then reciprocity is possible, like the parts of a ring, where each part's entering is conditioned by the other's exiting, resulting in a circularity of simultaneity, which is harmless. They are simultaneous, and causality is only priority in intellect, not time. Third, the judgment that the arising contrary is not more entitled to remove the remaining is false because the arising is closer to the cause. Fourth, the accident may perish simply because the agent refrains from sustaining it, without needing an effect of non-existence. Fifth, originated non-existence can be an effect of the agent, just like originated existence, so the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect is denied for originated non-existence. Finally, the accident may perish by the removal of a condition; if the condition is the substance, there is no circularity or infinite regress, and it is possible.

Key Terms
عرض
بقاء
زمان
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An accident cannot perish by itself because then it would never exist originally.
Correction: The commentator shows it could perish in a later moment conditioned on prior existence, so the original existence is not negated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the commentator deny regarding the claim that an accident perishes by itself?
A) That the accident can perish at all.
B) That the accident's essence brings about non-existence in the second moment.
C) That the accident's essence cannot bring about non-existence absolutely.
D) That the opponent's argument applies to the second moment.

Key Takeaway
The commentator shows that the opponent's own reasoning can be used to support the possibility of an accident's cessation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you assume that something must continue exactly as it is, forgetting that its existence might be conditional? How does this lesson's distinction between absolute and conditional necessity challenge that assumption?
?
When you face a disagreement, do you ever take your opponent's reasoning and turn it back on them to test your own position? If not, what fear holds you back from that kind of intellectual honesty?
?
This lesson shows that even logical arguments can be reversed. Have you ever been absolutely certain of a belief, only to realize later that your own reasoning applied to a different case would undermine it? How does that possibility change the way you hold your current convictions?

The Second Proof: Perishing by a Contrary and the Possibility of Reciprocal Conditionality
Defense of perishing by a contrary with conditionality analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the possibility of an accident perishing by an arising contrary, addressing conditionality and reciprocity.

Core Idea
An accident can perish due to a contrary, and mutual conditionality is possible without harm.

The commentator next addresses the argument that an accident may perish due to a contrary that arises in its substrate. He discusses whether the occurrence of the contrary is conditioned on the prior perishing of the accident, and shows that even if one does not require temporal priority, reciprocal conditionality (such as in the example of a ring's parts) is possible and does not entail absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Follow the discussion on conditionality. The opponent claims the occurrence of a contrary requires the prior perishing of the accident.
2
Understand the two options
The commentator presents two possibilities: either the condition requires temporal priority (which he denies due to no proof) or it only requires inseparability. In the latter case, reciprocity is possible.
3
Examine the ring example
The example of a rotating ring's parts shows how entry is conditioned by exit and vice versa, illustrating harmless reciprocal conditionality (دور معية).
4
Note the conclusion
The perishing of the remaining and the arising of the new can be simultaneous in time, and causality is only priority in intellect, not time.

( أنه يزول بنفسه قولك فلا يوجد ) ابتداء ( ممنوع لجواز أن يوجب ) ذاته ( العدم في الزمان الثالث أو الرابع خاصة أي دون الزمان الثاني فلا يلزم أن يوجب ذاته العدم مطلقاً حتى يكون ممتنعاً فلا يوجد ابتداء بل يلزم أن يكون اقتضاء ذاته عدمه في زمان مشروطاً بوجوده في زمان سابق عليه واستحالته ممنوعة (ثم هذا الدليل) الذي ذكرتموه ( وارد عليكم في الزمان الثاني بعينه ) وذلك بأن يقال : لا يجوز زواله في الزمان الثاني لأن زواله فيه إما لغيره إلى آخر الكلام ( فما هو جوابكم) عنه في صورة النقض ) فهو جوابنا ) عنه في صورة النزاع ) وأيضا قد يزول بضد ) طارئ على محله ( قولك حدوثه ) في ذلك المحل ( مشروط بزواله ) عنه ( قلنا : إن أوجبت في الشرط تقدمه على المشروط (منعنا ) كون حدوث الضد الطارئ مشروطاً بزوال الضد الباقي إذ لا دليل عليه سوى امتناع الاجتماع ولا دلالة له على هذا الاشتراط ( وإلا ) أي وإن لم توجب في الشرط تقدمه بل اكتفيت بمجرد امتناع الانفكاك (لم يمتنع التعاكس) كما فجاز أن يكون كل منهما شرطاً للآخر ويكون الدور اللازم منه دور معية ( كما أن دخول كل جزء من أجزاء ( الحلقة ( الدوارة على نفسها (في حيز) الجزء ( الآخر مشروط بخروج الآخر عنه وبالعكس ولا محذور في ذلك لأن مرجعه إلى تلازمهما ( وبالجملة ) أي سواء جوز التعاكس في الاشتراط أو لا ( فهما ) أي زوال الباقي وطريان الحادث ( معاً في (الزمان ) وهذه المعية لا تنافي العلية (إذ العلية تقدم في العقل فقد يكون طريانه علة لزوال الباقي (مع كونهما معاً في الزمان كالعلة والمعلول ( فإنهما متقاربان بحسب الزمان مع كون العلة متقدمة في العقل

والحكم بأن الطارئ ليس أولى بإزالة الباقي من عكسه باطل لأن الطارئ أقوى لقربه من السبب وبعد الباقي عنه ( وأيضا فقد يزول لأن الفاعل الذي فعله لا يفعله لا لأنه يفعل عدمه وذلك لا يحتاج إلى أثر (للفاعل) صادر عنه بل مجرد امتناع الفاعل من إبقاء ما فعله كاف في زواله ) وأيضاً لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أن يكون ( أثراً ) صادراً عن الفاعل ( نعم ذلك ) مسلم ( في العدم المستمر وأما العدم الحادث فقد يكون بفعل الفاعل) كالوجود الحادث ( وما الدليل على امتناعه وأيضاً فقد يزول بزوال شرط قولك هو الجوهر ) إذ لو كان عرضاً تسلسل وإذا كان ذلك الشرط هو الجوهر المشروط في بقائه بالعرض ( فيدور قلنا : ممنوع ) إذا لا دور ولا تسلسل ( ولم لا يجوز أن يكون )
( That it perishes by itself, your statement that it does not exist ) originally ( is denied because it is possible that ) its essence ( brings about non-existence in the third or fourth moment specifically, i.e., not in the second moment, so it does not follow that its essence brings about non-existence absolutely such that it is impossible and thus it does not exist originally. Rather, it follows that its essence's requirement of its non-existence in a moment is conditioned by its existence in a previous moment, and its impossibility is denied. (Then this) proof that you mentioned ( applies to you regarding the second moment exactly ), namely by saying: its perishing in the second moment is not permissible because its perishing in it is either due to another, etc. ( So whatever your answer ) regarding it in the form of a counterargument ( is our answer ) regarding it in the form of the dispute. ( And also it may perish by a contrary ) that arises in its substrate. ( Your statement that its occurrence ) in that substrate ( is conditioned by its perishing ) from it ( we say: If you require in the condition its priority over the conditioned, we deny ) that the occurrence of the arising contrary is conditioned by the perishing of the remaining contrary, since there is no proof for it except the impossibility of coexistence, and that does not indicate this conditionality. ( Otherwise ) i.e., if you do not require priority in the condition but are content with mere inseparability, ( then reciprocity is not impossible ), so it is possible that each is a condition for the other, and the resulting circularity is a circularity of simultaneity, ( just as the entry of each part of the ) ring ( rotating on itself ) into the space of the other part is conditioned by the exit of the other from it, and vice versa, and there is no harm in that because its reference is their mutual entailment. ( In summary ) i.e., whether we allow reciprocity in conditionality or not, ( they are ) i.e., the perishing of the remaining and the arising of the new ( simultaneous in ) time, and this simultaneity does not contradict causality, ( for causality is priority in intellect; thus its arising may be the cause of the perishing of the remaining ) while they are simultaneous in time, like cause and effect, ( for they are approximate in terms of time while the cause is prior in intellect ).

( And the judgment that the arising is not more entitled to remove the remaining than its opposite is false, because the arising is stronger due to its closeness to the cause and the remaining's distance from it. ( And also it may perish because the agent that did it does not do it ), not because it brings about its non-existence, and that does not require an effect from the agent, but rather mere refraining of the agent from sustaining what it did is sufficient for its perishing. ( And also we do not grant that non-existence cannot be an effect ) proceeding from the agent. ( Yes, that ) is granted ( regarding perpetual non-existence, but as for originated non-existence, it may be by the act of the agent ) like originated existence. ( And what is the proof for its impossibility? And also it may perish by the removal of a condition. Your statement that it is the substance ), for if it were an accident, there would be an infinite regress, and if that condition is the substance conditioned in its persistence by the accident, ( then there is circularity. We say: It is denied ), for there is no circularity and no infinite regress. ( And why should it not be that )
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a series of five refutations of the claim that an accident does not endure for two moments. The author addresses an opponent who argues that if an accident perishes by itself, it would not exist originally. The author first denies this, noting that its essence could bring about non-existence in a later moment, not immediately, so the requirement is conditional. Then, the opponent's own argument is turned against them: their reasoning about the second moment applies equally to them, and whatever answer they give, the author gives the same. Second, the accident may perish due to a contrary that arises in its substrate. The author argues against the condition that the contrary's occurrence requires the prior perishing of the accident; the only proof is the impossibility of coexistence, which does not imply that conditionality. If one does not require temporal priority, then reciprocity is possible, like the parts of a ring, where each part's entering is conditioned by the other's exiting, resulting in a circularity of simultaneity, which is harmless. They are simultaneous, and causality is only priority in intellect, not time. Third, the judgment that the arising contrary is not more entitled to remove the remaining is false because the arising is closer to the cause. Fourth, the accident may perish simply because the agent refrains from sustaining it, without needing an effect of non-existence. Fifth, originated non-existence can be an effect of the agent, just like originated existence, so the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect is denied for originated non-existence. Finally, the accident may perish by the removal of a condition; if the condition is the substance, there is no circularity or infinite regress, and it is possible.

Key Terms
ضد
شرط
دور
علية
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The occurrence of a contrary must be temporally prior to the perishing of the previous accident.
Correction: The commentator shows that they can be simultaneous, and even reciprocal conditionality is possible without absurdity.

Checkpoint
What example does the commentator use to illustrate that reciprocal conditionality is possible?
A) The entry and exit of parts of a rotating ring.
B) The relationship between cause and effect.
C) The persistence of substance.
D) The arising of a contrary.

Key Takeaway
An accident can perish by a contrary, and the conditionality can be reciprocal without harm.

Causality and Created Things

The Third Proof: The Arising is Stronger; The Fourth: The Agent's Withholding
Two short proofs: strength of arising and agent's withholding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional proofs: the arising contrary is stronger due to its proximity to the cause, and the accident may perish simply because the agent refrains from sustaining it.

Core Idea
The arising contrary is naturally stronger, and the agent's mere withholding is sufficient for an accident's cessation.

The commentator presents two brief but distinct arguments. First, the arising contrary is more forceful than the remaining one because it is closer to the cause. Second, an accident may cease without any positive act of destruction: the agent's mere decision not to sustain it is enough, as no effect of non-existence is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read both arguments
The passage contains two separate proofs linked by 'وأيضا'. Read each and notice their brevity.
2
Understand the first argument
The commentator rejects the claim that the arising is not more entitled to remove the remaining. He argues that the arising is stronger due to its closeness to the cause and the remaining's distance.
3
Understand the second argument
The agent's mere withholding of sustenance is sufficient for the accident to perish. No positive act of bringing about non-existence is required.
4
Reflect on the implications
Both arguments show that an accident's cessation does not always require an external efficient cause; sometimes its own nature or the agent's inaction suffices.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the arising contrary stronger?
A) Because it is closer to the cause and the remaining is farther.
B) Because it is more recent in time.
C) Because it has a greater essence.
D) Because it is created by a different agent.

Key Takeaway
An accident may cease either because a stronger contrary arises or simply because the agent stops sustaining it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you realize that your good deeds cease not only because of sin (a contrary arising) but also because you fail to sustain them through continued effort? What are you doing today to actively maintain the good you have?
?
Consider a time when your faith or a good habit faded. Was it due to a stronger opposing desire, or simply because you stopped nurturing it? How can you bring your deeds closer to the cause (the command of Allah) so they are not overcome?
?
If mere withholding of the agent suffices for loss, then what in your spiritual life is perishing right now because you are refraining from sustaining it—whether it be prayer, charity, or patience? Will you take one concrete step today to reverse that withholding?

The Fifth Proof: Originated Non-existence as an Effect of the Agent
Argument that originated non-existence can be an effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that originated non-existence can be a genuine effect of an agent, contrary to the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect.

Core Idea
Originated non-existence can be caused by an agent, just like existence.

The commentator now addresses the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect proceeding from an agent. He concedes this for perpetual non-existence but argues that originated non-existence (like the cessation of an accident at a specific time) can be brought about by the agent, just as originated existence is. He challenges the opponent to provide a proof for its impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
This short argument distinguishes between perpetual non-existence (عدم مستمر) and originated non-existence (عدم حادث).
2
Understand the distinction
Perpetual non-existence cannot be an effect, but originated non-existence can be, like the cessation of an accident at a particular moment.
3
Note the challenge
The commentator asks for a proof that originated non-existence cannot be an effect, implying no such proof exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( And also it may perish by a contrary ) that arises in its substrate. ( Your statement that its occurrence ) in that substrate ( is conditioned by its perishing ) from it ( we say: If you require in the condition its priority over the conditioned, we deny ) that the occurrence of the arising contrary is conditioned by the perishing of the remaining contrary, since there is no proof for it except the impossibility of coexistence, and that does not indicate this conditionality. ( Otherwise ) i.e., if you do not require priority in the condition but are content with mere inseparability, ( then reciprocity is not impossible ), so it is possible that each is a condition for the other, and the resulting circularity is a circularity of simultaneity, ( just as the entry of each part of the ) ring ( rotating on itself ) into the space of the other part is conditioned by the exit of the other from it, and vice versa, and there is no harm in that because its reference is their mutual entailment. ( In summary ) i.e., whether we allow reciprocity in conditionality or not, ( they are ) i.e., the perishing of the remaining and the arising of the new ( simultaneous in ) time, and this simultaneity does not contradict causality, ( for causality is priority in intellect; thus its arising may be the cause of the perishing of the remaining ) while they are simultaneous in time, like cause and effect, ( for they are approximate in terms of time while the cause is prior in intellect ).

( And the judgment that the arising is not more entitled to remove the remaining than its opposite is false, because the arising is stronger due to its closeness to the cause and the remaining's distance from it. ( And also it may perish because the agent that did it does not do it ), not because it brings about its non-existence, and that does not require an effect from the agent, but rather mere refraining of the agent from sustaining what it did is sufficient for its perishing. ( And also we do not grant that non-existence cannot be an effect ) proceeding from the agent. ( Yes, that ) is granted ( regarding perpetual non-existence, but as for originated non-existence, it may be by the act of the agent ) like originated existence. ( And what is the proof for its impossibility? And also it may perish by the removal of a condition. Your statement that it is the substance ), for if it were an accident, there would be an infinite regress, and if that condition is the substance conditioned in its persistence by the accident, ( then there is circularity. We say: It is denied ), for there is no circularity and no infinite regress. ( And why should it not be that )
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage is a series of five refutations of the claim that an accident does not endure for two moments. The author addresses an opponent who argues that if an accident perishes by itself, it would not exist originally. The author first denies this, noting that its essence could bring about non-existence in a later moment, not immediately, so the requirement is conditional. Then, the opponent's own argument is turned against them: their reasoning about the second moment applies equally to them, and whatever answer they give, the author gives the same. Second, the accident may perish due to a contrary that arises in its substrate. The author argues against the condition that the contrary's occurrence requires the prior perishing of the accident; the only proof is the impossibility of coexistence, which does not imply that conditionality. If one does not require temporal priority, then reciprocity is possible, like the parts of a ring, where each part's entering is conditioned by the other's exiting, resulting in a circularity of simultaneity, which is harmless. They are simultaneous, and causality is only priority in intellect, not time. Third, the judgment that the arising contrary is not more entitled to remove the remaining is false because the arising is closer to the cause. Fourth, the accident may perish simply because the agent refrains from sustaining it, without needing an effect of non-existence. Fifth, originated non-existence can be an effect of the agent, just like originated existence, so the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect is denied for originated non-existence. Finally, the accident may perish by the removal of a condition; if the condition is the substance, there is no circularity or infinite regress, and it is possible.

Key Terms
عدم
أثر
فاعل
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence cannot be an effect in any sense.
Correction: The commentator distinguishes: perpetual non-existence cannot, but originated non-existence can be an effect of an agent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what kind of non-existence can be an effect of an agent?
A) Perpetual non-existence.
B) Originated non-existence.
C) Both perpetual and originated.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
Originated non-existence can be caused by an agent, just like originated existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience the end of something beloved—a relationship, a season of ease, a phase of life—do you truly see that cessation as an act of Allah's wisdom and power, or do you attribute it to mere chance or secondary causes? How might this lesson's truth about originated non-existence reshape your reliance on the Agent in every loss? Commit to acknowledging Allah's hand when things end, not just when they begin.
?
If originated non-existence can be an effect of the Agent, then your own death—the ultimate cessation—is not a mere biological accident but a direct act of the Creator. Are you living today with the awareness that both your existence and your non-existence are in His hands? What would change if you truly believed that your final breath is as much His creation as your first? Make one concrete choice today that reflects this accountability.

The Sixth Proof: Perishing by Removal of a Condition
Argument for cessation by condition removal, denying circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that an accident may perish because its condition (such as a substance) is removed, without circularity or infinite regress.

Core Idea
An accident can cease when its conditioning substance is removed, and this does not entail circularity or infinite regress.

The commentator presents the final argument: an accident may perish by the removal of a condition. If the opponent objects that the condition is a substance (which itself persists) and if the substance's persistence were conditioned by the accident, that would lead to circularity. The commentator denies both circularity and infinite regress, asserting that such conditional relations are possible without these problems.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
This argument is incomplete in our excerpt, but the main idea is presented. The accident perishes when a necessary condition (like the substrate substance) is removed.
2
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent says the condition is a substance, but if it were an accident, infinite regress would follow. Also, if the condition is a substance that itself depends on the accident for its persistence, that would be circular.
3
Note the commentator's denial
The commentator denies both circularity and infinite regress, implying that such dependencies are acceptable.

Commentary

Why the Agent's Refrainment Suffices for an Accident's Cessation
Defends the sufficiency of the agent's refrainment for an accident's cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the new occurrence is considered stronger than the remaining in removing an accident and why the agent's mere refrainment from preserving it is sufficient for its cessation, without needing a positive act of annihilation.

Core Idea
Inaction, not a positive act of annihilation, causes an accident to cease.

In kalam, accidents are held not to persist for two moments. This passage responds to an objection: why should the new occurrence be more effective than the remaining? The answer involves the new occurrence's proximity to the cause and the agent's refrainment. The commentator explains that the agent need not produce non-existence; merely refraining from preserving the accident suffices for its cessation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the rhetorical style and the key terms.
This passage is a direct response to an objection.
2
Understand the objection about priority
The objection claims that the new occurrence should not be more entitled to remove the remaining. The response argues the new occurrence is stronger due to closeness to the cause.
The commentator notes this argument is rhetorical.
3
Grasp the refrainment argument
The core point: the agent does not actively annihilate the accident; instead, ceasing to preserve it suffices. No separate effect of non-existence is needed.
This avoids requiring a positive act for destruction.
4
Distinguish free agent from necessitating agent
The commentator clarifies that for a free agent (al-mukhtār), refrainment means the will not to preserve; for a necessitating agent (al-mūjib), it means the absence of a condition for necessitation.
This shows the principle applies across different causal frameworks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحكم بأن الطارئ ليس أولى بإزالة الباقي من عكسه باطل لأن الطارئ أقوى لقربه من السبب وبعد الباقي عنه ( وأيضا فقد يزول لأن الفاعل الذي فعله لا يفعله لا لأنه يفعل عدمه وذلك لا يحتاج إلى أثر (للفاعل) صادر عنه بل مجرد امتناع الفاعل من إبقاء ما فعله كاف في زواله ) قوله : ( والحكم بأنَّ الطارئ إلخ ) جواب عن الوجه الثاني المذكور بقوله أو نقول لما كان من الطرفين إلخ ترك المصنف لظهوره. قوله : ( لقربه من السبب ) بناء على عدم تخلل زمان الحدوث بينه وبين السبب بخلاف الباقي وهذا أيضاً خطابي . قوله : ( لأن الفاعل الذي فعله في الزمان الأول والثاني لا يفعله أي في الزمان الثالث . قوله : (بل مجرد امتناع إلخ هذا في المختار ظاهر بأن لم تتعلق إرادته بإبقائه وأما في الموجب فبأن لم يتعلق إيجابه بإبقائه بانتفاء شرط من شروط إيجابه وفاعليته .
And the judgment that the new occurrence is not more entitled to remove the remaining than its opposite is false, because the new occurrence is stronger due to its closeness to the cause and the remaining's distance from it. (And also, it may cease because the agent who did it does not do it, not because he does its non-existence, and that does not need an effect (from the agent) proceeding from him, but rather the mere refrainment of the agent from preserving what he did is sufficient for its cessation.) His statement: (And the judgment that the new, etc.) is a response to the second aspect mentioned in his statement: 'or we say: since it was from both sides, etc.' The author omitted it for its obviousness. His statement: (due to its closeness to the cause) is based on the absence of an intervening time of origination between it and the cause, unlike the remaining; and this is also rhetorical. His statement: (because the agent who did it in the first and second time does not do it) i.e., in the third time. His statement: (But mere refrainment, etc.) This is clear in the case of the free agent: that his will is not attached to preserving it. As for the necessitating agent, it is that his necessitation is not attached to preserving it due to the absence of a condition among the conditions of his necessitation and his agency.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the argument that accidents do not persist for two moments. It addresses an objection: why should the new occurrence be more likely to remove the remaining? The response is that the new occurrence is closer to the cause, while the remaining is further. The commentator then explains that the agent's mere refrainment from preserving the accident is sufficient for its cessation; no positive act of annihilation is needed. He distinguishes between a free agent (al-mukhtār) and a necessitating agent (al-mūjib): for the free agent, the absence of will to preserve is enough; for the necessitating agent, the absence of the necessitation condition suffices. The objection that the new occurrence is not necessarily more effective is dismissed as rhetorical.

Temporal Non-Existence as an Effect of the Agent
Refutes the objection that non-existence cannot be an effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimūn defended the idea that temporal non-existence can be directly caused by an agent, distinguishing it from continuous non-existence.

Core Idea
Temporal non-existence is just as much an effect as temporal existence.

In this passage, the author refutes the claim that non-existence cannot be an effect by distinguishing between two types of non-existence. This distinction is crucial for the atomic theory of accidents, where both existence and non-existence of accidents are brought about by God's action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the objection and response.
Notice the distinction between two types of non-existence.
2
Identify the two types of non-existence
The passage distinguishes between continuous non-existence (al-ʿadam al-mustamirr) and temporal non-existence (al-ʿadam al-ḥādith). Understand the difference.
Continuous non-existence is absolute absence; temporal non-existence is the ceasing of an existent.
3
Understand why the distinction matters
Ask why someone would deny non-existence as an effect. The response shows that only continuous non-existence cannot be caused.
This directly addresses the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً لا نسلم أن العدم لا يصلح أن يكون ( أثراً ) صادراً عن الفاعل ( نعم ذلك ) مسلم ( في العدم المستمر وأما العدم الحادث فقد يكون بفعل الفاعل) كالوجود الحادث ( وما الدليل على امتناعه وأيضاً فقد يزول بزوال شرط قولك هو الجوهر ) إذ لو كان عرضاً تسلسل وإذا كان ذلك الشرط هو الجوهر المشروط في بقائه بالعرض ( فيدور قلنا : ممنوع ) إذا لا دور ولا تسلسل ( ولم لا يجوز أن يكون ) التضاد يزول ) يعني ذلك الشرط ( أعراضاً لا تبقى على التبادل إلى أن تنتهي إلى ما لا بدل عنه وعنده أنَّ الأعراض عندنا قسمان: قسم يجوز بقاؤه كالألوان وقسم لا يجوز بقاؤه كالحركات وحينئذ جاز أن يقال : شرط العرض الباقي عرض لا بعينه من أعراض من الأعراض التي لا تبقى بذاتها كدورات متعددة من الحركات مثلا فيكون كل واحد من تلك الأعراض المتعددة بدلاً الآخر فيستمر وجود ذلك العرض باستمرار شرطه ما دام يتبادل تلك الأعراض فإذا انتهت إلى ما لا بدل عنه كالدورة الأخيرة من تلك الدورات المعدودة فقد زال الشرط فيزول العرض الباقي بلا تسلسل وجاز أيضاً أن يقال : شرط العرض الباقي هو الجوهر وشرط الجوهر هو تلك الأعراض المتبادلة فلا يلزم دور وإنما اعتبر في الشرط تبادل الأعراض الغير القارة لأن الواحد من هذه الأعراض لا بقاء له فلا يبقى ما هو مشروط به هكذا ينبغي أن يضبط هذا الكلام
And also we do not concede that non-existence is not suitable to be an effect proceeding from the agent. (Yes, that) is conceded (in the case of continuous non-existence. But as for temporal non-existence, it may be by the action of the agent) like temporal existence. (And what is the proof of its impossibility? And also, it may cease by the cessation of a condition, your statement: it is the substance) for if it were an accident, there would be a regress; and if that condition is the substance which is conditional in its persistence upon an accident, (then there is circularity. We say: it is prevented) for there is no circularity and no regress. (And why should it not be) the contrary? (It ceases) meaning that condition (by accidents that do not persist in succession until they end at what has no substitute. And according to him, accidents with us are of two types: a type whose persistence is permissible like colors, and a type whose persistence is not permissible like motions. Then it is permissible to say: the condition of the persisting accident is an accident not specified from among the accidents that do not persist by themselves, like multiple cycles of motions for example, so each one of those multiple accidents is a substitute for the other, so the existence of that accident continues by the continuity of its condition as long as those accidents succeed each other. And when they end at what has no substitute, like the last cycle of those counted cycles, then the condition has ceased, so the persisting accident ceases without regress. And it is also permissible to say: the condition of the persisting accident is the substance, and the condition of the substance is those successive accidents, so no circularity occurs. Rather, the condition considers the succession of non-stationary accidents because the individual of these accidents has no persistence, so what is conditional upon it does not persist. Thus this discourse should be regulated.)
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections against the claim that accidents do not persist. First, the objector denies that non-existence can be an effect of the agent. The response distinguishes between continuous non-existence (al-ʿadam al-mustamirr) and temporal non-existence (al-ʿadam al-ḥādith); the latter can be caused just like temporal existence. Second, the objector argues that if an accident's persistence depends on a condition (sharṭ), that condition must be either a substance (jawhar) or an accident; if an accident, it leads to infinite regress; if a substance, then since the substance itself persists by an accident, there is circularity. The answer denies both: there is no regress because the condition can be a series of transient accidents (aʿrāḍ ghayr qārra) that succeed each other, ending at a final one without substitute. There is no circularity because the substance's persistence is conditioned by these successive accidents, while the accident's persistence is conditioned by the substance; since the accidents are not specified individuals but a generic type, there is no mutual dependence. The commentator notes that this is how the text should be understood.

Key Terms
العدم الحادث
العدم المستمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence can never be caused by an agent.
Correction: Temporal non-existence, which is the cessation of an existent, is a real event and can be caused. Only absolute, continuous non-existence is uncaused.

Checkpoint
Which type of non-existence is said to be impossible as an effect?
A) Temporal non-existence
B) Continuous non-existence
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Temporal non-existence is a valid effect of an agent, just like temporal existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the ceasing of a thing is a real event caused by God, then your own mortality is a real event He will bring about. Are you living today as if that event is real and coming?
?
When you witness something perish, do you see it as mere nothingness, or do you recognize the hand of the Creator who brings about both existence and non-existence? What one thing will you do today to remind yourself that nothing happens without His will?

Resolving the Condition Regress with Successive Accidents
Resolves two logical problems (regress and circularity) by positing successive transient accidents as conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the use of successive non-stationary accidents (aʿrāḍ ghayr qārra) avoids infinite regress and circularity in the persistence of accidents.

Core Idea
The condition for an accident's persistence can be a series of transient accidents, not a single persisting entity.

This passage addresses a second objection: if an accident persists due to a condition, that condition leads to regress or circularity. The ingenious solution involves a succession of non-stationary accidents that function as a single extended condition, ending without regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about condition
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Focus on the objection that the condition must be either a substance or an accident, leading to problems.
The objection claims regress (if accident) or circularity (if substance).
2
Identify the two problems: regress and circularity
If condition is an accident, regress. If condition is substance, circularity because substance persists by accident.
The text says 'if accident, regress; if substance, circularity'.
3
Understand the division of accidents
Accidents are divided into stationary (persisting) and non-stationary (not persisting). The solution uses non-stationary accidents like motions.
Stationary: colors; non-stationary: motions.
4
See how the solution works
The condition for a persisting accident is a series of non-stationary accidents that succeed each other. When the series ends, the condition ceases, avoiding regress. Circularity is avoided because the condition for the substance is a generic series, not a specific accident.
The series ends at a final one without substitute.

This passage is a refutation of two objections against the claim that accidents do not persist. First, the objector denies that non-existence can be an effect of the agent. Al-Nazzam's Extension to Bodies and the Contrary Objection
Al-Nazzam extends the argument to bodies; commentator addresses the contrary objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nazzam extended the argument from accidents to bodies and why the lack of a known contrary for substances does not invalidate the argument.

Core Idea
An argument can remain valid even when one of its branches falls away.

Al-Nazzam applied the same reasoning he used for the non-persistence of accidents to bodies. However, bodies do not have a known contrary like accidents do, which raises an objection. The commentator clarifies that this does not break the argument because only one branch of the disjunction drops out; the proof remains intact. In this lesson, we will unpack the extension and the commentator's response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English carefully to grasp the flow of the argument and the commentator's response.
Notice the logical structure: if bodies persisted, cessation would be impossible; but cessation is known, so they must not persist.
2
Identify the disjunction for cessation
The argument assumes that if bodies cease, it must be either by themselves, by another agent, or by the cessation of a condition. Understand each branch.
3
Understand the objection about the contrary
The objection is that bodies lack a known contrary, so one branch of the disjunction (the one relying on a contrary) may not apply. See why this is raised.
This is a specific objection from within the kalam tradition.
4
Reflect on the commentator's resolution
The commentator states that the fall of that branch does not affect the rest of the argument. The proof remains 'that argument' because the core logic is still sound.
This shows the robustness of the argumentative structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: طرد هذا بأدنى تغيير فقال : لو بقيت الأجسام لامتنع زوالها لكن زوالها معلوم بالإجماع وبشهادة الحس بيان الملازمة أنها لو زالت فإما بنفسها فيلزم امتناعها وبغيرها إما بوجودي موجب أو مختار فيلزم أن يكون العدم والنفي الصرف أثراً للفاعل وأيضاً لا فرق بين قولنا : لا أثر له وأثره لا وأما بزوال شرط فإنَّ كان جوهراً نقلنا الكلام في زواله فيكون بزوال جوهر قوله : ( واعلم أن النظام طرد إلخ) عدم ثبوت الضد للجوهر باعتبار اشتراط الموضوع فيه دون الاكتفاء بالمحل المطلق لا ينافي هذا الطرد إذ غايته سقوط هذا الشق من الترديد المذكور وبهذا القدر لا يخرج الدليل من كونه ذلك الدليل .
His statement: He extended this with minimal change and said: If bodies persisted, their cessation would be impossible; but their cessation is known by consensus and sensory evidence. The explanation of the entailment: if they cease, then either by themselves—then their impossibility follows—or by another, either a necessitating or free agent—then it would follow that non-existence and pure negation are an effect of the agent. Also, there is no difference between our saying: 'he has no effect' and 'his effect is not.' Alternatively, by the cessation of a condition: if [the condition] is a substance, we transfer the discussion to its cessation, so it would be by the cessation of a substance. His statement: (And know that al-Nazzam extended, etc.) The non-establishment of a contrary for the substance, considering the condition of the subject in it without being satisfied with the absolute locus, does not contradict this extension, for its utmost is the fall of this branch of the mentioned disjunction, and to this extent the argument does not cease to be that argument.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage reports al-Nazzam's extension of the argument against the persistence of accidents to bodies. The original argument assumed a known contrary (ḍidd) for accidents, but bodies may lack a specific contrary. The commentator notes that this does not invalidate the extension because the structure of the argument remains: if bodies persisted, they could not cease; but they do cease, so they must not persist. The objector might say that a body's cessation could be due to the cessation of its condition (a substance), but then the discussion transfers to that condition. The fact that a substance does not have a known contrary (like black and white for color) does not undermine the argument; it only means that one branch of the disjunction (the contrary argument) drops out, but the overall proof still stands.

الأجسام
الضد
الموضوع
المحل
الشرط

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Nazzam's argument fails because bodies have no known contrary.
Correction: The commentator explains that the lack of a known contrary only removes one branch of the disjunction; the argument remains valid because the other branches still hold.

Key Takeaway
