Objection and Two Responses
The commentator presents the objection and two responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that bodies are a counterexample to the proof of non-persistence of accidents is answered through two responses.

Core Idea
Bodies can cease just like accidents, so they do not break the proof.

The commentator presents a potential counterexample: if the proof that accidents do not persist is applied to bodies, it fails because many consider bodies to persist. However, the response shows that bodies can also cease due to accidents, so they are not a counterexample. Two specific responses are given, one involving an accident of annihilation and another involving the withholding of sustaining accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the beginning of the passage where the commentator states that applying the proof to bodies makes them a counterexample for those who affirm the persistence of bodies. Note the reasoning.
The objection is based on the apparent persistence of bodies in contrast to accidents.
2
First response: annihilation accident
The first response claims that bodies can cease due to an accident that subsists in them, like annihilation according to the Mu'tazila. Understand the sub-debate about whether annihilation is created in a locus.
The commentator gives a better interpretation: likening the accident to annihilation in being contrary to persistence.
3
Second response: withholding accidents
The second response is simpler: the substance may cease because Allah does not create the accidents necessary for its persistence.
This is a direct answer without invoking a specific accident.

بلا
His doctrine, and what we mentioned is supported by his statement: (And from it, i.e., from applying this proof to bodies, it is known that bodies are brought as a counterexample against it) i.e., against this proof according to the one who affirms the persistence of bodies. (And it may be answered) i.e., concerning this counterexample (that it, meaning the body, indeed the substance absolutely, may cease to exist due to an accident that subsists in it) i.e., by Allah Most High creating an accident contrary to persistence, so that accident subsists in the substance and it ceases (like annihilation (al-fana') according to the Mu'tazila) for it is with them an accident which, when Allah creates it, all substances perish. If it is said: The well-known view of the Basran Mu'tazila is that annihilation is an accident contrary to persistence, created by Allah not in a locus, so by it substances perish and it does not subsist in the perishing thing as you claimed; it is answered: It is possible that He creates it first in a locus, then it attaches to a locus Allah wills to annihilate. And it is better to say: The intent is to liken that accident to annihilation according to their doctrine merely in being contrary to persistence, even if they differ in that one of them subsists in a locus and the other does not. (Or) that the substance may cease to exist due to an accident that Allah does not create in it, according to us. He intends: His statement: (It is only needed when bodies are etc.) And what was said: The indivisible atom according to him is composed of accidents, so there is no difference between the two statements in not needing the application, is an error, for al-Nazzam does not affirm the indivisible atom, let alone its being composed of accidents, for composition is incompatible with indivisibility. He said in Sharh al-Tajrid: Since he stated that in a body there are actually infinite parts, he is forced to affirm the part; because if every possible division in a body is actually present in it, then what is not present in the body is impossible to occur in it, so its parts are indivisible. So he fell into what he was fleeing from without acknowledging it. So the meaning of his statement: composed of individual substances is that it is composed of parts which are individual substances in reality, not that he acknowledges it. His statement: (And what we mentioned is supported etc.) For the one who affirms the persistence of bodies only affirms that they are composed of the indivisible atom. His statement: (An accident which, when Allah creates it etc.) either multiple, as Abu Ali said: He Most High creates for each substance an annihilation, or not multiple, as others said: a single annihilation suffices to annihilate all bodies. His statement: (And it is better etc.) because what he mentioned first is merely a rational possibility not transmitted from them, and besides on the view of its non-multiplicity, a single accident subsisting in many loci would be necessary. His statement: (That it be said: the intent etc.) Then his statement 'according to the Mu'tazila' is attached to his statement 'like annihilation', not to the statement 'it may cease' in the first aspect. The sum, and he did not make the basis of negating the mentioned need that the renewal of the part necessitates the renewal of the whole, even if the whole is conceded to be substantial, as the expression of necessity suggests; because in it there is a hint of specifying the way in proving the renewal of the body, so it is not a valid statement. So contemplate his statement: (It is answered: it is possible etc.) This is the intelligible, for if it were always not in a locus, its relation to all substances would be uniform, so the perishing of some by it without others would be a preference without a preferrer. But what is understood from the context of speech is that it is a bare possibility not transmitted from the Mu'tazila, so he turned away from it and said: And it is better etc. Blank.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the objection that bodies are a counterexample to the proof that accidents do not persist for two moments. The commentator first identifies the objection: by applying the proof to bodies, they become a counterexample against it according to those who affirm the persistence of bodies. Then he presents two responses. The first response: a body, or substance generally, may cease to exist because of an accident that arises in it—Allah creates an accident contrary to persistence, and that accident subsists in the substance, causing it to perish. The example given is annihilation (al-fana') according to the Mu'tazila. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: the Basran Mu'tazila hold that annihilation is an accident created not in a locus, so it does not subsist in the perishing thing. The answer is that it is possible that Allah first creates it in a locus, then it attaches to the locus He wills to annihilate. But the commentator considers it better to say that the intention is to liken that accident to annihilation merely in being contrary to persistence, even though one subsists in a locus and the other does not. The second response: the substance may cease to exist due to an accident that Allah does not create in it, meaning He does not create the necessary accidents for its continuation. The passage then turns to a digression about when the application (طرد) of the proof is needed. The commentator explains that it is needed only when bodies are composed of atomic parts. He refutes the claim that al-Nazzam (who denies the indivisible atom) actually holds that the atom is composed of accidents, pointing out that al-Nazzam does not affirm the atom at all. The commentator then explains various textual points, including the multiplicity or unity of annihilation, and the rationale for preferring the second answer over the first.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof from non-persistence of accidents is refuted by the persistence of bodies.
Correction: Bodies can also perish via accidents, so they are not a counterexample.

Checkpoint
What is the first response to the objection that bodies are a counterexample to the proof?
A) Bodies persist forever and cannot perish.
B) Substances may cease due to an accident that arises in them.
C) The proof is invalid.
D) Bodies are not composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The objection of bodies is answered by showing that bodies can cease due to accidents, either by creation of an annihilation accident or by withholding accidents of persistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that bodies, including your own, can cease due to an accident or a withholding of persistence. Are you living as if your physical existence is guaranteed? What would change if you truly accepted that your next breath depends entirely on Allah's direct action?
?
The Mu'tazila used annihilation as an accident, but the Ash'aris respond with a different explanation. Do you see the differences as mere intellectual debates, or do they affect how you rely on Allah? How does your understanding of 'no persistence without a preserver' impact your daily trust in Him?
?
Everything you own, every relationship, every ability is subject to the same principle: it can vanish by Allah's will. What attachment do you hold that feels permanent but, in reality, is just an accident waiting to be removed? Will you detach from it now, or wait until it is taken?

Clarification on Application and Refutation of al-Nazzam
The commentator clarifies conditions for the proof's application and refutes a rival theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when the application of the proof (طرد) is needed and why al-Nazzam's position on the indivisible atom does not undermine it.

Core Idea
The proof is only needed when bodies are composed of atomic parts, and al-Nazzam's view does not challenge it.

The commentator continues by discussing a crucial point: the proof's application is only necessary when bodies are composed of the indivisible atom. He then refutes a claim that al-Nazzam holds the atom to be composed of accidents, showing that al-Nazzam does not affirm the atom at all. Subsequent comments address the multiplicity of annihilation and the rationale for preferring one response over another.

Guided Path
1
Understand when the proof is needed
The commentator explains that the application of the proof (طرد) is only needed when bodies are composed of atomic parts. If bodies are not atomic, the proof may not apply.
This sets the stage for the refutation of al-Nazzam.
2
Refutation of al-Nazzam's alleged view
The commentator refutes the claim that al-Nazzam believes the indivisible atom is composed of accidents. He points out that al-Nazzam does not affirm the atom at all; rather, he holds that bodies have infinite actual parts.
Thus, the claim that his view avoids the need for the proof is false.
3
Final comments on annihilation and preference
The commentator discusses whether annihilation is multiple or single, and why the second response (withholding accidents) is preferable.
This ties back to the earlier responses.

He said in Sharh al-Tajrid: Since he stated that in a body there are actually infinite parts, he is forced to affirm the part; because if every possible division in a body is actually present in it, then what is not present in the body is impossible to occur in it, so its parts are indivisible. So he fell into what he was fleeing from without acknowledging it. So the meaning of his statement: composed of individual substances is that it is composed of parts which are individual substances in reality, not that he acknowledges it. The Two Ways of Perishing and the Answer to the Rebuttal
Explanation of the two ways and answer to the rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite and Ash'arite views on how substances perish, and how the answer to the rebuttal forces the opponent to retreat to a key premise.

Core Idea
If you allow two ways for a substance to perish, you must allow them for accidents too.

In this passage, the commentator explains the two theological paths for the perishing of substances: the Mu'tazilite way (by an accident) and the Ash'arite way (by non-creation of necessary accidents). He then provides the answer to a rebuttal that threatens to undo the proof against the persistence of accidents. This answer turns the tables on the opponent, showing that unless they retreat to a specific premise, their argument fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the two ways described and the logical 'if...then' structure of the rebuttal.
2
Identify the two ways of perishing
Find the Mu'tazilite way (perishing by an accident inhering in the substance) and the Ash'arite way (perishing by Allah not creating necessary accidents). Understand the difference.
3
Follow the answer to the rebuttal
The commentator says: if you allow these two ways for substances, you must allow them for accidents too. This undermines the original proof that accidents persist forever. Notice the conditional logic.
4
Understand the escape clause
The only way to save the proof is to insist that an accident cannot subsist in an accident, so the two ways cannot apply to accidents. This is the 'return' mentioned.
This premise is crucial for the proof to hold.

That what was mentioned first is the way of the perishing of substances according to the Mu'tazila, and we have another way for their perishing: that Allah does not create the accidents from which substances cannot be free, so they perish necessarily. (And the answer) to the answer of the counterexample is to say: If you allow concerning the perishing of the permanent substance that which you mentioned—that an accident contrary to its persistence subsists in it, or that Allah does not create in it an accident without which it cannot persist—then let the like be allowed concerning the perishing of the permanent accident, so the proof is not established regarding the original claim either, unless you, or the arguer, return to the fact that an accident does not subsist in an accident, so its perishing by one of the two ways cannot be conceived. His statement: (For an accident He does not create etc.) is an indication that his earlier statement 'He creates it' is conjoined to his statement 'subsists in it', and therein [is] that its perishing is by not creating the accident in it, not by an accident He does not create, unless the perspective is considered, i.e., from the perspective that He does not create it. His statement: (According to the Mu'tazila where they said: annihilation is an accident). His statement: (The substance cannot be free from them, like the accidents of location). His statement: (He intends that what was mentioned first is the way of the perishing of the substance according to the Mu'tazila.) The reason for the exclusivity of the first way to the Mu'tazila is apparent according to the first direction, because annihilation with us is not an accident but rather a non-existent matter, i.e., the non-existence of persistence. How? And it is among the repeated types, for if it existed, it would be described by annihilation, otherwise its locus would also persist. And it has been established that repeated types are non-existent matters. As for the second direction which he presented and considered better, there is obscurity in it, because the well-known view of the Mu'tazila is that they affirm annihilation as an accident created by Allah Most High not in a locus, then by it He annihilates substances, and according to some of them annihilation subsists in the perishing thing, mentioned in the Prophecies of Sharh al-Maqasid. As for them affirming another accident similar to annihilation and different from it in what he mentioned, it is not transmitted from them. And if it is interpreted as a rational possibility, then our not allowing it is not apparent in its aspect, unless it is said: Since the Mu'tazila allowed annihilation in the way they mentioned, it is closer that allowing its like and the statement that substances perish by this way also is specific to them. As for the reason for the exclusivity of the second way to us, it seems to be based on the fact that the Mu'tazila's doctrine is that the way of perishing of substances is only the lack of an accident that subsists in it, but this is not free from defect unless it is established that the Mu'tazila allow the substance to be free from all accidents unlike the Ash'aris. For if the mentioned allowing is not established, then the lack of some accident among the accidents from which substances cannot be free would be a cause for the perishing of the substance, so they would not specify the way of perishing by the lack of annihilation. His statement: (Except that you return to the fact that an accident does not subsist in an accident) if the subsistence of annihilation in the perishing thing is stipulated in the first moment or the second, then the aspect of this return is apparent; but if it is not stipulated at all, as is well-known from the Mu'tazila, then it is not [apparent] .

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that presents the answer to the rebuttal (jawaab al-naqd) and then explains the two paths (the Mu'tazilite way and the Ash'arite way) regarding the perishing of substances. The commentator begins by stating that what was mentioned earlier (in the previous passage) is the way of perishing of substances according to the Mu'tazila, and that the Ash'aris have another way: Allah does not create the accidents from which substances cannot be free, so they necessarily perish. Then the commentator gives the answer to the rebuttal: If you (the opponent) allow for the perishing of a permanent substance either by an accident contrary to its persistence or by the non-creation of a necessary accident, then you must also allow the same for a permanent accident. Hence the original proof that accidents do not persist fails, unless the arguer retreats to the claim that an accident does not subsist in an accident, so its perishing by either of these two ways cannot be conceived. The remainder of the passage is a detailed commentary on the text, explaining that the first way (perishing by an accident) is specific to the Mu'tazila, and the second way (perishing by non-creation of necessary accidents) is specific to the Ash'aris. The commentator discusses the views of al-Ka'bi, who also held that perishing occurs by non-creation of persistence, showing that the second way is not exclusively Ash'ari. He then elaborates on the Mu'tazilite view of annihilation, whether it is an accident created in a locus or not, and the implications for the exclusivity of the two ways. The passage concludes with a note on the condition of the return: if the subsistence of annihilation in the perishing thing is stipulated, the return to the claim that an accident does not subsist in an accident is clear; otherwise, it is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your persistence from moment to moment depends entirely on Allah's continuous creation of the accidents that sustain you. Do you live with that awareness, or do you take your continued existence for granted? What would change in your day if you truly felt that without His constant act, you would cease to exist?
?

?

Sectarian Specificity of the Two Ways
Commentary on the two ways and their sectarian specificity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite way of perishing is considered specific to them, and why the Ash'arite way is not exclusively Ash'ari, including the view of al-Ka'bi.

Core Idea
The two ways are not mutually exclusive; the second is shared with some Mu'tazilites.

The commentator now delves into the details of why the first way (perishing by an accident) is attributed to the Mu'tazila and the second way (perishing by non-creation) to the Ash'aris. He notes that al-Ka'bi, a Mu'tazilite, also held the second way, so it is not exclusive. He also discusses the Mu'tazilite conception of annihilation as an accident and the condition for the 'return' to the premise that an accident does not subsist in an accident.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed commentary
Read the passage that follows the main argument. It explains the text phrase by phrase, clarifying the sectarian attributions.
Focus on the commentator's reasoning for exclusivity.
2
Understand why the first way is Mu'tazilite
The commentator argues that for the Ash'aris, annihilation is not an accident but a non-existence, so the first way (accident causing perishing) is incompatible with their ontology.
He mentions two possible interpretations (tawjih) and discusses their implications.
3
Note that the second way is not exclusively Ash'ari
Al-Ka'bi also held that substances perish by Allah not creating persistence. So the second way is shared.
This challenges oversimplified sectarian dichotomies.
4
Consider the condition for the 'return'
The commentator ends by discussing the condition for the return to the premise that an accident does not subsist in an accident: it depends on whether the Mu'tazila stipulate that annihilation subsists in the perishing thing.
If they do, the return is clear; if not, it is not apparent.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that presents the answer to the rebuttal (jawaab al-naqd) and then explains the two paths (the Mu'tazilite way and the Ash'arite way) regarding the perishing of substances. The commentator begins by stating that what was mentioned earlier (in the previous passage) is the way of perishing of substances according to the Mu'tazila, and that the Ash'aris have another way: Allah does not create the accidents from which substances cannot be free, so they necessarily perish. Then the commentator gives the answer to the rebuttal: If you (the opponent) allow for the perishing of a permanent substance either by an accident contrary to its persistence or by the non-creation of a necessary accident, then you must also allow the same for a permanent accident. Hence the original proof that accidents do not persist fails, unless the arguer retreats to the claim that an accident does not subsist in an accident, so its perishing by either of these two ways cannot be conceived. The remainder of the passage is a detailed commentary on the text, explaining that the first way (perishing by an accident) is specific to the Mu'tazila, and the second way (perishing by non-creation of necessary accidents) is specific to the Ash'aris. The commentator discusses the views of al-Ka'bi, who also held that perishing occurs by non-creation of persistence, showing that the second way is not exclusively Ash'ari. He then elaborates on the Mu'tazilite view of annihilation, whether it is an accident created in a locus or not, and the implications for the exclusivity of the two ways. The passage concludes with a note on the condition of the return: if the subsistence of annihilation in the perishing thing is stipulated, the return to the claim that an accident does not subsist in an accident is clear; otherwise, it is not.

Causality and Created Things

The Argument from Re-creation and Its Initial Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the re-creation argument and refutes it by denying re-creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that re-creation of accidents implies the possibility of their endurance, and the initial refutation that denies the possibility of re-creation.

Core Idea
Re-creation of accidents is denied, so the a fortiori argument for endurance collapses.

This lesson presents the third method used by proponents of accidents' endurance: the argument from re-creation. If an accident can be brought back into existence after ceasing, then a continuous existence without interruption should be even more possible. However, the master (al-Shaykh) rejects the very premise that accidents can be re-created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument and refutation. Then read the translation.
Notice the use of parentheses to indicate the structure of the argument.
2
Identify the a fortiori reasoning
The proponents argue: if an accident can exist in two moments with non-existence between them, then existing continuously (without such intermission) is more entitled to permissibility. Find this in the passage.
The argument uses the principle of 'الاولى' (priority).
3
Note the refutation: denial of re-creation
The response simply states that al-Shaykh denied the possibility of re-creating accidents. This is a direct denial of the premise.
Consider why this denial is sufficient: if re-creation is impossible, the a fortiori argument has no basis.
4
Reflect on why this denial is not problematic
The text says 'there is no harm upon him in that'—the denial does not contradict any necessary belief, such as bodily resurrection, because accidents are not re-created in that process.
This point is elaborated in the glosses but is implicit here.

(The third: the accident may be re-created) and it—i.e., its re-creation by interpretation that it is re-created—its existence in the second moment, which is after the time of its non-existence, which is immediately after the time of its existence. And if the existence of an accident in two moments is allowed with the interposition of non-existence between them in a time intermediate between the two moments, then without it—i.e., its existence without the interposition of non-existence, rather in the manner of continuity—is more entitled to permissibility, so the endurance of accidents is not impossible. We say: the master (al-Shaykh) denied the re-creation of an accident and there is no harm upon him in that. And even if it be granted that re-creation is permissible, it is a comparison without a commonality—i.e., your comparison of its existence in two moments without the interposition of non-existence to its existence in them without that is a comparison with no commonality. And the claim of priority—i.e., the priority of existence without the interposition of non-existence in permissibility—is a claim without evidence, because it is possible that the interposition of non-existence is a condition for existence in the second moment, so re-creation is possible without endurance. Rather, we say: that—i.e., existence in two moments with the interposition of non-existence—is permissible according to us, and this—i.e., existence in them without its interposition—is impossible, so it is not valid to compare the second to the first in permissibility at all. And it may be said: just as the judgment of the endurance of bodies is necessary, the intellect judges it with the aid of the sense... His saying: (as is well-known) is an indication that if death were interpreted as absence of life from that which was qualified by life, the judgment of death would be valid, but that is contrary to the well-known. His saying: (by interpretation) the interpretation is for the purpose of the masculine pronoun; it is not needed, because it is for the masculine predicate, and because the infinitive that is not distinguished from its masculine by the ta' marbuta may be treated as masculine or feminine, even if by means of attributing existence to it the difficulty remains because of the lack of correctness of attributing existence to 'that it is re-created' in reality, and the way of leniency is wide. His saying: (and there is no harm upon him because it does not affect the resurrection of bodies, since accidents are not re-created at all; the dispute is only about whether it is with all the original parts and their re-creation after annihilation. His saying: (by interpretation that it is re-created) the need for interpretation is both verbal and conceptual: the verbal is the masculinization of the pronoun, and the conceptual is that re-creation is bringing into existence, not existence. His saying: (and there is no harm upon him in that) i.e., according to the law, for the aforementioned denial does not contradict the statement of bodies, because it is possible that bodies are re-created with the succession of similars of accidents that cannot be devoid of substances upon those bodies. His saying: (and this—i.e., existence in them without its interposition—is impossible because then would follow the subsistence of the accident, i.e., endurance, by accident, and its invalidity has already passed; and the interposition of non-existence between the two moments of the existence of the accident does not entail this problem. This is the difference between permitting the re-creation of the accident with the interposition of non-existence and not permitting existence in two moments without that interposition, although the weakness of this difference has been mentioned by denying that endurance is an accident.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the endurance of accidents: re-creation (إعادة). The proponents argue: if an accident can exist in two moments with non-existence intervening (i.e., be re-created after ceasing to exist), then its existence in two moments without such non-existence (i.e., continuous endurance) is a fortiori possible. Therefore, endurance is not impossible. The refutation begins by noting that the master (al-Shaykh, i.e., Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or similar authority) denied the possibility of re-creating accidents, and there is no harm in that denial. Even if re-creation is granted, the argument is a comparison (قياس) without a commonality (جامع): comparing existence with continuous duration to existence with intermittent non-existence is invalid because the two cases are not analogous. The claim of priority (أولوية) is baseless; perhaps non-existence is a necessary condition for existence in the second moment, so re-creation is possible but continuous endurance is not. In fact, the author asserts that existence with intervening non-existence is permissible, while existence without it is impossible; thus the analogy fails. The subsequent glosses explain nuances: the interpretation regarding the pronoun is to reconcile grammatical gender; the denial of accident re-creation does not conflict with bodily resurrection because bodies can be re-created along with a succession of similar accidents. The final gloss clarifies the underlying metaphysical difference: continuous existence would imply the subsistence of the accident (البقاء) as an accident, which is invalid, whereas intermittent existence avoids this. Thus the third argument is refuted.

Key Terms
إعادة (re-creation)
عدم (non-existence)
أولوية (priority)
عرض (accident)
بقاء (endurance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If re-creation of an accident is possible, then endurance must be more possible.
Correction: The refutation denies the possibility of re-creation itself, so the premise is rejected.

Checkpoint
What is the initial refutation given against the re-creation argument?
A) The master denied that re-creation is possible.
B) The comparison between re-creation and endurance is invalid.
C) The priority claim is baseless.
D) The endurance of bodies is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The argument from re-creation fails because its premise—that accidents can be re-created—is rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief because it seemed logically necessary, only to realize that a key premise you assumed was not actually certain? Are you willing to examine the premises of your own faith to ensure they are built on truth, not just convenience? Commit this week to identify one belief whose foundation you have not questioned.
?
The master denied the re-creation of accidents to protect a deeper truth. Is there a plausible idea you have accepted without considering whether it undermines a core principle of your deen? What would it cost you to reject that idea if it conflicts with revelation? Pray for the courage to prioritize divine truth over intellectual appeal.

Refutation of Re-creation Argument: Comparison Without Commonality
Al-Jurjani refutes the a fortiori argument by showing the comparison lacks commonality and the priority claim is undemonstrated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why even granting re-creation, the a fortiori argument fails due to lack of commonality between the two cases and the baseless claim of priority.

Core Idea
Even if re-creation is allowed, continuous endurance is not more permissible; the analogy is invalid.

In this lesson, we explore the deeper refutation of the re-creation argument. Even if we grant that an accident can be re-created after non-existence, the comparison between re-creation (with interruption) and endurance (without interruption) is invalid because the conditions differ. The claim that endurance is 'more entitled' to permissibility is baseless, and in fact, continuous endurance is impossible due to the problem of subsisting accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage from 'وإن سلم' to the end, along with the literal translation. Notice the structure of the refutation.
This passage includes glosses that clarify terms and the metaphysical basis.
2
Understand the critique of the comparison
The refutation states that even if re-creation is allowed, the analogy (قياس) between existence with and without non-existence is without commonality (جامع). Identify why the two cases are not analogous.
The presence of non-existence may be a necessary condition for existence in the second moment.
3
Examine why the priority claim is unsupported
The proponents claim that existence without interruption is 'more entitled' (أولى) to permissibility. The refutation calls this a claim without evidence, because it is possible that non-existence is a condition for the second existence.
This blocks the a fortiori reasoning.
4
Note the conclusion and metaphysical reason
The refutation concludes that re-creation (with non-existence) is permissible, but continuous existence (without non-existence) is impossible. The reason given is that continuous existence would imply the subsistence of the accident as an accident, which is invalid.
This is explained in the gloss: 'قيام العرض بالعرض' is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

:قوله : ( كما هو المشهور إشارة إلى أنه لو فسر الموت بعدم الحياة عن مثل ما اتصف بالحياة يصح الحكم بالموت لكنه خلاف المشهور. قوله : ( بتأويل) إن التأويل لأجل تذكير الضمير فلا حاجة إليه ؛ لأنه لتذكير الخبر، ولأنَّ المصدر الذي لا يفرق بينه وبين مذكره بالتاء يجوز فيه التذكير والتأنيث وإن كان بواسطة حمل الوجود عليه فالإشكال باق لعدم صحة حمل الوجود على أن يعاد أيضاً حقيقة وطريق التسامح

قوله : ( وهذا أي الوجود فيهما بلا تخلله ممتنع لأنه يلزم حينئذ قيام العرض، أعني البقاء ) بالعرض وقد مر بطلانه وأما أن تخلل العدم بين زماني وجود العرض فلا يلزم هذا المحذور هذا هو الفارق بين تجويز إعادة العرض مع تخلل العدم وعدم تجويز وجود في زمانين بدون ذلك التخلل وإن مر تضعيف هذا الفارق بمنع كون البقاء عرضاً .
(The third: the accident may be re-created) and it—i.e., its re-creation by interpretation that it is re-created—its existence in the second moment, which is after the time of its non-existence, which is immediately after the time of its existence. And if the existence of an accident in two moments is allowed with the interposition of non-existence between them in a time intermediate between the two moments, then without it—i.e., its existence without the interposition of non-existence, rather in the manner of continuity—is more entitled to permissibility, so the endurance of accidents is not impossible. We say: the master (al-Shaykh) denied the re-creation of an accident and there is no harm upon him in that. And even if it be granted that re-creation is permissible, it is a comparison without a commonality—i.e., your comparison of its existence in two moments without the interposition of non-existence to its existence in them without that is a comparison with no commonality. And the claim of priority—i.e., the priority of existence without the interposition of non-existence in permissibility—is a claim without evidence, because it is possible that the interposition of non-existence is a condition for existence in the second moment, so re-creation is possible without endurance. Rather, we say: that—i.e., existence in two moments with the interposition of non-existence—is permissible according to us, and this—i.e., existence in them without its interposition—is impossible, so it is not valid to compare the second to the first in permissibility at all. And it may be said: just as the judgment of the endurance of bodies is necessary, the intellect judges it with the aid of the sense... His saying: (as is well-known) is an indication that if death were interpreted as absence of life from that which was qualified by life, the judgment of death would be valid, but that is contrary to the well-known. His saying: (by interpretation) the interpretation is for the purpose of the masculine pronoun; it is not needed, because it is for the masculine predicate, and because the infinitive that is not distinguished from its masculine by the ta' marbuta may be treated as masculine or feminine, even if by means of attributing existence to it the difficulty remains because of the lack of correctness of attributing existence to 'that it is re-created' in reality, and the way of leniency is wide. His saying: (and there is no harm upon him because it does not affect the resurrection of bodies, since accidents are not re-created at all; the dispute is only about whether it is with all the original parts and their re-creation after annihilation. His saying: (by interpretation that it is re-created) the need for interpretation is both verbal and conceptual: the verbal is the masculinization of the pronoun, and the conceptual is that re-creation is bringing into existence, not existence. His saying: (and there is no harm upon him in that) i.e., according to the law, for the aforementioned denial does not contradict the statement of bodies, because it is possible that bodies are re-created with the succession of similars of accidents that cannot be devoid of substances upon those bodies. His saying: (and this—i.e., existence in them without its interposition—is impossible because then would follow the subsistence of the accident, i.e., endurance, by accident, and its invalidity has already passed; and the interposition of non-existence between the two moments of the existence of the accident does not entail this problem. This is the difference between permitting the re-creation of the accident with the interposition of non-existence and not permitting existence in two moments without that interposition, although the weakness of this difference has been mentioned by denying that endurance is an accident.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument for the endurance of accidents: re-creation (إعادة). The proponents argue: if an accident can exist in two moments with non-existence intervening (i.e., be re-created after ceasing to exist), then its existence in two moments without such non-existence (i.e., continuous endurance) is a fortiori possible. Therefore, endurance is not impossible. The refutation begins by noting that the master (al-Shaykh, i.e., Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or similar authority) denied the possibility of re-creating accidents, and there is no harm in that denial. Even if re-creation is granted, the argument is a comparison (قياس) without a commonality (جامع): comparing existence with continuous duration to existence with intermittent non-existence is invalid because the two cases are not analogous. The claim of priority (أولوية) is baseless; perhaps non-existence is a necessary condition for existence in the second moment, so re-creation is possible but continuous endurance is not. In fact, the author asserts that existence with intervening non-existence is permissible, while existence without it is impossible; thus the analogy fails. The subsequent glosses explain nuances: the interpretation regarding the pronoun is to reconcile grammatical gender; the denial of accident re-creation does not conflict with bodily resurrection because bodies can be re-created along with a succession of similar accidents. The final gloss clarifies the underlying metaphysical difference: continuous existence would imply the subsistence of the accident (البقاء) as an accident, which is invalid, whereas intermittent existence avoids this. Thus the third argument is refuted.

Key Terms
قياس بلا جامع (comparison without commonality)
أولوية (priority)
شرط (condition)
دليل (evidence)
بقاء (endurance)
قيام العرض بالعرض (subsistence of accident by accident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If re-creation is possible, then endurance must also be possible because it is a simpler case.
Correction: The two cases are not analogous; non-existence may be a necessary condition for re-creation, so endurance cannot be inferred.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for why the comparison between re-creation and endurance is invalid?
A) Re-creation is impossible, so the comparison is moot.
B) The comparison lacks a commonality; non-existence may be a necessary condition.
C) Endurance is actually more difficult to achieve.
D) The argument only applies to substances.

Key Takeaway
The re-creation argument fails because the analogy between re-creation and endurance is invalid; non-existence may be a necessary condition for re-creation, making endurance not analogous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The refutation shows that what seems more 'entitled' to existence may actually be impossible. Is there an assumption in your life about what you 'deserve' or what should happen that you have never examined? Will you let this lesson push you to test that assumption against revealed truth, even if it feels uncomfortable?
?
The disputants argued about the re-creation of bodies and accidents. Do you live as if your own existence depends on Allah's continuous will, or do you subconsciously assume your life is self-sustaining? How would your daily reliance on Him change if you truly believed that your existence at each moment is a fresh act of creation?
?
The passage emphasizes that a claim without evidence (دعوى بلا دليل) is baseless. In your faith, have you ever held a belief about Allah's actions or attributes that you cannot support from revelation or sound reason? What would it mean for you to honestly seek evidence for that belief, or to abandon it if none is found?

Refutation of a Single Relation in Two Loci
Al-Jurjani refutes the ancient philosophers' argument for a single relation in two relata.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani refutes the ancient philosophers' claim that a single relation must subsist in two relata, and how the distinction between specific and personal unity resolves the issue.

Core Idea
Two distinct relations can connect two things without being one.

Al-Jurjani here tackles a subtle point in metaphysics: how do relations bind two things? The ancient philosophers argued that to connect two relata, a single relation must exist in both. Jurjani counters that two relations, each personally distinct but sharing the same specific nature, are enough. He illustrates with examples like proximity and paternity to clarify his reasoning and exposes a further absurdity in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' position
Start by reading the passage to understand what the ancient philosophers claimed: that a single relation must subsist in both relata to avoid their disconnection.
Note their key reasoning: if each relatum had its own relation, they would be cut off.
2
Identify Jurjani's counter-argument
Focus on where Jurjani says "the truth is that they are two likes" — this is his core refutation. He argues the two relations are personally distinct, even if they share the same specific reality.
The distinction between personal unity (being one individual) and specific unity (being of the same kind) is crucial.
3
Follow the illustration and the further objection
See how he uses paternity and sonship as an example, then consider his objection that if a single relation could subsist in two loci, it would also have to subsist in more than two, which is absurd.
This shows the philosophers must provide a distinction to avoid the consequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الجزم اليقيني حاصلاً بدونه ولم نجد له مخالفاً إلا أن قدماء الفلاسفة القائلين بوجود الإضافات جوزوا قيام نحو الجوار والقرب والأخوة وغيرها من الإضافات المتشابهة بالطرفين قالوا : المضافان إن قام بكل منهما إضافة على حده كان كل واحد منهما منقطعاً عن الآخر فلا بد أن يقوم بهما إضافة واحدة لتربط بينهما والحق أنهما مثلان فقرب هذا من ذاك مخالف بالشخص لقرب ذاك من هذا وإن شاركه في الحقيقة النوعية وهذه المشاركة أعني الوحدة النوعية كافية في الربط بين المضافين ولا حاجة فيه إلى الوحدة الشخصية ويوضحه المتخالفان من الإضافات كالأبوة والبنوة، إذ يشتبه على ذي مسكة أنهما متغايرتان بالشخص بل بالنوع أيضاً مع وجود الارتباط بهما بين المضافين أعني الأب والابن ويلزمهم قيامه بأكثر من أمرين أعني محلين فإنَّ الجوار والقرب والأخوة مثلاً كما يتحقق بين شيئين يتحقق أيضاً بين أشياء متعددة فلو جاز اتحادها هناك جاز اتحادها هاهنا أيضا ولا يندفع هذا الإلزام عنهم إلا ببيان الفرق
And even if certain conviction is obtained without it, we find no opponent except that the ancient philosophers who affirm the existence of relations allowed the subsistence of such things as adjacency, proximity, brotherhood, and other relations similar at both ends. They said: if each of the two relata has a distinct relation subsisting in it, each would be cut off from the other, so there must be a single relation subsisting in both to connect them. But the truth is that they are two likes: the proximity of this to that is specifically different (mukhalif bi al-shakhs) from the proximity of that to this, even if they share in the specific reality. And this sharing, i.e., specific unity, suffices for connecting the two relata, and there is no need for personal unity. This is clarified by the two different kinds of relations, like paternity and sonship, for it is obvious to anyone with discernment that they differ in person and even in species, while the connection between the relata, i.e., the father and the son, exists through them. And it follows from their view that a single relation subsists in more than two things, i.e., two loci, for adjacency, proximity, and brotherhood, for example, also obtain between multiple things, just as they obtain between two. If unification is allowed there, it would be allowed here as well, and this objection cannot be repelled except by showing a difference.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the ancient philosophers' argument for a single relation subsisting in two loci. The author first clarifies that he is discussing a point about which there is near consensus except for these philosophers. They argued that if each relatum had its own relation, they would be disconnected, so a single relation must connect them. The author counters that the two relations (e.g., the proximity of A to B and B to A) are personally distinct, even if they share the same specific nature. The specific unity is sufficient for connection; personal unity is unnecessary. He illustrates with different relations like paternity and sonship, which are distinct in person and species yet still connect father and son. Furthermore, he points out a necessary consequence of their view: if a single relation can subsist in two things, then it should also subsist in more than two, since relations like adjacency can exist among multiple things. This leads to an absurdity unless they can prove a distinction.

Key Terms
إضافة
جوار
قرب
أخوة
وحدة نوعية
وحدة شخصية
مخالف بالشخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That two relata necessarily require a single, personally identical relation to connect them.
Correction: Two relations that are personally distinct but share the same specific nature are sufficient for connection, as shown by the proximity example and the case of paternity and sonship.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the refutation against the ancient philosophers?
A) Two relata cannot be connected without a single relation.
B) The two relations are personally distinct and specific unity suffices for connection.
C) A single relation can subsist in more than two loci.
D) Relations like paternity and sonship are identical.

Key Takeaway
Two relations that are personally distinct but share the same specific nature are sufficient to connect two relata; a single personal relation is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your relationships with others, do you assume that because two people share a label (e.g., 'friend' or 'brother'), the connection is identical from both sides? How might recognizing the personal distinctness of each relation change how you treat them?
?
When you face a conflict between two people, do you search for a single explanation to tie everything together? What if the truth is that there are two distinct perspectives that share only a specific reality — are you willing to accept that complexity without demanding unity?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that specific unity is enough for connection. Is your connection to Allah built on a specific unity (e.g., shared attributes of mercy) or do you demand a personal unity that reduces Him to your own expectations? How can you deepen your relationship without forcing Him into your mold?

Abu Hashim on Composition as an Accident in Two Substances and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes Abu Hashim's argument that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hashim's argument that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances and why Al-Jurjani refutes it by appealing to the free agent.

Core Idea
Composition is not a real quality causing cohesion; it is the agent's act.

This passage presents a key debate in kalam about the nature of composition in bodies. Abu Hashim argues that composition must be an accident subsisting in two substances because some bodies cohere tightly. Al-Jurjani refutes this by pointing to the free agent as the direct cause. Understanding this debate clarifies how kalam theologians explained physical cohesion without compromising divine omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to grasp Abu Hashim's argument and the refutation.
Notice the text breaks off mid-sentence for the second point; focus on the complete first part.
2
Identify Abu Hashim's two claims
Abu Hashim makes two claims: (1) composition is an accident, (2) it subsists in two substances. Identify where each claim is stated and how he argues for them.
3
Understand the argument from difficulty of separation
Abu Hashim argues that difficulty of separating parts must be caused by a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) he calls composition. Recognize why he rejects non-existence as the cause.
The logic: difficulty requires a cause; pure non-existence cannot cause; therefore composition is a real accident.
4
Analyze why composition cannot subsist in one part alone
Abu Hashim concludes that composition cannot be in a single part because composition is inconceivable in a single thing. It must subsist in both parts individually, not in their aggregate.
Al-Jurjani notes that Abu Hashim's wording could be clearer; the point is that composition inheres in each of the two parts simultaneously.
5
Study the refutation and its significance
Al-Jurjani rejects the premise by denying that difficulty is due to a composition accident. Instead, the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) attaches parts by choice, causing cohesion. This upholds occasionalist causation.
The second point about more than two substances is only introduced; the text breaks off before the full argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو هاشم: التأليف عرض وأنه يقوم بجوهرين أكثر أما الأول وهو كونه عرضاً يقوم بجوهرين فلان من الجسم ما يصعب انفكاكه وانفصال أجزائه بعضها عن بعض وليس ذلك العسر في الانفكاك إلا لتأليف يوجب ذلك العسر إذ لولاه لما صَعُبَ الانفكاك بين أجزائه كما في المتجاورات ولا يتصور إيجاب العسر وصعوبة الانفكاك نفي العدم المحض فهو يعني التأليف صفة ثبوتية موجودة موجبة لصعوبة الانفصال ولا يقوم التأليف بكل واحد من الجزئين ضرورة أي لا يجوز أن يقوم بهذا الجزء فقط ولا بذاك الجزء فقط لأنَّ التأليف لا يعقل في أمر واحد بالضرورة ولو قال : ولا يقوم بواحد من الجزأين لكان أظهر فهو قائم بهما أي بكل واحد منهما معاً لا بمجموعها من حيث هو مجموع وإلا كان المحل واحداً وهو المطلوب وجوابه منع أن عسر الانفكاك أجزاء بعض الأجسام للتأليف القائم بتلك الأجزاء بل للفاعل المختار الذي ألصق باختياره بعض تلك الأجزاء ببعض على وجه يصعب الانفكاك به وأما الثاني وهو أنه لا يقوم بأكثر من جوهرين فلانه لو قام التأليف الواحد بثلاثة أجزاء
And Abu Hashim said: Composition (ta'lif) is an accident and that it subsists in two substances. As for the first—namely that it is an accident subsisting in two substances—it is because some bodies have difficulty in their separation and the disconnection of their parts from each other. And that difficulty in disconnection is only due to a composition that necessitates that difficulty, since otherwise the disconnection between its parts would not be difficult, as in the case of contiguous things. And it is inconceivable that the causation of difficulty and the difficulty of disconnection [is] a negation of pure non-existence; rather, it is an existing positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) that causes the difficulty of separation. And composition does not subsist in each of the two parts individually, necessarily, i.e., it is not permissible for it to subsist in this part alone nor in that part alone, because composition is inconceivable in a single thing by necessity. Had he said: 'it does not subsist in either of the two parts,' it would be clearer. So it subsists in both, i.e., in each of them together, not in their aggregate insofar as it is an aggregate; otherwise the locus would be one. And that is what is required. Its answer is: We deny that the difficulty of disconnection of the parts of some bodies is due to composition subsisting in those parts; rather, it is due to the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) who attached by His choice some of those parts to others in a way that makes disconnection difficult. As for the second [point]—that it does not subsist in more than two substances—it is because if the single composition [i.e., the same individual] subsisted in three parts...
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hashim's argument that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances, followed by its refutation. The argument has two parts: first, composition is an accident in two substances because some bodies have difficulty in separation, which must be due to a positive attribute (not a negation) that causes that difficulty; this attribute cannot be in one part alone, so it must be in both. Second, it does not subsist in more than two substances, implied but the text breaks off. The refutation denies the premise: the difficulty of separation is not due to a composition attribute but to the free agent who conjoined the parts. The author also notes that Abu Hashim's wording could have been clearer.

Key Terms
تأليف
عرض
جوهر
صفة ثبوتية
عسر الانفكاك
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difficulty of separating parts of a body must be due to a real quality inherent in the body.
Correction: The refutation shows that this difficulty can be directly caused by the free agent's act of conjoining, not by an inherent accident.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the actual cause of difficulty in separating parts of a body?
A) A composition accident subsisting in the parts.
B) The free agent who attached the parts.
C) The nature of the parts themselves.
D) The absence of a separating cause.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's argument that composition is an accident subsisting in two substances is refuted by attributing cohesion to the free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience resistance or difficulty in your life—whether in relationships, work, or personal struggles—do you instinctively search for natural causes, or do you immediately recognize the direct hand of your Creator? How often do you attribute even the very cohesion of your body and circumstances to His constant will rather than to inherent laws? This lesson teaches that the difficulty of separation itself is due to the Free Agent. What would change in your daily consciousness if you truly believed that every 'stickiness' in your life—every obstacle or bond—is directly placed by Allah and can be removed by Him alone? Commit today to mentally affirm Allah's direct action in one situation you normally take for granted.
?
Reflect on a time when you felt trapped or stuck, as if parts of your life were bound together with no way to separate. Did you consider that the very resistance to change was created by Allah and could be undone by His power alone? This passage denies that cohesion is an inherent property of things. It is al-Mukhtar who attaches and detaches. Do you live as if this is true? What would change if you prayed for detachment in that situation with full conviction that Allah can remove the difficulty instantly? Be specific about one area where you need to let go of the belief that obstacles have intrinsic power.
?
The refutation in this lesson redirects your attention from the thing itself to the One who made it so. In your spiritual life, have you ever attributed your own steadfastness in faith or your moral 'cohesion' to your own character or habits, forgetting that Allah holds you together at every moment? How might your humility and reliance increase if you saw every good quality in yourself as a direct creation of Allah, not a natural possession? Identify one trait you pride yourself on and consciously thank Allah for creating it in you right now, this moment.

Ibn Sina's Objection: Number Has Both External and Mental Existence
Ibn Sina asserts the external reality of number and units.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that number has external existence and that the constitutive unit must also exist.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina insists number is real both in the mind and in the world.

Ibn Sina challenges the notion that number is purely mental by asserting that numbers in external objects are undeniable, and from this he deduces the reality of units. Let's examine his reasoning in his own words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Ibn Sina's words once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice his distinction between existence in things and in the soul.
He uses two phrases: 'وجود في الأشياء' and 'وجود في النفس'.
2
Identify his two main claims
First, number has both external and mental existence. Second, since number exists, the unit (الوحدة المقومة) must also exist. Find these in the text.
3
Consider his dismissal of the opposing view
He says the view that number exists only in the soul is not worth heeding. But he qualifies: if someone said it has no abstract existence outside counted items, that would be correct.
He is not saying number has no mental existence; he affirms both.
4
Reflect on the implication of the unit
Ibn Sina thinks that because number is composed of units, if number exists externally, then the units must also exist externally. This is a key step in his argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن سينا: إنَّ العدد له وجود في الأشياء ووجود في النفس، ولا اعتداد بقول من قال لا وجود له إلا في النفس نعم لو قال لا وجود له مجردا عن المعدودات التي هي في الأعيان إلا في النفس لكان حقاً فإنه لا يتجرد عنها قائماً بنفسه، وأما أن في الموجودات أعداداً فذلك أمر لا شك فيه ولما ثبت وجود العدد ثبت وجود الوحدة المقومة له. فأشار المصنف رحمه الله إلى دفع هذه المعارضة بقوله: وأما إن أمراً واحداً يقوم بالمجموع الذي هو المعدودات فإن تخيل لم يكن ذلك الأمر واحداً موجوداً، بل كان اعتبارياً ضرورة أن الاثنين لا يقوم بهما أمر موجود واحد بالهوية. وإن شئت زيادة استيقان فاستبصر بموجود في الخارج ومعدوم فيه فإنهما اثنان وحينئذ فلا يتصور كونها أمراً موجوداً فضلاً عن كونها واحدة بالهوية. أو استبصر بشخص موجود في الشرق وشخص آخر موجود في المغرب فإنهما أيضاً اثنان ويعلم بالضرورة أنه لم يقم بهما معنى واحد بالهوية. وإن أمكن أن يقوم بهذين الاثنين الموجودين معنى موجود فيه تعدد بخلاف الاثنين الأولين إذ لا يمكن أن يقوم بهما أمر موجود أصلاً. بل ذلك الأمر القائم بالمعدودات مجرد فرض واعتبار.
Ibn Sīnā: Number has existence in things and existence in the soul, and no heed is paid to the one who says it has no existence except in the soul. Indeed, if he said it has no existence abstracted from the enumerated things that are in the external world except in the soul, he would be correct, because it is not abstracted from them subsisting by itself. But that there are numbers in existing things is something beyond doubt. And since the existence of number is established, the existence of the unit that constitutes it is established. So the author (may God have mercy on him) alluded to rebutting this objection by his saying: 'As for one thing subsisting by the aggregate that is the enumerated things, if imagined, that thing would not be one existent; rather it would be mental, necessarily because two cannot have a single existent thing identical in identity subsisting by them. And if you wish further certainty, consider an existent [thing] in the external world and a non-existent [thing] in it: they are two, and then it is inconceivable that they be an existent thing, let alone one identical in identity. Or consider a person existing in the East and another person existing in the West: they are also two, and it is known necessarily that no single meaning identical in identity subsists by them. It is possible that a meaning existent in which there is multiplicity subsists by these two existent [things], unlike the first two, for no existent thing can subsist by them at all. Rather that thing subsisting by the enumerated things is mere supposition and consideration.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage records a major objection from Ibn Sīnā (Avicenna) and the author's response. Ibn Sīnā asserts that number has both external and mental existence, and that external numbers are beyond doubt, from which the reality of units follows. The author (al-Ījī or al-Jurjānī) refutes by arguing that a single existent cannot inhere in a set of things (like two enumerated objects) because two things cannot share one identical attribute in a real sense. Two examples are given: (1) an existent and a non-existent together form twoness, but no single existent attribute can belong to both; (2) two persons in the East and West are two, and clearly no single identical meaning inheres in both. Therefore, the number subsisting in the counted items is not a real existent but a mental construct (iʿtibārī). The conclusion: number is mere supposition and consideration, not an external reality.

Key Terms
العدد
الوجود في الأشياء
الوجود في النفس
الوحدة المقومة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ibn Sina denies mental existence and says number only exists externally.
Correction: Ibn Sina explicitly says number has existence both in things and in the soul. He affirms both.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, what follows from the established existence of number?
A) The existence of the soul is established.
B) The existence of the constitutive unit is established.
C) The existence of the counted items is established.
D) The existence of abstract numbers is denied.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argues that numbers and their constitutive units are real both in external objects and in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina insists that numbers are real in the world, not just in your mind. When you look at the objects around you, do you ever pause to see them as signs of an ordered, countable creation? Or do you take that order for granted, as if it were just a mental projection? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that every number you encounter points to a reality beyond your own thoughts?
?
You use numbers every day—counting money, time, people. But do you ever reflect that these numbers are not mere conventions but reflect a real order put in place by the Creator? How can you train yourself to see the numerical structure of the world as a reminder of Allah's wisdom rather than as a neutral tool? What small habit could you start today to make counting an act of worship?

Refutation: Number Cannot Be a Single Existent in Multiple Things
The author refutes Ibn Sina by arguing number cannot be a single existent in multiple things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of Ibn Sina by demonstrating that a single existent cannot inhere in multiple distinct things.

Core Idea
Two distinct things cannot share a single identical existent attribute.

The author counters Ibn Sina by arguing that if number were a real existent inhering in the counted objects, it would have to be one thing present in many, which is impossible. Clear examples show why number is merely a mental consideration.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the author's response carefully. Notice he starts by stating that if one thing were to subsist by a set, it would be mental, not real.
Key phrase: 'لم يكن ذلك الأمر واحداً موجوداً، بل كان اعتبارياً'.
2
Understand the main argument
The argument is that two distinct things (e.g., an existent and a non-existent) cannot have a single existent thing identical in identity (واحد بالهوية) subsisting by them. This is because the two-ness prevents a single real attribute.
3
Examine the first example
Consider an existing thing and a non-existing thing: they are two. No single existent attribute can belong to both, because one is existent and the other is not.
This shows the impossibility clearly.
4
Examine the second example
Consider two persons, one in the East and one in the West. They are two, and it is self-evident that no single identical meaning (like 'humanity' in a real sense) inheres in both as one identical existent.
They share a universal concept mentally, not a real existent.
5
Conclude the refutation
Since number, if real, would have to be such a single existent in many items, and that is impossible, number is merely a mental construct (فرض واعتبار).
The author concludes that the thing subsisting by counted items is mere supposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?

Second Proof from Water Surfaces
Second proof from water surfaces with objections and answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the change of water surfaces demonstrates the reality of continuous quantity (the teaching body) and how objections to this proof are answered.

Core Idea
Water surfaces don't vanish into nothing—they trade places, proving continuous quantity is real.

The second proof of the philosophers for continuous quantity uses a concrete example everyone can observe: water surfaces. When two bodies of water join, two surfaces become one; when one is split, one becomes two. The author argues that these changes are real—surfaces are not mere nothing—so a real continuous quantity must exist that is gained and lost. He then anticipates and refutes two objections to this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before analysis.
Notice the two examples: water joining and water splitting.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The author first gives two examples, then draws a conclusion (gives existence and gives change). Find these parts.
3
Understand the first objection
The first objection says the renewal is only in bodily form, not a separate quantity. See how the author refutes it by denying the restriction.
4
Understand the second objection
The second objection says the removal/occurrence is the very change, causing redundancy. Note the answer: difference in description suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً فالماءان إذا اتصلا فقط بطل السطح المتعدد الذي كان لهما وحدث سطح آخر هو واحد . والشيء الواحد كالماء في كوز إذا قطع بأن صب مثلا في كوزين زال عنه سطحه الواحد وحصل فيه سطحان بعد العدم . وكل ذلك الذي ذكرناه من زوال مقدار جسمي إلى مقدار آخر ومن زوال سطحين وحدوث سطح واحد، ومن زوال سطح واحد وحدوث سطحين يعطي الوجود أي وجود المقدار الذي هو الجسم التعليمي والسطح لأن الزائل والمتجدد المذكورين ليسا محض العدم بل هما موجودان زال أحدهما وحدث الآخر، ويعطي التبدل أي توارد المقادير الجسمية. قوله : (أيضا فالماءان الخ) فإن قلت : التجدد في الصورتين المذكورتين للصورة الجسمية فلا يثبت على تقدير تمام الدليل إلا وجودها قلت : انحصار التبدل فيها ممنوع. قوله : ( ويعطي التبدل) لا يقال زوال مقدار جسمي إلى مقدار آخر عين التبدل فيتحد المعطي والمعطى فلا يصح لأنا نقول يكفي في الصحة التغاير في العنوان والاعتبار
And also: two bodies of water, when they only come into contact, the multiple surface that belonged to them ceases and another surface that is one arises. And a single thing like water in a jug, when it is divided—for example by pouring it into two jugs—its single surface disappears and two surfaces arise after non-existence. All that which we have mentioned—from the removal of a bodily magnitude to another magnitude, and from the removal of two surfaces and the occurrence of one surface, and from the removal of one surface and the occurrence of two surfaces—gives existence, i.e., existence of the magnitude that is the teaching body and the surface, because the removed and renewed mentioned are not mere non-existence, but they are existents, one removed and the other occurred. And it gives change, i.e., the succession of bodily magnitudes. His saying: (also: two bodies of water...) For if you say: The renewal in the two mentioned images is of the bodily form, so on the assumption of the proof's completeness, only its existence would be established. I say: The restriction of change to it is denied. His saying: (And it gives change) It cannot be said: The removal of a bodily magnitude to another magnitude is exactly change, so the giver and the given unite and are not valid. For we say: Sufficient for validity is the difference in description and consideration.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof from water surfaces and splitting, showing the existence of continuous quantity (the teaching body and surface). When two water bodies touch, their two surfaces vanish and a single surface appears; when one body is split, one surface vanishes and two appear. These changes are real—surfaces are not mere nothing—so they must be existents that disappear and appear, indicating a continuous quantity that changes. The commentary addresses objections: First, that the change might be only in the 'bodily form' (al-sura al-jismiyya) not in a separate quantity—denied as arbitrary restriction. Second, that the removal and occurrence are the very change itself, causing redundancy—answered by pointing out that the terms are distinct in description and consideration.

Key Terms
السطح
المقدار الجسمي
التبدل
الجسم التعليمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the change of surfaces is merely a change of bodily form, not proof of a distinct continuous quantity.
Correction: The author denies that change is restricted to bodily form, insisting it proves a separate teaching body.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the disappearance of two water surfaces and appearance of one show?
A) That water has no real surface
B) That a bodily magnitude exists and changes
C) That water is composed of atoms
D) That surfaces are accidental forms

Key Takeaway
The change of water surfaces proves the existence of a real continuous quantity (teaching body) distinct from the body itself, and objections based on restricting change to bodily form or redundancy are refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The objection that change is only in 'bodily form' is refuted by insisting on the distinct reality of continuous quantity. Are there areas in your faith where you reduce Allah's signs to mere abstractions? What will you do today to see them as they really are—proofs of His existence and power?

Time, Motion, and Body as Atomic
Al-Jurjani's conclusion on atomic time, motion, and body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani argues that time, motion, and distance must all be composed of indivisible parts if time exists.

Core Idea
Time, motion, and distance are mutually interdependent and all atomic.

Al-Jurjani now extends the earlier proof to all dimensions of change. He shows that if time exists as a series of present moments, then every part of time is itself an indivisible instant. Since motion and distance correspond to time, they too must be composed of indivisible atoms. This completes the atomist argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the argument progresses from time to motion to distance.
The passage is dense; reading it slowly will help.
2
Identify the three things and their relationship
Identify the three concepts: time, motion, and distance (body). Observe how al-Jurjani connects them: time is an accident of motion, motion is an accident of distance.
The key is that they are mutually corresponding.
3
Understand the conclusion about indivisibility
Recognize that because each part of time is present and indivisible, time is composed of instants. Then, since motion and distance correspond to time, they too must be atomic. The final sentences summarize this chain.
This is a logical necessity if time exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجزء الثاني) الذي سيحضر عقيب هذا الحاضر (و) الجزء ( الثالث ) الذي يحضر عقيب الثاني. إذ ما من جزء من أجزاء الزمان ماضياً كان أو مستقبلاً (إلا وهو حاضر حيناً ما وقد عرفت أن الحاضر غير منقسم فتكون أجزاء الزمان غير منقسمة المسماة بالآنات ( فيتركب ( الزمان ) من آنات متتالية والمفروض أنه ) أي الزمان ( ) موجود فتكون الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ لأنه أعني الزمان ( من عوارضها وينطبق عليها وكذلك الجسم الذي هو المسافة يكون مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجرأ ( لأنها ) أي الحركة (من عوارضه ) أي منطبقة عليه وبالجملة فالزمان والحركة والمسافة أمور متطابقة، بحيث إذ فرض في أحدها جزء يفرض بإزائه من كل واحد من الآخرين جزء، فإذا تركب أحدهما من أجزاء لا تتجزأ كان الآخر كذلك . فظهر أنه لو كان الزمان موجوداً لكان الزمان الحاضر موجوداً ولو كان الزمان الحاضر موجوداً لكان الجسم مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ
The second part that will come after this present, and the third part that comes after the second. For there is no part of time, whether past or future, except that it is present at some time. And you have known that the present is indivisible; thus parts of time are indivisible, called instants. So time is composed of successive instants, and it is assumed that time exists; so motion is composed of indivisible parts, because time is an accident of motion and corresponds to it; likewise, the body which is the distance is composed of indivisible parts, because motion is an accident of it and corresponds to it. In summary, time, motion, and distance are mutually corresponding things, such that if a part is assumed in one, a part opposite to it is assumed in each of the others. So if one of them is composed of indivisible parts, the other is likewise. Thus it appears that if time existed, then the present moment would exist; and if the present moment existed, then the body would be composed of indivisible parts.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is the logical extension of the previous proof. It starts by considering any part of time after the present moment, showing that each such part is itself present at some time and therefore indivisible. Consequently, all parts of time are indivisible instants (anats), and time is composed of consecutive instants. Since time exists (by hypothesis), motion—which is associated with time—must also be composed of indivisible parts. Similarly, the body (or distance) that motion traverses is composed of indivisible parts because motion is its accident and corresponds to it. The three—time, motion, and distance—are mutually corresponding: if one has indivisible parts, the others do too. The final sentence summarizes the entire argument: if time exists, the present exists; if the present exists (and is indivisible), then body is composed of indivisible parts.

Key Terms

Correction: The argument shows that if time exists, each part is a present instant, and the present is indivisible, so time must be composed of discrete, atomic instants. Continuity would require a part that is not present.
