Time's Existence Implies Indivisible Parts
Argument that if time exists, it is composed of indivisible nows, forcing body into indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that if time exists, it must be composed of indivisible instants, leading to the conclusion that the body must be composed of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
If time exists, atoms follow; opponents must reject time.

This passage presents a clever argument by necessary implication (ilzām). The Mutakallimūn argue that if time existed, it would have to be made up of indivisible ‘nows’, and that would force motion and the body to be likewise indivisible. Since the opponent rejects atoms, time cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Note that the author uses a very structured logical chain.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify the terms: present (al-ḥāḍir), time (az-zamān), motion (al-ḥaraka), distance/al-body (al-masāfa). Understand how they relate as accidents.
3
Follow the logical chain
The argument proceeds: time is composed of instants → motion is composed of indivisible parts → body is composed of indivisible parts. Trace each step.
This is an ilzām: the opponent is forced to accept atoms or deny time.
4
Recognize the argument as ilzām
Understand that the author does not claim time is actually composed of instants; he shows that if time existed, this would follow, and since the opponent rejects atoms, time does not exist.
This is a standard dialectical technique in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد السابع : إنكار المتكلمين للزمن
وهي
الجزء الثاني) الذي سيحضر عقيب هذا الحاضر (و) الجزء ( الثالث ) الذي يحضر عقيب الثاني. إذ ما من جزء من أجزاء الزمان ماضياً كان أو مستقبلاً (إلا وهو حاضر حيناً ما وقد عرفت أن الحاضر غير منقسم فتكون أجزاء الزمان غير منقسمة المسماة بالآنات ( فيتركب ( الزمان ) من آنات متتالية والمفروض أنه ) أي الزمان ) ) موجود فتكون الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ لأنه أعني الزمان ( من عوارضها وينطبق عليها وكذلك الجسم الذي هو المسافة يكون مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ( لأنها ) أي الحركة (من عوارضه ) أي منطبقة عليه وبالجملة فالزمان والحركة والمسافة أمور متطابقة، بحيث إذ فرض في أحدها جزء يفرض بإزائه من كل واحد من الآخرين جزء، فإذا تركب أحدهما من أجزاء لا تتجزأ كان الآخر كذلك . فظهر أنه لو كان الزمان موجوداً لكان الزمان الحاضر موجوداً ولو كان الزمان الحاضر موجوداً لكان الجسم مركباً من أجزاء لا تتجزأ ( وأنتم لا تقولون (به ) أي بتركب الجسم من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ فيتم الاستدلال عليكم إلزاماً
The second observatory - the seventh aim: The Mutakallimun's denial of time. And it is: the second part that will exist after this present, and the third part that exists after the second. For there is no part of the parts of time, past or future, except that it is present at some time, and you have known that the present is indivisible; thus the parts of time are indivisible, called instants. So time is composed of successive instants, and it is assumed that it (i.e., time) exists. Thus motion is composed of indivisible parts, because I mean time is among its accidents and applies to it; and likewise the body that is the distance is composed of indivisible parts, because it (i.e., motion) is among its accidents (i.e., applying to it). In sum, time, motion, and distance are corresponding things, such that if a part is assumed in one of them, a part is assumed corresponding to it from each of the other two. So if one of them is composed of indivisible parts, the other is likewise. So it appears that if time existed, then present time would exist; and if present time existed, then the body would be composed of indivisible parts – and you do not say (i.e., accept) that (i.e., the composition of the body from indivisible parts); thus the argument is completed against you as a necessary implication.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage presents a key argument against the existence of time, aimed at those who deny atomic composition of the body. It is an argument by necessary implication (ilzām). The author begins by stating that every part of time, past or future, is present at some moment. Since the present moment is indivisible (as established earlier), the parts of time must be indivisible, i.e., instants. Therefore, time is composed of successive instants. If time exists, motion (which is a temporal accident) must also be composed of indivisible parts because it corresponds to time. Similarly, the body (the distance traversed) must be composed of indivisible parts because motion applies to it. The conclusion is that if time exists, the body would be composed of indivisible atoms – a position the opponent (presumably the philosopher) rejects. Thus the argument forces the opponent to either accept atoms or deny time's existence.

جزء لا يتجزأ
الزمان

المسافة
الحاضر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The Mutakallimūn are asserting that time is actually composed of instants.
Correction: No, this is an ilzām argument: they show that if time existed, this would follow, but they themselves deny time's existence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Mutakallimūn use an ilzām argument to show that time's existence would entail atoms, which the opponent denies, thus proving time's non-existence.

Are you willing to follow the argument wherever it leads?
?
The Mutakallimūn maintained that time is not an independent reality. How does the way you live your life reflect whether you believe time is real or merely a succession of created moments? Does your daily schedule reflect an urgency for the Hereafter or a comfort in the illusion of time?

Defending the Argument: Imaginary vs. Actual Division
Response to the objection that division can be imaginary, not actual, preserving the argument's force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mutakallimun's position on the possibility of all divisions defends the argument for atomism against the objection of merely imaginary division.

Core Idea
Imaginary division only matters if it can become actual; otherwise it is irrelevant.

The objection to the argument for indivisible parts claims that the present moment could be divided in imagination even if not in reality. This lesson unpacks the author's response: he distinguishes two senses of division—imaginary and actual—and shows that only actual division threatens the argument, but the Mutakallimun hold that all divisions are possible, so imaginary division could become actual, leading to an infinite regress. The passage clarifies the conditions under which imaginary division is relevant.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the author's two branched response.
The passage is concise; reread for clarity.
2
Identify the two types of division
Find where the author distinguishes imaginary (wahmi) and actual (fi'li) division. Understand the different implications.
3
Grasp the Mutakallimun's position
Note the claim that all divisions are possible by God's power. This undermines the objection because imaginary division could become actual.
This connects to the infinite regress argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لامتناع إلخ فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد الانقسام الوهمي فلا نسلم امتناعة وإن راد الفعلي فمسلم لكن اللازم أن يكون الحاضر غير منقسم بالانقسام الفعلي وهو لا يستلزم الجزء إلا بدعي أن الانقسام الوهمي يستلزم الفعلي على ما عليه المتكلمون حيث قالوا: إِنَّ جميع الانقسامات ممكنة فيجوز أن يكون متعلقاً به قدرته تعالى فيمكن وقوعه فحينئذ نختار الشق الأول ويبين امتناعه بأنه يستلزم إمكان وجود الأمور الغير المتناهية بالفعل.
الوهمي إن طابق الواقع بأن يكون فيه شيء غير شيء بحسب نفس الأمر لزم اجتماع الأجزاء المحكوم ببطلانه أو لا، وإن لم يطابق فلا عبرة به ولزم الجزء في نفس الأمر لأن الانقسام الفرضي المنفي من الجزء هو الفرضي المطابق للواقع كما حقق في موضعه.
His saying: 'for the impossibility etc.' – there is discussion in it, because if he means imaginary division, we do not concede its impossibility; and if he means actual division, it is conceded, but the necessary consequence is that the present is indivisible by actual division, and that does not entail the part unless one claims that imaginary division entails actual division, as is the position of the Mutakallimun who say: indeed all divisions are possible, so it is permissible that it be related to His power, may He be exalted, so it is possible for it to occur. Then we choose the first alternative and show its impossibility in that it entails the possibility of actually existing infinite things. Imaginary division: if it corresponds to reality, such that there is something other than something according to the actual state of affairs, then the aggregation of parts, which is judged false, necessarily follows; or if it does not correspond, then it is disregarded, and the part necessarily follows in actual fact, because the hypothetical division whose negation is part of the part is the hypothetical that corresponds to reality, as verified in its place.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection regarding the indivisibility of the present moment. The objection likely argued that the present could be divided in imagination (wahm) even if not actually. The author clarifies that if the opponent means imaginary division, then its impossibility is not conceded; but if actual division is meant, then it is conceded that the present is indivisible in actual fact. However, the required conclusion (that the body is composed of indivisible parts) does not follow unless one claims that imaginary division implies actual division. This is exactly the position of the Mutakallimun (Islamic theologians): all divisions are possible in relation to God's power, so imaginary division could become actual. Thus the objector might choose the first branch (imaginary division) but that leads to infinite regress, which is impossible. The author then explains that imaginary division is only relevant if it corresponds to reality; if it does, then aggregation of parts results (which is false), and if not, such division has no bearing. The actual indivisibility of the present remains.

Correction: Imaginary division only matters if it can become actual; the Mutakallimun hold that it can, leading to infinite regress, which actually supports atomism.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imaginary division only weakens the argument for atomism if it can become actual, which the Mutakallimun affirm is possible, leading to an infinite regress, thus preserving the necessity of indivisible parts.

The Argument from Differential Speed for a Magnitude in Motion
Argument for an extended magnitude in motion from differential speed cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that motion possesses an extended quantity beyond speed, based on the independence of distance covered from speed.

Core Idea
Variation in motion's magnitude proves it has quantity by essence.

This passage presents a key argument from the commentator establishing that motion contains a magnitude (quantity) beyond mere speed. By considering two cases — where speed differs but distance is constant, and where speed is constant but distance differs — the arguer demonstrates that motion must have an extended property that admits increase and decrease, which ultimately must be quantity (al-kamm) by essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Note the two scenarios describing differential speed and distance.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The text gives two cases: (1) two motions with different speeds but the same extended thing (showing variation is not due to speed), and (2) two motions with the same speed but different extended things (showing variation exists even when speed is constant).
3
Understand the inference to quantity
The argument concludes that motion contains something that admits increase/decrease, and this must terminate at quantity by essence because equality/inequality are proper to quantity.
This is a typical kalam reasoning about properties.

The second observation - seventh section: The denial of time by the mutakallimun. The distance (as in the fast and slow [movements] supposed later) and (nor) is that variation also due to speed and slowness because of the unity of that extended thing by which variation occurs (with difference in speed and slowness as in this mentioned picture as well). I mean the fast and slow supposed later. (And because of its difference) i.e., the difference of that extended thing (with unity in speed and slowness as in the two movements which were supposed equal in speed and different in beginning and cessation). And it will come in explaining its reality that it is continuous quantity (kamm muttasil) and magnitude with a non-stationary form which is the fastest of movements. (And the answer) to this aspect (is that movement from the beginning of the distance to its end) i.e., movement in the sense of cutting (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ) does not exist at all except according to imagination, and necessity also rules the impossibility of its external existence, as we pointed out previously. And in al-Shifā: since the distance exists and the limits of the distance exist, the thing that is like being upon it and corresponding to it or cutting it or being a measure of cutting it has some kind of existence, so if it is said that it has no existence at all, it would be false.) His saying: (as we pointed out—you have known the state of what he pointed out with it.) His saying: (and not) returning to speed, etc. The conclusion is that the cause of variation between two movements in increase and decrease is not that one of the two movements is faster than the other, because of the non-circularity in existence and non-existence. As for the first, because of the realization of difference in speed and slowness, the variation between the two movements in increase and decrease is negated. And as for the second, because of the realization of variation between them with unity in speed and slowness, so he alluded in the first to the unity of that extended thing from the unity of the two movements, and in the second to its difference from their difference, due to apparent entailment. His saying: (And the answer to this—this answer is a counter-argument as is not hidden; as for the solution, i.e., the detailed refutation, it is what we mentioned previously.

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the existence of a magnitude (quantity) in motion distinct from speed, followed by its refutation. The argument proceeds: Two motions may differ in distance covered even when speed is the same, or be equal when speed differs, so motion must contain an extended thing that admits increase and decrease. This extended thing must ultimately be quantity by essence. The commentator then quotes the answer: motion in the sense of cutting (movement from start to end) does not exist externally; it is only imaginary. He then defends this answer, discussing objections from both mutakallimun and philosophers, clarifying that the answer negates the externality of this quantity but not its existence as an imagined extension. The passage concludes with a note that the answer is a counter-argument and the detailed refutation is given elsewhere.

Refuting the Argument: The Imaginary Nature of Motion-as-Cut
Refutation of the argument for a magnitude in motion by denying the external existence of motion-as-cut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument for a magnitude in motion fails because motion in the sense of cutting does not exist externally.

Core Idea
Motion-as-cut is merely imaginary; no external magnitude is proven.

This passage presents the refutation of the previous argument. The answer stated that movement understood as 'cutting' (al-haraka bi-ma'na al-qat') — i.e., the traversing from start to end as a continuous whole — does not exist in the external world; it is only a mental construct. Therefore, the supposed extended quantity is not a real property of motion. The passage then discusses further nuances and objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the answer and the following commentary. Focus on the claim that motion-as-cut does not exist externally.
The answer is short: 'movement from the beginning of the distance to its end does not exist at all except according to imagination'.
2
Understand the reason
For mutakallimun, motion is composed of discrete atoms (akwan) with no connection; for philosophers, motion-as-cut has no independent existence. Note the disagreement.
The commentary explains both perspectives.
3
Appreciate the force of the refutation
If motion-as-cut is not external, then the argument's extended quantity is not a real attribute of motion, thus the argument fails.
The passage ends by noting this answer is a counter-argument.

Source Text

The second observation - seventh section: The denial of time by the mutakallimun. The distance (as in the fast and slow [movements] supposed later) and (nor) is that variation also due to speed and slowness because of the unity of that extended thing by which variation occurs (with difference in speed and slowness as in this mentioned picture as well). I mean the fast and slow supposed later. (And because of its difference) i.e., the difference of that extended thing (with unity in speed and slowness as in the two movements which were supposed equal in speed and different in beginning and cessation). So in movement there is something that admits variation (by increase and decrease) and it must terminate to what admits it essentially, which is quantity (al-kamm); because it has passed that the acceptance of equality and inequality is among the properties of quantity essentially, and that what is other than it is only characterized by them following it. And it will come in explaining its reality that it is continuous quantity (kamm muttasil) and magnitude with a non-stationary form which is the fastest of movements. (And the answer) to this aspect (is that movement from the beginning of the distance to its end) i.e., movement in the sense of cutting (al-ḥaraka bi-maʿnā al-qaṭʿ) does not exist at all except according to imagination, and necessity also rules the impossibility of its external existence, as we pointed out previously. And in al-Shifā: since the distance exists and the limits of the distance exist, the thing that is like being upon it and corresponding to it or cutting it or being a measure of cutting it has some kind of existence, so if it is said that it has no existence at all, it would be false.) His saying: (as we pointed out—you have known the state of what he pointed out with it.) His saying: (and not) returning to speed, etc. The conclusion is that the cause of variation between two movements in increase and decrease is not that one of the two movements is faster than the other, because of the non-circularity in existence and non-existence. As for the first, because of the realization of difference in speed and slowness, the variation between the two movements in increase and decrease is negated. And as for the second, because of the realization of variation between them with unity in speed and slowness, so he alluded in the first to the unity of that extended thing from the unity of the two movements, and in the second to its difference from their difference, due to apparent entailment. His saying: (And the answer to this—this answer is a counter-argument as is not hidden; as for the solution, i.e., the detailed refutation, it is what we mentioned previously.

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the existence of a magnitude (quantity) in motion distinct from speed, followed by its refutation. The argument proceeds: Two motions may differ in distance covered even when speed is the same, or be equal when speed differs, so motion must contain an extended thing that admits increase and decrease. This extended thing must ultimately be quantity by essence. The commentator then quotes the answer: motion in the sense of cutting (movement from start to end) does not exist externally; it is only imaginary. He then defends this answer, discussing objections from both mutakallimun and philosophers, clarifying that the answer negates the externality of this quantity but not its existence as an imagined extension. The passage concludes with a note that the answer is a counter-argument and the detailed refutation is given elsewhere.

الوجود الخارجي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument successfully proves that motion contains a real external magnitude.
Correction: The refutation shows that the magnitude is only imaginary because motion-as-cut does not exist externally.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the argument from differential speed not prove an external magnitude in motion?
A) Because motion does not admit increase and decrease
B) Because movement in the sense of cutting (الحركة بمعنى القطع) does not exist externally
C) Because speed and slowness are the only factors
D) Because the argument only applies to circular motion

Key Takeaway
The refutation defeats the argument by showing that motion-as-cut is not externally real, so any inferred quantity is not an attribute of real motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Think of a time when you assumed something was true about yourself (e.g., 'I am not good at X') because it felt real in your mind. Is that assumption based on external reality or just your estimation? What step can you take this week to test it against reality?
?
The commentator notes that the refutation is incomplete—it only negates subsistence, not existence. In your spiritual journey, are there areas where you settle for a partial answer or a comfortable half-truth? What would it cost you to seek the full truth, even if it means admitting your earlier reasoning was insufficient?

Time as a Self-Subsistent Substance with Accidental Relations
The author states that time is a substance with accidental relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that time is a self-subsistent substance whose relations to events are accidental, not essential.

Core Idea
Time's essence is unchanging; its relations to events are accidental.

This lesson examines a key philosophical claim about time. The author asserts that time is a substance (jawhar) that exists independently, and its various relations to events (like before, after) are accidental modifications. This defends the substantiality of time against those who see it as merely a relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the terms: جوهر, قائم بنفسه, نسب, عرض.
The passage is short but dense; take time with each term.
2
Identify the key claim
The claim is that time is a substance with accidental relations. What does 'accidental' mean here? It means not essential to time's nature.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider an example: the event of sunrise is 'after' the event of the call to prayer. Is that 'after' part of time's essence? According to the author, no — it's an accidental relation.
This shows that time can have different relations without changing its substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أنه جوهر إلخ) قالوا الزمان جوهر قائم بنفسه وله نسب مختلفة إلى الحوادث وتلك النسب قابلة للزيادة والنقصان والمساواة فالزمان قابل لهذه الأمور لا لذاته بل بالعرض.
His saying: ('that it is a substance etc.') They said: time is a substance subsisting by itself, and it has different relations to events. Those relations are capable of increase, decrease, and equality. So time is capable of these things not by itself but accidentally.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage further elaborates on the view that time is a substance. The commentator explains that according to some, time is a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qa'im bi nafsihi). It has various relations (nisab) to events (hawadith), such as being prior, posterior, simultaneous, etc. These relations can change—they can increase, decrease, or be equal. However, time itself, in its essence, does not possess these relations; rather, they are accidental ('arad) to it. This means that time's essence is unchanging, but its relations to events are contingent and variable.

Key Terms
جوهر
قائم بنفسه
نسب
حوادث
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time's properties like 'before' and 'after' are essential to time.
Correction: The passage states that these properties are accidental, not essential.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the relations of time to events relate to time itself?
A) They are essential to time.
B) They are accidental to time.
C) They are the substance of time.
D) They are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Time is a self-subsistent substance, and its relations to events are accidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live your life ruled by the relations of time – schedules, deadlines, memories. But this lesson teaches that time's essence is unchanging; only its relations to events are accidental. Are you confusing the accidental rush of events with the substance of time itself? Will you today separate your sense of purpose from the shifting accidents of your schedule?
?
If time's capability for increase and decrease is accidental, then your busyness or slowness does not affect time's reality – only your relation to it. Are you allowing the accidental relations of time (work, distractions) to define your worth? Name one way you will realign your priorities based on the unchanging nature of time as a substance.
?
The source says time itself cannot inherently increase or decrease – only its relations can. Reflect: when you feel 'time is running out,' are you feeling a change in time's essence or a change in your relation to fixed events? How will you use this truth to reduce anxiety about time 'passing'? Write one concrete step.

The First Refutation: Non-Existence After Existence vs. Absolute Non-Existence
First refutation distinguishing non-existence after existence from absolute non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first answer refutes the necessity of time by showing that the argument only negates non-existence after existence, not absolute non-existence.

Core Idea
Time could have never existed; only non-existence after existence is impossible.

The argument that time must be necessary assumes that if time could be non-existent, it would be after existence, leading to contradiction. But what if time never existed at all? The first refutation exposes this gap, showing that absolute non-existence is not ruled out.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the first answer once, then its literal translation. Notice how the author distinguishes two types of non-existence.
The key term is 'العدم المطلق' (absolute non-existence).
2
Identify the two types of non-existence
Find where the text contrasts 'non-existence after existence' with 'absolute non-existence'. Understand that the argument only addresses the former.
The text says: 'فالممتنع على الزمان هو العدم الذي يكون بعد وجوده والعدم الذي يكون قبل وجوده'.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this distinction undermines the claim that time is necessary. If time could have never existed (absolute non-existence), then it is not necessary.
The conclusion: 'فلا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه أي جواب دليل هذا المذهب من وجوه الأول أن هذا الذي استدللتم به ينفي انتفاء الزمان وهو طريان العدم عليه بعد وجوده ولا ينفي عدمه ابتداء بأن لا يوجد أصلاً لأنه لا يصدق أن يقال لو عدم الزمان أصلاً ورأساً لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا يجامع فيها البعد القبل حتى يلزم اجتماع وجوده وعدمه معاً إنما يلزم هذا المحال على تقدير عدمه بعد وجوده وعلى تقدير وجوده بعد عدمه أيضاً فالممتنع على الزمان هو العدم الذي يكون بعد وجوده والعدم الذي يكون قبل وجوده والعدم بعد الوجود أو قبله أخص من العدم المطلق فلا يوجب امتناعه امتناعه لأن العدم المطلق له فرد آخر هو العدم المستمر الذي ليس مسوقاً بالوجود ولا سابقاً عليه وهذا الفرد منه ليس ممتنعاً على الزمان فلا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته الوجه الثاني من وجوده الجواب عن وكل ذلك الدليل هو النقض بأن يقال قولكم أن عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا يجامع فيها البعد القبل بعدية كذلك فهي بالزمان منقوض بتقدم أجزاء الزمان المراد بأجزاء الزمان الأجزاء المفروضة المرتمسة الخيال وهذا لا ينافي بساطة المبدأ عن القائل بأن الزمان جوهر مجرد
And its answer (i.e., the answer to the argument of this position) is in two aspects. The first: that which you have adduced negates the non-existence of time (i.e., the occurrence of non-existence after existence) and does not negate its non-existence initially (i.e., that it does not exist at all), because it cannot be said: if time were non-existent absolutely and completely, its non-existence would be after its existence, a posteriority that does not allow combination with anteriority, such that the coexistence of its existence and non-existence would follow. This impossibility only follows on the assumption of non-existence after existence, and also on the assumption of existence after non-existence. So what is impossible for time is non-existence that is after existence and non-existence that is before existence. And non-existence after existence or before it is more specific than absolute non-existence, so its impossibility does not necessitate the impossibility of absolute non-existence, because absolute non-existence has another individual, which is continuous non-existence that is neither preceded by existence nor antecedent to it, and this individual is not impossible for time, so it does not become necessary of existence in itself. The second aspect of the answer to that whole argument is the refutation by saying: your saying that its non-existence after existence is a posteriority that does not allow combination with anteriority – such a posteriority is temporal, and is refuted by the priority of the parts of time (meaning the supposed parts of time, those of imaginary estimation), and this does not contradict the simplicity of the principle for the one who holds that time is an abstract substance.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two responses to the earlier argument that time is necessary. The first response: the argument only shows that if time existed, it cannot cease to exist (non-existence after existence leads to contradiction). But it does not prove that time must exist from eternity; time could have never existed at all (absolute non-existence without any prior existence). The impossibility of non-existence after existence does not entail impossibility of absolute non-existence. The second response is a counterexample: the argument's reasoning that 'non-existence after existence implies temporal posteriority' can be turned against it by considering the priority of parts of time. The parts of time are successive; one part is prior to another. If we assume the non-existence of a later part, that would involve a posteriority that is temporal, thus requiring time itself to exist. This refutes the argument's claim.

Key Terms
انتفاء
عدم
عدم مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first answer admits that time can be created ex nihilo.
Correction: The first answer only rejects the impossibility of absolute non-existence; it does not affirm that time was created. It simply shows the argument fails to prove necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what does the argument for time's necessity prove?
A) That time cannot be non-existent at all.
B) That time cannot be non-existent after existence.
C) That time must be eternal.
D) That time is an accident.

Key Takeaway
The first refutation shows that the argument only addresses one type of non-existence, not absolute non-existence, so time's necessity is not proven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is impossible in one specific way, it must be impossible altogether? How does this lesson's distinction between 'specific non-existence' and 'absolute non-existence' challenge you to think more precisely about your own assumptions regarding God's power or the nature of creation?
?
The refutation here shows that the burden of proof is not fully met. In your own faith, are there areas where you accept arguments too easily without checking if they really prove what you think? Commit to examining one argument you rely on and see if it truly holds up under this kind of logical scrutiny.
?
If time could have never existed, what does that imply about your own attachment to time-bound things? Are you living as if this world is permanent, or do you truly internalize that even time itself is contingent and could have been absent? What would change if you lived with that reality today?

The Second Refutation: Turning the Argument Using Temporal Parts
Second refutation using the priority of parts of time as a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second answer refutes the argument by showing its reasoning about posteriority is temporal and can be rebutted using the priority of time's parts.

Core Idea
The argument's logic about posteriority itself implies time, so it cannot prove time's necessity.

The second response takes the argument's own reasoning and turns it against itself. If 'non-existence after existence' implies a temporal posteriority, then the priority of parts of time provides a counterexample. This refutes the claim that time must be necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage for the second answer and its literal translation. Focus on the term 'أجزاء الزمان' (parts of time).
The second answer begins with 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Understand the refutation method
The argument claims that non-existence after existence involves a posteriority that cannot combine with anteriority. The second answer points out that this posteriority is itself temporal, and then uses the priority of the parts of time as a counterexample.
The text says: 'فهي بالزمان منقوض بتقدم أجزاء الزمان'.
3
See how the refutation works
Consider: if the argument's reasoning about posteriority is temporal, then it presupposes time. So the argument cannot prove time's necessity without circularity. The priority of parts of time shows that temporal posteriority is not problematic in the way the argument assumes.
This refutation is a 'نقض' (counterexample).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه أي جواب دليل هذا المذهب من وجوه الأول أن هذا الذي استدللتم به ينفي انتفاء الزمان وهو طريان العدم عليه بعد وجوده ولا ينفي عدمه ابتداء بأن لا يوجد أصلاً لأنه لا يصدق أن يقال لو عدم الزمان أصلاً ورأساً لكان عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا يجامع فيها البعد القبل حتى يلزم اجتماع وجوده وعدمه معاً إنما يلزم هذا المحال على تقدير عدمه بعد وجوده وعلى تقدير وجوده بعد عدمه أيضاً فالممتنع على الزمان هو العدم الذي يكون بعد وجوده والعدم الذي يكون قبل وجوده والعدم بعد الوجود أو قبله أخص من العدم المطلق فلا يوجب امتناعه امتناعه لأن العدم المطلق له فرد آخر هو العدم المستمر الذي ليس مسوقاً بالوجود ولا سابقاً عليه وهذا الفرد منه ليس ممتنعاً على الزمان فلا يكون واجب الوجود لذاته الوجه الثاني من وجوده الجواب عن وكل ذلك الدليل هو النقض بأن يقال قولكم أن عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا يجامع فيها البعد القبل بعدية كذلك فهي بالزمان منقوض بتقدم أجزاء الزمان المراد بأجزاء الزمان الأجزاء المفروضة المرتمسة الخيال وهذا لا ينافي بساطة المبدأ عن القائل بأن الزمان جوهر مجرد
And its answer (i.e., the answer to the argument of this position) is in two aspects. The first: that which you have adduced negates the non-existence of time (i.e., the occurrence of non-existence after existence) and does not negate its non-existence initially (i.e., that it does not exist at all), because it cannot be said: if time were non-existent absolutely and completely, its non-existence would be after its existence, a posteriority that does not allow combination with anteriority, such that the coexistence of its existence and non-existence would follow. This impossibility only follows on the assumption of non-existence after existence, and also on the assumption of existence after non-existence. So what is impossible for time is non-existence that is after existence and non-existence that is before existence. And non-existence after existence or before it is more specific than absolute non-existence, so its impossibility does not necessitate the impossibility of absolute non-existence, because absolute non-existence has another individual, which is continuous non-existence that is neither preceded by existence nor antecedent to it, and this individual is not impossible for time, so it does not become necessary of existence in itself. The second aspect of the answer to that whole argument is the refutation by saying: your saying that its non-existence after existence is a posteriority that does not allow combination with anteriority – such a posteriority is temporal, and is refuted by the priority of the parts of time (meaning the supposed parts of time, those of imaginary estimation), and this does not contradict the simplicity of the principle for the one who holds that time is an abstract substance.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents two responses to the earlier argument that time is necessary. The first response: the argument only shows that if time existed, it cannot cease to exist (non-existence after existence leads to contradiction). But it does not prove that time must exist from eternity; time could have never existed at all (absolute non-existence without any prior existence). Key Terms

?

?

Second Answer: Counterexample Using the Parts of Time
Counterexample using parts of time followed by refinement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counterexample argument using the priority of time's parts and the refined response about the need for time when only one relatum is temporal.

Core Idea
The priority between time's parts does not require an external time—so why must priority between existence and non-existence of time?

This passage presents the second answer to an objection: a counterexample drawn from the parts of time themselves. The opponent claimed that the priority between existence and non-existence of time must be temporal, but the author points out that the priority among time's parts is itself a priority yet does not involve a separate time. However, a careful consideration reveals that this analogy is not perfect, leading to a nuanced refinement. Let's unpack this argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the counterexample
Read the first part of the passage where the counterexample is introduced: the priority of parts of time over each other is not temporal in the sense of requiring an additional time.
Notice the argument against infinite regress if it were additional.
2
Understand the refinement (nazar)
Read the second part where the author notes a consideration: the priority among time's parts is temporal but identical to the parts themselves, whereas the non-existence of time has no inherent priority.
This shows the limit of the analogy.
3
Apply the distinction
Understand the gist: when one relatum is time and the other is not, the non-temporal relatum needs time, but the temporal one does not. This applies to priority of existence over non-existence.
Our case is of the latter kind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني من وجوه الجواب هو النقض بأن يقال قولكم أن عدمه بعد وجوده بعدية لا يجامع فيها البعد القبل بعدية كذلك فهي بالزمان منقوض بتقدم أجزاء الزمان بعضها على بعض فإنه ليس بالزمان لما قلنا من لزوم التسلسل في الأزمنة المجتمعة المتطابقة فجاز أن يكون تقدم وجوده على عدمه أو تقدم عدمه على وجوده كذلك أي يكون التقدم والتأخر بين وجوده وعدمه ليس بالزمان كما أجزاء الزمان، وفيه نظر لما تقدم من أنّ التقدم بين أجزائه تقدم زماني لكنه ليس بزمان زائد على المتقدم والمتأخر بل بزمان هو عينهما لأن التقدم والتأخر فيما بينها ناشئان من ذواتها بخلاف عدم الزمان فإنه لا يقتضي لذاته لا تقدماً ولا تأخراً بل لا بد أن يكون معه زمان ليعرض له التقدم أو التأخر بحسبه وتحريره أن كل واحد من المتقدم والمتأخر إذا كان زماناً لم يحتج في شيء منهما إلى زمان زائد عليه وإذا لم يكن شيء منهما زماناً احتيج فيهما إلى الزمان وإذا كان أحدهما زماناً والآخر ليس بزمان احتيج في الآخر إلى الزمان دون الأول وما نحن بصدده من هذا القبيل.
The second face of the answers is the counterexample (al-naqd): by saying that your statement that its non-existence after its existence is a posteriority that does not include prior posteriority—such a posteriority is temporal—is refuted by the priority of some parts of time over others. For that [priority] is not [due to] time, because of what we said about the impossibility of an infinite regress in [the case of] simultaneous coincident times. So it is possible that the priority of its existence over its non-existence, or the priority of its non-existence over its existence, is likewise—i.e., that the priority and posteriority between its existence and non-existence is not due to time, just like the parts of time. But there is a consideration: because, as mentioned earlier, the priority among its parts is temporal priority, but it is not a time additional to the prior and the posterior; rather, it is a time that is identical to them, because the priority and posteriority between them arise from their essences. In contrast, the non-existence of time does not entail in its essence any priority or posteriority; rather, it must be accompanied by time for priority or posteriority to accidentally occur to it. The gist: if both the prior and the posterior are time, neither needs an additional time. If neither is time, both need time. If one is time and the other is not, the non-time needs time but not the time. Our case is of this latter kind.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage gives the second answer: a counterexample (naqd) by pointing to the priority of parts of time themselves. The opponent argues that the priority between existence and non-existence of time must be temporal, but the author observes that the priority of one part of time over another is also a priority, yet it is not a separate time; otherwise an infinite regress would result. Thus, the priority between existence and non-existence of time could also be non-temporal. However, a note (nazar) is added: the priority of time's parts is indeed temporal, but it is not an additional time; it is identical to the parts themselves because the priority arises from their essences. In contrast, the non-existence of time has no inherent priority or posteriority, so it requires an external time to be described as prior or posterior. In the case of the non-existence of time after existence, one of the two relata (existence) is temporal, the other (non-existence) is not. Therefore, the non-existence needs time, but existence does not. So the argument that the priority must be temporal stands, but the counterexample is refined.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any priority must involve an external time; the parts of time themselves are prior without an additional time.
Correction: The priority of time's parts is temporal but not due to an external time; it arises from their essences. However, when one relatum is non-temporal (like non-existence of time), it does require an external time to be described as prior or posterior.

Checkpoint
What is the counterexample (naqd) used in this passage?
A) The impossibility of time's existence
B) The priority of parts of time over each other
C) The necessity of time
D) The non-existence of time before existence

Key Takeaway
The priority between existence and non-existence of time does not require an external time because the existence is itself temporal, while the non-existence requires time to be attributed as posterior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this affect the way you think about the urgency of repentance or the value of each moment? What specific change will you make today because time itself is a created thing that points to its Creator?
?
The passage distinguishes between times that are 'time' and 'non-time'. Your existence is temporal, but your Creator is not. In what areas of your life are you acting as if you are self-sufficient and not dependent on the Eternal? Be honest: where do you feel the most tension between your temporal desires and the eternal purpose for which you were created? What will you realign starting today?

Time as a Continuous Quantity
Aristotle defines time as a continuous quantity measuring motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Aristotle considers time a continuous quantity that measures motion, not a stable thing.

Core Idea
Time is a continuous quantity that measures motion, not rest.

Aristotle begins his proof by establishing that time is a quantity—it can increase or decrease. He then argues that time cannot be composed of indivisible instants, making it a continuous quantity. Finally, he shows that time must measure something non-stable, namely motion. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify time as a quantity
Notice that Aristotle starts by saying time varies by increase and decrease, so it must be a quantity. This is a key property.
Equality and variability are properties of quantities.
2
Understand continuity vs. discreteness
He denies that time is composed of indivisible instants (since that would imply discrete units). Instead, time is continuous—its parts meet at common boundaries.
3
Why time cannot measure a stable thing
If time measured something stable, time itself would be stable, but time flows. Therefore, it must measure a non-stable state: motion.
The stability of the measured would imply the stability of the measure.
4
Summarize the conclusion so far
Time is a continuous quantity that measures motion. This sets the stage for identifying which motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها وهو المشهور فيما بين القوم ما ذهب إليه أرسطو ومن تبعه من أنه مقدار حركة الفلك الأعظم واحتج أرسطو على ذلك بأنه أي الزمان متفاوت بالزيادة والنقصان فهو كم لما مر من أن المساواة والمفاوتة من خواصه وقد ثبت بالبرهان امتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ وتركب الجسم منه فلا يكون الزمان مركباً من آنات متتالية وإلا تركب الجسم من الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ فلا يكون الزمان كماً منفصلاً لاستلزامه تركبه من الآنات المتتالية التي هي الوحدات بل يكون كماً متصلاً فهو مقدار أي كمية متصلة تتلاقي أجزاؤها على حدود مفروضة مشتركة وليس مقداراً لأمر قار تجمع أجزاؤه وإلا كان الزمان قاراً مثله لأن مقدار القار قار بالضرورة، لكن الزمان يستحيل أن يكون قاراً، وإلا كانت الحوادث المتعاقبة مجتمعة معاً فهو مقدار لهيئة غير قارة للجسم المتحرك الذي لا يتصور وجوده متحركاً بدون الزمان وهي الحركة ويمتنع انقطاعها أي انقطاع الحركة التي يكون الزمان مقدارها وإلا انقطع الزمان أيضاً فيلزم عدمه بعد وجوده وهو محال للدليل الذي أثبت به المذهب الأول بعينه فيكون الزمان مقدار الحركة مستديرة لأن الحركة المستقيمة تنقطع لا محالة لتناهي الأبعاد فلا يجوز حينئذ ذهاب المستقيمة على استقامتها إلى غير النهاية ووجوب سكون بين كل حركتين متخالفتين في الجهة صادرتين عن متحرك واحد فلا يجوز أيضاً استمرار المستقيمة ودوامها بانعطاف المتحرك عن جهتها إلى جهة أخرى وهي أي الحركة المستديرة هي الحركة الفلكية ولا شك أنه يقدر به أي بالزمان كل الحركات المتخالفة بالسرعة والبطء فيقال: هذه الحركة مثلا في ساعة وتلك في ساعتين وعلى هذا فيكون الزمان مقداراً الأسرعها لأن أسرع الحركات يكون مقداره أي زمانه أقل
And the fourth of them, which is well-known among the people, is what Aristotle and his followers held: that it is the measure of the motion of the outermost celestial sphere. Aristotle argued for that by saying that time is variable by increase and decrease, so it is a quantity, because equality and variability are among its properties. And it has been demonstrated by proof the impossibility of the indivisible part and the composition of the body from it. So time is not composed of successive instants, otherwise the body would be composed of indivisible parts. So time is not a discrete quantity, because that would entail its composition from successive instants which are units, but it is a continuous quantity. So it is a measure, i.e., a continuous quantity whose parts meet at common assumed limits. And it is not a measure of a stable thing whose parts are combined, for then time would be stable like it, because the measure of a stable thing is necessarily stable. But time cannot be stable, otherwise successive events would be simultaneous. So it is a measure of a non-stable state of a moving body, which cannot be conceived as moving without time, and that is motion. And its interruption is impossible, i.e., the interruption of the motion of which time is the measure, otherwise time would also be interrupted, leading to its non-existence after existence, which is impossible by the same proof that established the first doctrine exactly. So time is the measure of circular motion, because rectilinear motion inevitably ceases due to the finitude of dimensions; thus it is not possible for rectilinear motion to continue in a straight line indefinitely, and there must be rest between any two motions in different directions emanating from a single mover, so it is also not possible for rectilinear motion to be continuous and perpetual by the mover turning from its direction to another direction. And that is, i.e., circular motion, is celestial motion. And there is no doubt that by it, i.e., by time, all motions varying in speed and slowness are measured. So it is said: this motion, for example, is in one hour and that in two hours, and so on. Thus time is the measure of the fastest of them, because the fastest motion has the smallest measure, i.e., its time is least.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents Aristotle's doctrine that time is the measure of the motion of the celestial sphere. The proof proceeds in several steps: (1) Time admits of increase and decrease, so it is a quantity. (2) It has been proven that indivisible points are impossible, so time cannot be composed of indivisible instants; thus it is a continuous quantity, not discrete. (3) As a quantity, it measures something, but it cannot measure a stable thing, because then time would be stable, contradicting its flowing nature. Therefore it must measure a non-stable state, i.e., motion. (4) The motion that time measures must be continuous and unceasing, so it cannot be rectilinear (which would end due to finite space or require rest between direction changes) but must be circular, namely the motion of the celestial sphere. (5) Time then measures all other motions by comparison to this uniform motion, and the fastest motion has the smallest time. The author follows Aristotle's line of reasoning to establish time as a continuous quantity and to identify it specifically with the measure of celestial circular motion.

آنات متتالية
حدود مشتركة

غير قار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Time is composed of indivisible instants (like moments).
Correction: The passage argues that indivisible parts are impossible, so time is continuous, not made of instants.

Checkpoint
Why does Aristotle say time is not a discrete quantity?
A) Because it has parts that meet at common limits
B) Because it would then be composed of indivisible instants, which is impossible
C) Because it is variable
D) Because it measures motion

Key Takeaway
Time is a continuous quantity that measures motion, not a stable thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Time is a measure of motion, not rest. Where in your life have you become stationary—spiritually, in acts of worship, or in seeking knowledge? What specific step will you take today to move again, using this time that Allah has given you?
?
The source argues time is continuous, not made of separate moments. Yet we often live as if our days are isolated checkpoints, forgetting that the past and future flow into now. How does this continuous nature of time affect your sense of accountability to Allah? Will you begin viewing your life as a single, flowing journey toward Him?
?
Aristotle proves time cannot measure a stable thing; otherwise time would be stable. But our hearts often seek stability in this world. What are you clinging to that you should instead let flow? How will you entrust that to Allah and move with time's motion toward Him?

Why Time Must Measure Circular Motion
Aristotle argues time measures circular motion, not rectilinear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Aristotle concludes that time measures the circular motion of the celestial sphere and how it measures all other motions.

Core Idea
Only circular motion is continuous enough to be measured by time.

After establishing that time measures motion, Aristotle must identify which motion it measures. He argues that rectilinear motion cannot be perpetual due to finite space and required rest between direction changes. Only circular motion can be continuous, and this is the motion of the celestial sphere. Time then serves as a measure for all motions by comparison. Let's unpack this reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand why rectilinear motion fails
Read the part about rectilinear motion. Note two reasons: (1) finite dimensions cause it to stop, (2) changing direction requires a pause.
The passage explicitly says rectilinear motion 'inevitably ceases'.
2
Recognize circular motion as perpetual
Since rectilinear motion cannot be continuous, the motion that time measures must be circular. The passage says 'circular motion is celestial motion'.
3
See how time measures other motions
Time measures all motions by comparison to the fastest (celestial) motion. The fastest motion has the smallest time.
The passage gives the example: 'this motion is in one hour, that in two hours'.
4
Synthesize the full argument
Combine this with Lesson 1: time is a continuous quantity measuring motion, and now we know it must be circular motion. This completes Aristotle's definition.

Classification of Relations Between Changing and Constant Things
The author classifies relations between changing and constant things, showing when time is necessary, and refutes Abu al-Barakat's view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author classifies relations between changing and constant things and how this classification refutes Abu al-Barakat's view that time is the measure of all existence.

Core Idea
Constant things exist without time; time is only needed for changing things.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif dives into the relationship between time, change, and constancy. The author presents a clear classification: when two changing things are related, time is required on both sides; when a constant relates to a changing thing, time is needed only on the changing side; and when two constants relate, time is not needed at all. This classification directly refutes Abu al-Barakat's claim that time measures all existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening sentence: constant things can exist without time. This is the foundation for the classification.
The author starts by asserting that time can be conceived such that essences can exist without it.
2
Examine the three types of relations
Identify each type: (1) changing to changing, (2) constant to changing, (3) constant to constant. Notice which require time on one side, both, or none.
These are the core distinctions that will refute Abu al-Barakat.
3
See how the classification refutes Abu al-Barakat
After reflecting on these meanings, the author rejects Abu al-Barakat's view that time is the measure of existence, because constant things are independent of time.
This shows why the classification matters for theology.

يتصور الزمان بحيث إذا نظر إلى ذواتها يمكن أن تكون موجودة بلا زمان، فإذا نسب متغير إلى متغير بالمعية أو القبلية فلا بد هناك من زمان في كلا الجانبين، وإذا نسب بهما ثابت إلى متغير، فلا بد من الزمان في أحد جانبيه دون الآخر، وإذا نسب ثابت إلى ثابت بالمعية كان الجانبان مستغنيين عن الزمان وإن كانا مقارنين له فهذه معان معقولة متفاوتة عبر عنها بعبارات مختلفة تنبيهاً على تفاوتها وإذا تؤمل فيها اندفع ما ذهب إليه أبو البركات من أنَّ الزمان مقدار الوجود حيث قال : إن الباقي لا بقاؤه إلا في الزمان ومالا يكون حصوله إلا في الزمان ويكون باقياً لا بد أن يكون لبقائه الزمان فالزمان مقدار الوجود .
Time can be conceived such that when one looks at their essences, they can exist without time. So if a changing thing is related to another changing thing by simultaneity or priority, there must be time on both sides. If a constant thing is related to a changing thing by them, time is necessary in one of its two sides without the other. If a constant thing is related to another constant thing by simultaneity, the two sides are independent of time, even if they are concomitant with it. These are intelligible differentiated meanings referred to by different expressions to draw attention to their differentiation. When one reflects on them, what Abu al-Barakat went to is repelled, that time is the measure of existence, for he said: 'The permanent does not persist except in time, and what does not occur except in time and is permanent must have time for its persistence, so time is the measure of existence.'
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. It explains that constant things can be conceived without time. It then classifies three types of relations: (1) changing to changing (by simultaneity or priority) requires time on both sides; (2) constant to changing requires time on one side (the changing side); (3) constant to constant (by simultaneity) requires no time, though they may be concomitant with time. These are distinct meanings expressed by different terms. Reflecting on this refutes Abu al-Barakat's claim that time is the measure of all existence, because constant things are independent of time, so time cannot be their measure. Abu al-Barakat's argument that the permanent requires time for its persistence is rejected because constant things are not inherently temporal.

Key Terms

قبلية
أبو البركات

Misconception: Time is the measure of all existence, including constant things.
Correction: Constant things are independent of time; time is only required when change is involved.

Checkpoint

A) Yes, on both sides.
B) Yes, on one side.
C) No, they are independent of time.
D) Only if they are the same.

Key Takeaway
Constant things can exist without time, proving that time is not the measure of all existence.

?

What is one worldly attachment you will consciously release today because it is temporal?
?
Abu al-Barakat mistakenly thought time is necessary for all existence. In what areas of your life do you mistakenly act as if things (like your own life or achievements) are permanent and require time to persist? The Goal of Motion: Place as Dimension
Uses the goal of motion to argue place is dimension, citing Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from the goal of motion forces place to be a dimension rather than a surface.

Core Idea
The goal of motion must exist before arrival, so place must be a real dimension.

This lesson presents Ibn Sina's argument that place must be a dimension because the goal of a moving body exists at the start of motion, yet no surface is present. The passage examines heavy and light bodies to demonstrate this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ibn Sina is cited as an authority.
2
Identify the two examples
The passage gives two cases: the absolute heavy (seeking the earth's center) and the absolute light (seeking the concave of the moon). For each, note what is sought and what is absent at the start of motion.
The heavy body, like a stone, aims for a location where no surface yet exists; similarly for the light body like fire.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since the goal exists during motion (as a target) but no surface is there, place must be the dimension (bu'd) that already exists, not a non-existing surface.
This argument supports the view that place is an interval or dimension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و كذا يؤيده أنَّ المكان مقصد المتحرك بالحصول فيه وقد صرح ابن سينا في إثبات الجهة بأنه أي مقصد المتحرك بالحصول فيه موجود حال الحركة ليتصور كونه مقصداً بالحصول فيه فالمكان الذي يقصده الثقيل المطلق وهو الذي يقتضي أن ينطبق مركزه على مركز الأرض كالحجر مثلاً موجود حال ما يفرض الحجر متحركاً طالباً للحصول فيه ولا سطح هناك موجود يحيط بهذا الثقيل وكذا ما يقصده الخفيف المطلق وهو الذي يقتضي أن ينطبق محيطه ويلتصق بمحيط المحدد الذي تنتهي إليه حركات العناصر أعني مقعر فلك القمر كقطعة النار مثلا أن يكون موجوداً حال ما يفرض هذا الخفيف متحركاً إليه طالباً للحصول فيه، ولا سطح هناك موجود يحيط بهذا الخفيف، فدل على أنَّ المكان هو البعد الموجود دون السطح المعدوم في حال حركتي الثقيل والخفيف
And likewise it supports it that place is the goal of the moving thing by being obtained in it. And Ibn Sina stated in proving direction that it—i.e., the goal of the moving thing by being obtained in it—exists during motion so that it can be conceived as a goal by being obtained in it. So the place that the absolute heavy seeks—which requires that its center coincide with the center of the earth, like a stone—exists while the stone is supposed moving seeking to be obtained in it, and there is no surface existing surrounding this heavy. And likewise what the absolute light seeks—which requires that its circumference coincide and adhere to the circumference of the boundary which the movements of the elements end at, namely the concave of the sphere of the moon, like a piece of fire—exists while this light thing is supposed moving towards it seeking to be obtained in it, and there is no surface existing surrounding this light thing. So this indicates that place is the existing dimension, not the non-existing surface, in the state of motion of the heavy and light.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents a second supporting argument, cited from Ibn Sina, based on the concept of the 'goal of motion' (maqṣad al-mutaḥarrik). For place to be the goal towards which a moving body strives (like a heavy body seeking the center of the earth, or a light body seeking the concave of the moon), that goal must exist during motion so that the motion is directed towards it. But at the moment of motion, before arrival, no surface exists at the target location: the heavy body is still above the center, and the light body has not yet reached the lunar sphere. Yet the motion is genuinely towards something—a dimension (buʿd) that is already present. Hence place must be that dimension, not a surface, because the surface does not yet exist. The argument relies on the metaphysical principle that a real goal must be real at the time of seeking.

Key Terms
مقصد المتحرك
الثقيل
الخفيف
المحدد
مقعر فلك القمر
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument presupposes a void space.
Correction: The argument does not presuppose a void; it only asserts that the dimension (place) exists as a real continuum distinct from surfaces, not that it is empty.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from the goal of motion, why must place be a dimension rather than a surface?
A) Because surfaces are not real entities.
B) Because heavy and light bodies aim for locations where no surface exists at the start of motion.
C) Because Ibn Sina said so.
D) Because surfaces cannot be goals.

Key Takeaway
The argument from the goal of motion shows that place must be an existing dimension, not a surface, because the goal of motion exists before arrival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a constant motion toward a goal. Is that goal—be it Allah's pleasure, Paradise, or simply meaning—real to you now, or only a hope for the future? What would change if you treated it as existent today?
?
The heavy and light bodies move unerringly to their places. What drives you? Are you drawn by the 'surface' of temporary gain or by the 'dimension' of eternal truth? Examine your recent choices—where is your motion headed?

Proof of Void from Smooth Surfaces in Contact
A proof of void using smooth surface contact and the rejection of indivisible parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator proves the possibility of void using the contact and instant separation of smooth surfaces, relying on the opponent's rejection of indivisible parts.

Core Idea
When smooth surfaces separate instantly, void necessarily occurs—and your own principles require you to admit it.

This passage presents a clever proof of void based on the contact of two smooth surfaces. The commentator builds on the opponent's denial of indivisible parts to show that smooth surfaces must touch in a continuous area, and when lifted instantly, a void must form. The argument is subtle, involving the necessity of complete contact and simultaneous lifting, and it counters objections about air rushing in through pores.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow before diving into details.
Notice the phrase 'except for indivisible parts' which sets up the conditions.
2
Understand the initial contact condition
Grasp the claim: two smooth surfaces must touch either entirely or by a part that is also a smooth surface. Otherwise, contact would be by indivisible parts, which the opponent denies.
This forces full or continuous contact.
3
Follow the separation argument
See why lifting one surface partially would cause disintegration (like a millstone). Thus, the entire surface must rise instantly.
The millstone analogy is key.
4
Conclude the void and handle objections
When the surfaces separate, no body can rush in because they are smooth and poreless. Therefore, a void necessarily occurs. The commentator also refines the definition of contact.
This final step shows the argument's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إلا لأجزاء لا تتجزأ) يعني إذا طبقنا صفحة ملساء على مثلها وجب أن يتماسا بتمامها، أو أن يماس شيء منقسم في جهتين من أحديهما نظيره من الأخرى، وإلا لم يكن التماس الحاصل بينهما إلا لأجزاء لا تتجزأ أصلاً ) وأنتم تقولون به أي بتماس الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ لاستحالتها عندكم وإذا ثبت جوز التماس بينهما، إما بالتمام أو بالبعض الذي هو أيضاً صفحة ملساء فنقول : ( ولا يمتنع رفع إحداهما عن الأخرى دفعة) بأن يرتفع جميع جوانبها معاً (لو ارتفع بعض إحداهما دون البعض لزم الانفكاك بين أجزاء الصفحة العليا فإنه إذا ارتفع بعض أجزائها عن السفلى، ولم يرتفع عنها الجزء المتصل بذلك المرتفع انفك أحدهما عن الآخر بالضرورة على قياس ما ذكروه في نفس الجزء من تفكك الرحى. وهكذا نقول في سائر الأجزاء فيجب ارتفاعها بأسرها معاً بلا تخلف. بل دفعة واحدة ( وأيضاً فأي جزء من أجزاء الصفحة العليا ( ارتفع ) عن السفلى (دفعة ) واحدة ( لو لم تكن صفحة ) منقسمة في جهتين ( كان ذلك ) الجزء المرتفع ( جزءاً لا يتجزأ ) أو ما في حكمه (وهو محال عندكم) فقد ثبت إمكان ارتفاعها عنها دفعة واحدة ) فإذا فرضنا ارتفاعها عنها) كذلك ( وقع الخلاء ) فيما بين الصفحتين (ضرورة) أنَّه لم يكن فيما بينهما جسم آخر وإلا لزم تداخل الأجزاء ) وإنَّ الهواء ) أو جسماً غيره ( إنما ينتقل إليه من الأطراف ويمر منافذ فغير لازم لكون كل واحد من الصفحتين ملساء، وإن أريد به التماس لأجزاء متصلة بعضها ببعض بحيث لا يكون بينهما منافذ ففيه المطلوب، لأنه حينئذ يتماس صفحة متصلة بمثله ويتم الاستدلال . فالصواب ترك قوله : وإلا لم يكن التماس إلخ ولو أريد بالأجزاء النقاط، ويقال : لو لم یکن تماس شيء منقسم في جهتين وإحداهما بنظيره من الأخرى، لم يكن التماس في شيء من الصور إلا بالنقاط وأنتم لا تقولون به، بل تقولون يتماس السطح بالسطح أيضاً، فإن محدب كل فلك مماس بمقعر آخر لكان له وجه.
His saying: (except for indivisible parts) means if we apply a smooth surface onto another like it, it is necessary that they touch in their entirety, or that something extended in two directions from one of them touches its counterpart from the other, otherwise the contact between them would only be by indivisible parts. And you (opponents) affirm contact of indivisible parts, but their impossibility is established with you. When it is established, contact between them is permissible, either entirely or by a part which is also a smooth surface. So we say: (It is not impossible to lift one from the other instantly), meaning that all its sides rise simultaneously. (If some of it rises without the other, then separation between the parts of the upper surface necessarily occurs, because when some of its parts rise from the lower surface, and the part connected to that rising part does not rise, one is separated from the other necessarily, analogous to what they mentioned regarding the part itself in the disintegration of the millstone. And similarly we say for all the parts, so all of them must rise together without lag, rather instantly. (Also, any part of the upper surface that rises from the lower surface instantly, if it were not a surface extended in two directions, that rising part would be an indivisible part or its equivalent, and that is impossible in your view.) So the possibility of its rising from it instantly is established. (If we assume its rising from it) likewise, (a void occurs) between the two surfaces (necessarily), because there was no other body between them, otherwise overlapping of bodies would be necessary. (Air) or another body (only moves to it from the sides and passes through pores, so it is not necessary due to each of the two surfaces being smooth. And if what is meant by contact is for connected parts such that there are no pores between them, then the desired is in it, because then a continuous surface touches another like it, and the argument is complete. So the correct thing is to leave his saying: 'otherwise there would be no contact, etc.' And if by 'parts' is meant points, and it is said: If there were not contact of something extended in two directions with its counterpart from the other, then there would be no contact in any form except by points, and you do not affirm that, but rather you affirm surface-to-surface contact. For the convexity of each sphere is tangent to the concavity of another, and it would have a face.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a detailed proof of the possibility of void from smooth surfaces. It begins by commenting on the phrase 'except for indivisible parts'. The commentator explains that when two smooth surfaces are placed together, either they touch completely, or a part extended in two dimensions touches its counterpart, otherwise contact would be only by indivisible parts. Since the opponents deny indivisible parts, full contact is necessary. Then the argument proceeds: it is possible to lift one surface from the other instantly, because if some parts rose and others did not, the surface would disintegrate, like the millstone analogy. Therefore, all parts must rise simultaneously. When they separate, a void necessarily forms between them because no other body was there and air would have to flow through pores, but the surfaces are smooth and poreless. The commentator then refines the point: if contact is defined as continuous surfaces without pores, then the argument holds perfectly. He concludes that the phrase 'otherwise there would be no contact' should be dropped. He also addresses an alternative: if parts are interpreted as points, then contact would only be by points, which the opponents reject; they affirm surface-to-surface contact, as in the contact of celestial spheres.

صفحة ملساء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the existence of indivisible parts.
Correction: The proof relies on the opponents' denial of indivisible parts to force them to accept full contact and thus void.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator conclude happens when one smooth surface is instantly lifted from another?

?
The argument relies on the impossibility of instantaneous communication between separated parts—the millstone analogy shows that if one part moves without the connected part, disintegration follows. In your spiritual life, when you allow one area (like prayer) to separate from another (like honesty), do you feel the 'disintegration' of your character? What would it take for all parts of your life to rise together in devotion?

Objection and Answer: Contact by a Point
Commentator answers objection that surfaces could touch at a point

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why surfaces cannot touch only at a point, and the implications for the debate between atomism and continuous extension.

Core Idea
Surfaces require contact of divisible parts, not points.

This passage tackles a subtle objection to the nature of surface contact. The objector suggests two smooth surfaces could touch at a single point, like small spheres contacting a large sphere. The commentator's answer clarifies that a point is not a surface part and that such contact would require indivisible parts, which the opponent (the philosopher) rejects. This discussion highlights the kalam commitment to continuous extension.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the objection: 'Why is it not permissible for them to touch at a point, just as small spheres touch a great sphere?' Consider the analogy.
The objection is from a hypothetical opponent.
2
Analyze the Answer
The answer states that a surface's existence necessitates contact of something divisible in two directions. A point is not divisible, so contact at a point would imply indivisible parts.
This relies on the definition of a surface.
3
Connect to the Broader Debate
The consequence of point-contact is indivisible parts (atomism), which the opponent denies. Thus, the only consistent option is contact by divisible surface parts.
This shows how the kalam view defends continuous extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإلا لم يكن التماس الحاصل بها إلا لأجزاء لا تتجزأ أصلاً) فإن قلت : لم لا يجوز يتماسا بنقطة؟ كما أن تماست الكرات الصغيرة بكرة عظيمة . قلت : لأن وجود السطح يقتضي تماس شيء منقسم في جهتين نظير لفرض تساوي وضع الأجزاء فإن لم يتماس كذلك يلزم أن لا يوجد السطح بل يكون هناك أجزاء لا تتجزأ، كما هو مذهب المتكلمين. وهذا ظاهر جداً.
His statement: (Otherwise the contact occurring between them would only be by indivisible parts). Then you might say: Why is it not permissible for them to touch at a point, just as small spheres touch a great sphere? I say: Because the existence of a surface necessitates the contact of something divisible in two directions with its counterpart, given the assumption of equality of the parts. If they do not touch in that way, it follows that the surface does not exist, but rather there are indivisible parts there, as is the doctrine of the mutakallimun. And this is very clear.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the contact of two smooth surfaces. The objection argues that surfaces could simply touch at a single point, like small spheres contacting a large sphere. The answer explains that a surface, by definition, requires contact of something divisible in two dimensions (i.e., a surface part) with its counterpart. If contact were only at a point, that point would be an indivisible part, which the opponent (the philosopher) denies, since they reject atomism. Thus, the only consistent possibility is contact by divisible surface parts.

Correction: The passage clarifies that a point is not a surface part divisible in two directions; contact at a point implies indivisible parts, which the opponent denies.

C) Because surfaces are always flat.
D) Because points do not exist in reality.

Key Takeaway
Surfaces cannot touch at a point because that would reduce to indivisible parts, which the opponent rejects; hence contact must involve divisible surface parts.
